The Philosophers' Explanation of Dream Perception
Philosophers' explanation via common sense

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophers' explanation that dream perceptions occur in the common sense (al-hiss al-mushtarak), and how the imaginative faculty contributes to dream imagery.

Core Idea
Dreams are perceptions formed in the common sense, the same faculty that assembles external sensory data.

The philosophers (al-hukama') offer a physiological account of dream perception. They explain that the perceived content in dreams is located in the common sense, which is the center where all external senses converge. Moreover, the imaginative faculty can combine and imprint images onto the common sense, making them appear as if they are being perceived externally.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation for the philosophers' view.
2
Identify the key faculty
Note that the common sense (al-hiss al-mushtarak) is the locus of dream perception.
The common sense gathers all external sensations.
3
Understand the role of external senses
External senses bring data to the common sense, making images 'witnessed'.
4
Learn how the imaginative faculty works
It combines images and imprints them in the common sense.
This is the imaginative faculty (القوة المتخيلة).
5
Conclude why dreams are real perceptions
They are real because they occur in the common sense, just like external perceptions.

?

How does that challenge your understanding of the nature of your own consciousness and the reality of the unseen world (al-ghayb)?

Al-Jubbai's Fetter Analogy and Its Refutation
Al-Jubbai's analogy and its refutation with distinction between fetter and gathered parts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Jubbai's argument that social gathering prevents movement like a fetter, and the rational response that distinguishes between a fetter's permanent barrier and a contingent one.

Core Idea
Prevention does not negate capacity; the barrier of gathering can be overcome with sufficient power.

This lesson examines the debate between al-Jubbai and his opponents regarding whether social gathering prevents movement. Al-Jubbai analogizes it to a fetter, but his opponents point out that a fetter's barrier is fixed whereas gathered parts can be moved if power is adequate. We also explore the assumption that the non-existent can be an object of power.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once to get the flow of al-Jubbai's claim and the rebuttal. Note the key terms.
Focus on the analogy and the distinction made.
2
Identify the analogy and its refutation
Find where al-Jubbai compares social gathering to a fetter. Then locate the refutation: the fetter's barrier persists even if power increases, while gathering's barrier can be removed with sufficient power.
The text states 'the barrier of the fetter does not disappear even if power is multiplied'.
3
Understand the implication on capability
Notice that the refutation implies that the agent is capable but prevented, not incapable. This connects to the doctrine that the non-existent can be an object of power.
The text says prevention entails capability, not lack of it.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
انضمام أجزاء القيد إلى رجليه وهو مبني على أصله في جواز منع القادر، وقد بان بطلانه وإن سلمنا صحة المنع فلا نسلم صحة التعليل بانضمام أجزاء القيد إلى رجليه، بل جاز أن يكون المنع لمعنى مختص بصورة القيد ولا وجود له فيما نحن فيه من الأجزاء المجتمعة وكيف لا والفرق واقع بينهما من جهة أن مانع القيد لا يزول وإن تضاعفت القدرة بخلاف الأجزاء المجتمعة فإنه قال : بزوال المانع بتقديران يوجد قدر موازية لعدد الأجزاء المنضمة، ومما نقلناه تبين أن كلام الجبائي من الفرع الثالث، كما هو المناسب لكن الموجود في أكثر نسخ الكتاب هكذا ( الرابع ) أي من الفروع : ( قال الجبائي : الاجتماع يمنع التحريك كالقيد ( فإنه مانع عن المشي لمن هو قادر عليه (وهو) أي كون القيد مانعا عن الفعل ( فرع أن المعدوم مقدور ) حتى يتصور كون القادر على فعل ممنوعاً منه، إذ لا مجال للمنع بالقياس إلى الفعل الموجود لكنا بينا بطلان كون المعدوم مقدورا بما ثبت من وجوب كون القدرة مع الفعل لا قبله (وبه ) أي بكون الاجتماع مانعا عن التحريك (منع) الجبائي (كون القادر على حمل مئة من قادراً على حمل المئة الأخرى معها وحكم بأنه ليس قادراً على حملها، وفيه بحث لأن كون الاجتماع مانعاً من الفعل يقتضي كون ذلك القادر قادراً على حمل الأخرى ممنوعاً منه لا كونه غير قادر عليه . [ المقصد الحادي عشر : اتجاهات القدرة المحركة ] مقاصد هذا النوع وكأنه سهو من الناسخ فإن هذا المبحث من فروع الثالث، أي من المعتزلة لا من. تتمة مقاصد النوع الرابع فإن جعل كلام الجبائي من الفرع كما فعله بعضهم في شرح هذا الكتاب كان هذا فرعاً رابعاً وإن جعل فرعاً على حدة قوله : ( من جواز منع القادر يعني أن الممنوع قادر . قوله : ( ومما نقلناه إلخ) قد عرفت حال ما نقله وإن جعله فرعاً رابعا أولى . قوله : ( كما فعله بعضهم ) بأن أسقط لفظ الرابع . قوله: (ومما نقلناه تبين أن كلام الجبائي إلخ ) سياق الكلام يدل على أن الجبائي يقول بوجود القدرة على تحريك الأجزاء المجتمعة، وتخلف التحريك عنها لمانع الاجتماع وغيره لا يقول بوجود القدرة، فبملاحظة هذا التفصيل يصح جعل كلام الجبائي فرعاً رابعاً، إلا أن الشارح نظر إلى أن الفرع الثالث المذكور في المتن توليد القدرة الواحدة في محال متفرقة حركات متعددة إلى جهات وعدم توليدها في محال مجتمعة فاندرج فيه مذهب الجبائي . غاية ما في الباب أنهم بعد ما اتفقوا على عدم التوليد هاهنا فالجبائي على تحقق القدرة على التحريك وتخلفه لمانع، والباقون على انتفاء القدرة عليه يشعر بما ذكرته قول الشارح من تتمة الفرع الثالث .
The joining of parts of the fetter to his legs—and it is based on his principle of the permissibility of preventing the capable [agent], and its falsehood has become clear. And even if we grant the validity of prevention, we do not grant the validity of the causal explanation by the joining of parts of the fetter to his legs. Rather, it is possible that the prevention is due to a meaning specific to the form of the fetter that does not exist in our case of the gathered parts. And how so, while there is a real difference between them in that the barrier of the fetter does not disappear even if power is multiplied, unlike the gathered parts, for he said that the barrier is removed on the condition that there exists a power equal to the number of the joined parts. And from what we have transmitted, it becomes clear that the statement of al-Jubbai belongs to the third branch, as is appropriate. But what is found in most copies of the book is like this: '(The fourth) of the branches: al-Jubbai said: Social gathering prevents movement like the fetter, for it is a barrier to walking for one who is capable of it. And it—i.e., the fetter being a barrier to the act—is a branch of the [doctrine] that the non-existent is an object of power, so that the capable agent can be conceived as being prevented from [the act]. For there is no room for prevention with respect to an existent act. But we have shown the falsehood of the non-existent being an object of power by what has been established of the necessity of power being with the act, not before it. And by it—i.e., by social gathering being a barrier to movement—al-Jubbai prevented the view that the one capable of carrying a hundred is capable of carrying another hundred with it, and he judged that he is not capable of carrying it. But there is discussion [about this], because social gathering being a barrier to the act entails that that capable agent is capable of carrying the other, [but] prevented from it, not that he is not capable of it.' [The Eleventh Section: Directions of Moving Power] The sections of this type—and it seems to be a scribal error, for this discussion belongs to the branches of the third, i.e., of the Mu'tazila, not from the completion of the sections of the fourth type. For if someone made al-Jubbai's statement from the branch, as some did in the commentary of this book, this would be a fourth branch. And if it is made a separate branch, [then it would be independent]. His saying: 'From the permissibility of preventing the capable' means that the prevented [agent] is capable. His saying: 'And from what we have transmitted, etc.'—you know the state of what he transmitted, and that making it a fourth branch is more appropriate. His saying: 'As some did'—by dropping the word 'fourth'. His saying: 'And from what we have transmitted, it becomes clear that al-Jubbai's statement, etc.'—the flow of speech indicates that al-Jubbai affirms the existence of power to move the gathered parts, but the movement fails to occur due to the barrier of gathering; others do not affirm the existence of power. Considering this detail, it is correct to make al-Jubbai's statement a fourth branch, except that the commentator considered that the third branch mentioned in the text is the generation of a single power in separate loci of multiple movements to directions, and its non-generation in gathered loci. Thus al-Jubbai's doctrine is included in it. The ultimate point is that after they agreed on the non-generation here, al-Jubbai holds the realization of power over movement and its failure due to a barrier, while the others hold the negation of power over it. This is indicated by what I mentioned—the statement of the commentator from the completion of the third branch.
Source pages p. 127

Commentary
This passage presents al-Jubbai's analogy comparing social gathering to a fetter that prevents movement, followed by a refutation. The refutation argues that the fetter's barrier is permanent regardless of power increase, whereas the barrier of gathered parts can be overcome if power is sufficient. The passage then quotes al-Jubbai's original statement from the text, which includes the claim that the non-existent can be an object of power—a doctrine the author rejects. Finally, the passage discusses whether this argument belongs to the third or fourth branch of Mu'tazilite classification, concluding that it fits as a fourth branch or as part of the third, depending on perspective.

الجبائي
الاجتماع
المانع
القيد
القدرة
المعدوم مقدور

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If someone is prevented from an act, they are not capable of it.
Correction: Prevention does not negate capacity; the capable agent is prevented, not incapable.

Checkpoint
In the rebuttal of al-Jubbai's analogy, what difference is cited between the fetter and gathered parts?
A) The fetter is made of metal.
B) The fetter's barrier persists even if power increases.
C) Gathered parts cannot be moved at all.
D) Power only applies to existent things.

Key Takeaway
Al-Jubbai's fetter analogy fails because the fetter's barrier is permanent, while the gathering's barrier can be overcome with sufficient power.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever convinced yourself you are incapable of a spiritual task (e.g., praying at night, giving charity) using a false analogy, like the one al-Jubbai used? Where in your life have you mistaken a temporary, removable barrier for a permanent fetter? Identify one such barrier and commit to testing whether it truly can be overcome with greater resolve.
?
The lesson shows that prevention and incapacity are not the same. In your own relationship with Allah, do you sometimes feel that your sins have made you permanently incapable of repentance or good deeds? The fetter of sin might seem permanent, but the gathering of obstacles can be removed by Allah's mercy if you increase your effort. What 'fetter' are you treating as unremovable that might actually be a gathered obstacle? Write one actionable step to test this.
?
Al-Jubbai's error was using a flawed analogy to limit God's power. Have you ever limited Allah's mercy or your own potential based on a weak comparison? Reflect on a time you assumed your situation was hopeless because it resembled a past failure. How can you actively replace that false analogy with the truth that Allah's power can overcome any gathering of difficulties?

Classification Debate: Which Branch Does al-Jubbai's View Belong?
Discussion of the placement of al-Jubbai's view among Mu'tazilite branches.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the scholarly discussion on whether al-Jubbai's argument belongs to the third or fourth branch of Mu'tazilite divisions.

Core Idea
A doctrinal classification can be disputed based on how one interprets the scope of a branch.

After presenting al-Jubbai's view, the commentator debates its proper classification within the Mu'tazilite system of branches. The passage reveals a scribal error and different viewpoints on whether it should be considered a fourth branch or part of the third.

Guided Path
1
Read the classification passage
Read the passage on classification carefully. Note the scribal error mentioned and the two possible placements.
The passage says most copies label it as the fourth branch.
2
Understand the reasoning for each side
The commentator explains that if al-Jubbai's view is considered a separate branch, it would be the fourth, but some see it as part of the third branch because it deals with generation of power.
The ultimate point is that al-Jubbai holds power is realized but movement fails due to a barrier, while others deny power.

انضمام أجزاء القيد إلى رجليه وهو مبني على أصله في جواز منع القادر، وقد بان بطلانه وإن سلمنا صحة المنع فلا نسلم صحة التعليل بانضمام أجزاء القيد إلى رجليه، بل جاز أن يكون المنع لمعنى مختص بصورة القيد ولا وجود له فيما نحن فيه من الأجزاء المجتمعة وكيف لا والفرق واقع بينهما من جهة أن مانع القيد لا يزول وإن تضاعفت القدرة بخلاف الأجزاء المجتمعة فإنه قال : بزوال المانع بتقديران يوجد قدر موازية لعدد الأجزاء المنضمة، ومما نقلناه تبين أن كلام الجبائي من الفرع الثالث، كما هو المناسب لكن الموجود في أكثر نسخ الكتاب هكذا ( الرابع ) أي من الفروع : ( قال الجبائي : الاجتماع يمنع التحريك كالقيد ( فإنه مانع عن المشي لمن هو قادر عليه (وهو) أي كون القيد مانعا عن الفعل ( فرع أن المعدوم مقدور ) حتى يتصور كون القادر على فعل ممنوعاً منه، إذ لا مجال للمنع بالقياس إلى الفعل الموجود لكنا بينا بطلان كون المعدوم مقدورا بما ثبت من وجوب كون القدرة مع الفعل لا قبله (وبه ) أي بكون الاجتماع مانعا عن التحريك (منع) الجبائي (كون القادر على حمل مئة من قادراً على حمل المئة الأخرى معها وحكم بأنه ليس قادراً على حملها، وفيه بحث لأن كون الاجتماع مانعاً من الفعل يقتضي كون ذلك القادر قادراً على حمل الأخرى ممنوعاً منه لا كونه غير قادر عليه . [ المقصد الحادي عشر : اتجاهات القدرة المحركة ] مقاصد هذا النوع وكأنه سهو من الناسخ فإن هذا المبحث من فروع الثالث، أي من المعتزلة لا من. تتمة مقاصد النوع الرابع فإن جعل كلام الجبائي من الفرع كما فعله بعضهم في شرح هذا الكتاب كان هذا فرعاً رابعاً وإن جعل فرعاً على حدة قوله : ( من جواز منع القادر يعني أن الممنوع قادر . قوله : ( ومما نقلناه إلخ) قد عرفت حال ما نقله وإن جعله فرعاً رابعا أولى . قوله : ( كما فعله بعضهم ) بأن أسقط لفظ الرابع . قوله: (ومما نقلناه تبين أن كلام الجبائي إلخ ) سياق الكلام يدل على أن الجبائي يقول بوجود القدرة على تحريك الأجزاء المجتمعة، وتخلف التحريك عنها لمانع الاجتماع وغيره لا يقول بوجود القدرة، فبملاحظة هذا التفصيل يصح جعل كلام الجبائي فرعاً رابعاً، إلا أن الشارح نظر إلى أن الفرع الثالث المذكور في المتن توليد القدرة الواحدة في محال متفرقة حركات متعددة إلى جهات وعدم توليدها في محال مجتمعة فاندرج فيه مذهب الجبائي . غاية ما في الباب أنهم بعد ما اتفقوا على عدم التوليد هاهنا فالجبائي على تحقق القدرة على التحريك وتخلفه لمانع، والباقون على انتفاء القدرة عليه يشعر بما ذكرته قول الشارح من تتمة الفرع الثالث .
The joining of parts of the fetter to his legs—and it is based on his principle of the permissibility of preventing the capable [agent], and its falsehood has become clear. And even if we grant the validity of prevention, we do not grant the validity of the causal explanation by the joining of parts of the fetter to his legs. Rather, it is possible that the prevention is due to a meaning specific to the form of the fetter that does not exist in our case of the gathered parts. And how so, while there is a real difference between them in that the barrier of the fetter does not disappear even if power is multiplied, unlike the gathered parts, for he said that the barrier is removed on the condition that there exists a power equal to the number of the joined parts. And from what we have transmitted, it becomes clear that the statement of al-Jubbai belongs to the third branch, as is appropriate. But what is found in most copies of the book is like this: '(The fourth) of the branches: al-Jubbai said: Social gathering prevents movement like the fetter, for it is a barrier to walking for one who is capable of it. And it—i.e., the fetter being a barrier to the act—is a branch of the [doctrine] that the non-existent is an object of power, so that the capable agent can be conceived as being prevented from [the act]. For there is no room for prevention with respect to an existent act. But we have shown the falsehood of the non-existent being an object of power by what has been established of the necessity of power being with the act, not before it. And by it—i.e., by social gathering being a barrier to movement—al-Jubbai prevented the view that the one capable of carrying a hundred is capable of carrying another hundred with it, and he judged that he is not capable of carrying it. But there is discussion [about this], because social gathering being a barrier to the act entails that that capable agent is capable of carrying the other, [but] prevented from it, not that he is not capable of it.' [The Eleventh Section: Directions of Moving Power] The sections of this type—and it seems to be a scribal error, for this discussion belongs to the branches of the third, i.e., of the Mu'tazila, not from the completion of the sections of the fourth type. For if someone made al-Jubbai's statement from the branch, as some did in the commentary of this book, this would be a fourth branch. And if it is made a separate branch, [then it would be independent]. His saying: 'From the permissibility of preventing the capable' means that the prevented [agent] is capable. His saying: 'And from what we have transmitted, etc.'—you know the state of what he transmitted, and that making it a fourth branch is more appropriate. His saying: 'As some did'—by dropping the word 'fourth'. His saying: 'And from what we have transmitted, it becomes clear that al-Jubbai's statement, etc.'—the flow of speech indicates that al-Jubbai affirms the existence of power to move the gathered parts, but the movement fails to occur due to the barrier of gathering; others do not affirm the existence of power. Considering this detail, it is correct to make al-Jubbai's statement a fourth branch, except that the commentator considered that the third branch mentioned in the text is the generation of a single power in separate loci of multiple movements to directions, and its non-generation in gathered loci. Thus al-Jubbai's doctrine is included in it. The ultimate point is that after they agreed on the non-generation here, al-Jubbai holds the realization of power over movement and its failure due to a barrier, while the others hold the negation of power over it. This is indicated by what I mentioned—the statement of the commentator from the completion of the third branch.
Source pages p. 127

Commentary
This passage presents al-Jubbai's analogy comparing social gathering to a fetter that prevents movement, followed by a refutation. The refutation argues that the fetter's barrier is permanent regardless of power increase, whereas the barrier of gathered parts can be overcome if power is sufficient. The passage then quotes al-Jubbai's original statement from the text, which includes the claim that the non-existent can be an object of power—a doctrine the author rejects. Finally, the passage discusses whether this argument belongs to the third or fourth branch of Mu'tazilite classification, concluding that it fits as a fourth branch or as part of the third, depending on perspective.

Direction of Moving Power: Can It Lift Upward?
Examines whether horizontal power can also lift upward, with sectarian debate.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the debate over whether the same power that moves an object horizontally can also move it vertically, and the Bahshamiyya's position requiring an additional power.

Core Idea
Raising an object is inherently different from rolling it; some require additional power.

This passage examines a specific question in kalam physics: does the power that causes horizontal motion also suffice for vertical motion? The author presents two positions and critiques the Bahshamiyya's requirement of an extra single power as arbitrary. This lesson unpacks the argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the source passage
Read the Arabic and translation carefully, noting the two opinions and the author's critique.
2
Understand the difference between rolling and raising
The passage argues that raising is harder; every rational person knows this.
3
Examine the Bahshamiyya's position
They require an additional single power for lifting.
4
Analyze the author's objection
Why is limiting the increase to one power arbitrary? Consider if more might be needed.
5
Reflect on the implications
What does this tell us about how kalam thinkers reasoned about power and motion?

القدرة المحركة يمنة ويسرة هل تقدر) وتقوى ( على التصعيد ) والرفع إلى جهة الفوق (منهم من جوزه ومنهم من منعه للفرق بين الدحرجة والرفع ضرورة ) فإن كل عاقل يجد تفاوتا بينهما ويعلم أن رفع شيء أشق وأقوى من تحريكه دحرجة ) وعليه ) أي على المنع ( البهشمية ) أي الطائفة التابعة لرأي أبي هاشم ( وأوجبوا ) للتصعيد والرفع ( زيادة قدرة واحدة) على القدرة المحركة يمنة ويسرة ) ولا يخفى ما فيه من التحكم) إذ لا وجه لحصر الزيادة الكافية على القدرة الواحدة لجواز الاحتياج إلى ما يزيد عليها .
The moving power right and left—does it have power and strength for ascent and raising to the upward direction? Some permit it and some prevent it due to the necessary difference between rolling and raising, for every rational person finds a disparity between them and knows that raising a thing is harder and stronger than moving it by rolling. And upon [this prevention]—i.e., upon preventing—are the Bahshamiyya, i.e., the sect following the opinion of Abu Hashim. And they required for ascent and raising an additional single power over the moving power right and left. And it is not hidden what arbitrariness there is in this, for there is no justification for limiting the sufficient increase to one power, since it is possible that more than it is needed.
Source pages p. 127

Commentary
This passage examines whether the power that moves an object horizontally (right and left) can also move it vertically (upward). Two opinions are presented: some allow it, others prevent it, arguing that there is an evident difference between rolling and lifting—lifting is harder and requires more strength. The Bahshamiyya (followers of Abu Hashim) hold the latter view and require an additional single power for lifting beyond the horizontal power. The author criticizes this as arbitrary, questioning why the needed increase is limited to one power, given that more might be required.

Key Takeaway

The First Way Power Differs from Temperament
Presents the first of two arguments that power is distinct from temperament.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first rational argument demonstrating that divine power (qudra) is not identical to temperament (mizāj), based on the distinction between tangible and non-tangible qualities.

Core Idea
Power is not tangible; temperament is. Therefore, they are not the same.

In this passage, the author begins to prove that power is distinct from temperament by presenting two lines of reasoning. The first argument relies on the sensory nature of temperament versus the non-sensory nature of power. Understanding this distinction is crucial for grasping the nature of divine attributes and their relationship to created things.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
2
Identify the claim and the supporting reason
The claim is that power differs from temperament. The first reason is that temperament is tangible while power is not. Notice how the author explicitly contrasts them.
3
Analyze the concept of temperament as an intermediate quality
The author explains that temperament is an intermediate quality derived from the four primary tangible qualities. Understand that this links temperament to the tangible realm.
This is important for the argument's premise.
4
Recognize that the argument is based on tangibility
The key distinction is that temperament and its effects are perceived by touch, while power is not. This difference in perceptibility shows they are distinct.
5
Consider the conclusion
The author concludes that power is not the same as temperament; it is a subordinate quality. Reflect on why this matters for kalam.
Power is an attribute of Allah, temperament is a created quality.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القدرة مغايرة للمزاج من وجهين: الأول : المزاج وأثره من جنس الكيفيات المحسوسة بالقوة اللامسة وذلك لأن المزاج كيفية متوسطة الكيفيات الأربع المشهورة وهي بالحقيقة من جنسها إلا أنها منكسرة ضعيفة بين بالنسبة إليها فيكون أثرها وحكمها من جنس أحكام هذه الكيفيات إلا أنه يكون أضعف من أحكامها ولا شك أن أحكام هذه الأربع وآثارها من جنسها أيضاً فالمزاج وأثره من جنس الكيفيات الملموسة دون القدرة فإنها ليست مدركة باللمس وليس أثرها من جنس هذه الكيفيات فليست القدرة نفس المزاج بل هي كيفية تابعة له قوله : ( بقدرة واحدة أي قدرة متعلقة بكل ذلك المحل من حيث هو، ولا يحتاج إلى قدرة متعددة بحسب الأجزاء على ما قال جمهورهم كما مر وعلى هذا اندفع التحكم الذي ذكره الشارح. قوله : (كيفية متوسطة إما حاصلة عن تلك الكيفيات المنكسرة أو فائضة على المركب بعد انکسارها علی اختلاف بين الأطباء والطبيعيين. قوله: (وهي في الحقيقة من جنسها وإن كان في الظاهر مخالفة لها من حيث أن الكيفيات الأربع تؤثر بالملاقاة بظاهر البدن بخلاف المزاج فإنه يؤثر بالملاقاة بالباطن . قوله : ( دون القدرة إلخ ) إشارة إلى الكبرى فهو استدلال بالشكل الثاني، بوجهين الأول : قوله : (لأن المزاج كيفية (متوسطة كون المزاج عبارة عن الكيفية المتوسطة، إنما هو مذهب الأطباء وأما عند الحكماء فكيفية أخرى حادثة عند الإنكسار التام. قوله : فليست القدرة نفس المزاج قد يقال : يجوز أن يكون لمتبوع واحد باعتبارات مختلفة توابع متبوعة، فتغاير لا يستلزم عدد المتبوع، وهذا إنما يرد إذا جعل دليل التعدد اختلاف الآثار، وأما إذا جعل كون أحدهما ملموساً دون الآخر وجعل اختلاف الآثار مؤيداً له فلا، وقد
Power is different from temperament in two respects: The first: Temperament and its effect are of the genus of sensible qualities perceptible by the sense of touch, because temperament is an intermediate quality of the four well-known qualities, and it is in reality of their genus except that it is broken and weak relative to them. So its effect and judgment are of the genus of the judgments of these qualities, except that it is weaker than their judgments. And there is no doubt that the judgments and effects of these four are also of their genus. So temperament and its effect are of the genus of tangible qualities, unlike power, for it is not perceived by touch, nor is its effect of the genus of these qualities. So power is not the same as temperament; rather, it is a quality subordinate to it. His saying (with one power): i.e., a power related to that entire locus as such, and it does not need multiple powers according to its parts, as their majority said, as passed. And on this, the arbitrariness mentioned by the commentator is rebutted. His saying (intermediate quality): either resulting from those broken qualities or superadded to the composite after their breaking, according to a difference among physicians and naturalists. His saying (And it is in reality of their genus): although in appearance it differs from them in that the four qualities affect by contact with the exterior of the body, unlike temperament, for it affects by contact with the interior. His saying (unlike power, etc.): an indication to the major premise, so it is an argument in the second figure, with two aspects: First: His saying (because temperament is an intermediate quality): that temperament is defined as the intermediate quality is only the doctrine of physicians; as for the philosophers, it is another quality arising upon complete breaking. His saying (so power is not the same as temperament): it may be said that it is possible for one followed [thing] to have different considerations that are followed things; so difference does not necessitate multiplicity of the followed. And this [objection] only applies if the proof of multiplicity is difference of effects. But if the proof is that one of them is tangible and the other not, and difference of effects is used as support, then [the objection] does not apply. And it has...
Source pages p. 128

Commentary
This passage presents two ways in which power differs from temperament. The first way is that temperament and its effects are sensory qualities (tangible), whereas power is not perceived by touch and its effects are not of the same genus. The explanation gives reasoning: temperament is an intermediate quality derived from the four primary qualities, so it shares their genus but is weaker; its effects are also tangible. Power, however, is not tangible. The commentary then elaborates on the definitions: 'one power' refers to a single power for the whole locus, not multiple per parts, thus rebutting the earlier charge of arbitrariness. The definition of temperament as an intermediate quality is discussed, noting a difference between physicians and philosophers. The proof is then analyzed as a second-figure syllogism with two aspects: one based on the definition of temperament, the other on the tangibility difference. A potential objection is raised: difference in attributes does not necessarily imply difference in substrate. The reply is that this objection only works if the proof relied solely on difference of effects; but here the proof is based on one being tangible and the other not, using difference of effects as support.

المزاج
الكيفيات المحسوسة
القوة اللامسة
الكيفيات الأربع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Power and temperament are the same because both are qualities.
Correction: They differ in tangibility: temperament is perceptible by touch, power is not.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
The first way to distinguish power from temperament is based on tangibility: temperament belongs to the genus of tangible qualities, while power does not.

What Makes Omission (Tark) Morally Significant?
Definition of omission and its conditions of intention and power over opposites.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that omission requires both intention (qasd) and power over the opposite act to be morally chargeable.

Core Idea
Omission is not mere absence—it requires intention and power.

This passage from a classical kalam text investigates what truly constitutes an omission. It distinguishes between mere non-action and a morally chargeable omission, establishing that intention and capability over the opposite act are necessary. Let's examine the text directly.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation to get the overall argument.
Notice the examples given at the end.
2
Identify the conditions
The passage states two main conditions: intention (qasd) and power over both opposites. Find where these are mentioned.
3
Examine the opinions on tark
Two views are presented: tark as an act of the heart, or as the act of the opposite. Understand both.
4
Analyze the examples of invalid omission
Three examples show when omission is not valid: sleeping, impossible ascent, involuntary movement. See why each fails.
These illustrate the conditions.
5
Reflect on moral accountability
Consider why these conditions are necessary for blame, reward, etc. Connect to the learning objective.

حاصلاً بالقصد فلا يقال : ترك النائم الكتابة ) ولذلك يتعلق به أي بالترك (الذم) والمدح والثواب والعقاب فلولا أنه اعتبر فيه القصد لم يكن كذلك قطعاً ( وقيل : إنه ) أي الترك ( من أفعال القلوب ) لأنه انصراف القلب عن الفعل وكف النفس عن ارتياده ( وقيل : هو ) أي الترك ( فعل الضد لأنه مقدور والعدم ) أي عدم الفعل ( مستمر) من الأزل ( فلا يصلح أثراً للقدرة الحادثة، وقد يقال : دوام استمراره مقدور لأنه قادر على أن يفعل ذلك الفعل فيزول استمرار ،عدمه فمن هذه الجهة صلح يكون العدم أثراً للقدرة قالوا ولا بد أن يكون كلا الضدين مقدورين حتى يكون ارتكاب أحدهما تركاً للآخر، فإذا لم يكن أحدهما أو كلاهما مقدوراً لم يصح استعمال الترك هناك فلا يقال : ترك بقعوده الصعود إلى السماء، ولا ترك بحركته الاضطرارية حركته الاختيارية ولا ترك بحركته الاضطرارية الصعود
Occurring with intention, so it is not said: the sleeping person's omission of writing. And therefore blame, praise, reward and punishment attach to it (i.e., to omission). For if intention were not considered in it, it would certainly not be so. And it is said: it (i.e., omission) is from the acts of the hearts because it is the turning away of the heart from the act and the restraining of the soul from seeking it. And it is said: it (i.e., omission) is the act of the opposite because it is within power, while non-existence (i.e., non-existence of the act) is continuous from eternity, so it is not fit to be an effect of originated power. And it is said: the persistence of its continuance is within power because he is able to perform that act, so the continuance of its non-existence ceases. So from this aspect non-existence becomes an effect of power. They said: it is necessary that both opposites be within power so that undertaking one of them is an omission of the other. So if one of them or both are not within power, the use of omission is not valid there. Thus it is not said: he omitted by his sitting the ascending to the sky, nor he omitted by his involuntary movement his voluntary movement, nor he omitted by his involuntary movement the ascent.
Source pages p. 139

Commentary
This passage defines the nature of omission (tark) and its conditions. It states that omission must occur with intention (qasd) to be subject to moral judgment; otherwise, a sleeping person's not writing is not called omission. Intention is necessary for blame, praise, reward, and punishment. Two views are presented: (1) omission is an act of the heart—turning away and restraining the soul; (2) omission is the act of the opposite, since non-existence (the absence of the act) is eternal and cannot be an effect of power, but persistence of non-existence can be within power if one can perform the act and thus end the persistence. The passage insists that both opposites must be within power for one to be considered omission of the other. Examples illustrate: sitting does not constitute omission of ascending if ascent is impossible; involuntary movement does not constitute omission of voluntary movement or ascent.

Key Terms
الترك
القصد
فعل الضد
العدم
القدرة الحادثة
الضدين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Omission is simply not performing an act, regardless of intention or ability.
Correction: The passage shows that omission requires both intention (qasd) and power over both opposites.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is necessary for omission (tark) to be subject to blame or praise?
A) That one intends NOT to perform the act
B) That one has power over the opposite act
C) That the act is not possible at all
D) Both A and B

Key Takeaway
Omission is morally significant only when it is intentional and the opposite act is within one's power.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Can you recall a specific instance today when you intentionally omitted a good deed that was clearly within your power? How will you address that omission tomorrow?
?
The sleeping person is excused due to lack of intention. When you have failed to act, have you hidden behind the excuse 'I didn't mean to' even though you were awake and able? What does this lesson demand of you now?
?
Omission requires both intention and power over the opposite. What act have you been neglecting that you now realize is a blameworthy omission? Write down a concrete intention to perform it within the next 24 hours.

Intention Does Not Attach to Non-Existences
Gloss explaining that intention does not apply to non-existences.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why intention (qasd) cannot directly pertain to non-existences and the metaphorical exception.

Core Idea
Intention is only for existents; non-existence is not intended directly.

This brief gloss clarifies a subtle point about the scope of intention. The author explains that intention does not attach to non-existences, as supported by a well-known hadith. However, there is a metaphorical sense in which one might be said to intend a non-existence by intending something that necessitates it. This lesson unpacks that distinction.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once through, then read the literal translation. Notice the key terms: qaṣd (intention) and i‘dām (non-existences).
2
Understand the main claim
The author states that intention does not attach to non-existences. Reflect on why this might be: intention is directed at bringing something about, but non-existences are not things to be brought about.
The hadith reinforces that only what God wills occurs; non-existences are not willed directly.
3
Examine the metaphorical exception
The passage allows that one might intend a non-existence metaphorically if the intended existent necessitates it. For example, intending to remove a wall entails the non-existence of the wall in its current state.
This shows that kalam reasoning accounts for subtle logical distinctions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (حاصلاً بالقصد فيه أن القصد لا يتعلق بالإعدام كما يدل عليه الحديث المرفوع ما شاء الله كان وما لم يشأ لم يكن اللهم إلا أن يراد منه كونه حاصلاً بقصد ما يستلزمه تجوزاً.
His statement: 'Occurring with intention' — therein is that intention does not attach to non-existences, as indicated by the raised hadith: 'What God wills occurs, and what He does not will does not occur.' Unless what is meant by it is that it occurs by the intention of something that necessitates it, metaphorically.
Source pages p. 139

Commentary
This passage is a gloss on the phrase 'occurring with intention' (حاصلاً بالقصد). It clarifies that intention (qasd) does not pertain to non-existences (i‘dām) because non-existences are not objects of intention. This is supported by the prophetic hadith: 'What God wills occurs, and what He does not will does not occur.' The only exception is metaphorical: if one intends something that entails the non-existence, it might be figuratively said that one intends the non-existence.

Key Terms
القصد
الإعدام
الحديث المرفوع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Intention can directly target non-existence.
Correction: Intention only applies to existents or to things that entail non-existence metaphorically.

Checkpoint
Why does intention not attach to non-existences according to this gloss?
A) Because non-existences are not within power
B) Because the hadith says what God wills occurs, so non-existences are not willed by God
C) Because intention only applies to existent objects or their prerequisites metaphorically
D) Because non-existences are not subject to moral judgment

Key Takeaway
Intention does not attach to non-existences except metaphorically via intending something that entails the non-existence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you say you intend to stop a sin, what exactly are you intending? Are you aiming at the non-existence of that sin, or are you intending some action that prevents it? How does this lesson change the way you set your intentions for repentance?
?
The hadith says, 'What Allah wills occurs, and what He does not will does not occur.' Do you sometimes find yourself wishing that certain difficulties would simply 'not exist'? How does realizing that non-existence is not directly willed affect your trust in Allah's decree?
?
Examine your daily intentions: do you focus more on avoiding bad (intending non-existences) or on doing good (intending existences)? What specific shift can you make today to align your intention with the principle that intention only attaches to existents?

The Linguistic Scope of Pleasure
Gloss clarifying the linguistic restriction of 'pleasure' in the classification of motives.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the term 'pleasure' is used in a restricted sensory sense in this theological discussion, distinguishing it from benefit and likeness.

Core Idea
In this context, 'pleasure' means only sensory pleasure, not intellectual.

A potential confusion arises: if pleasure is the perception of what is agreeable, then imagining pleasure seems present in all three categories of motivation (pleasure, benefit, likeness). This gloss resolves the issue by clarifying that here 'pleasure' has its linguistic meaning, restricted to sensory experience.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once through, then read the literal translation. Focus on the structure: objection (فإن قلت) and answer (قلت).
Notice the reference to Sharh al-Maqāsid as supporting authority.
2
Identify the objection
The objection states that if pleasure is perception of the agreeable, then it is present in all three forms. Ask yourself why this is problematic.
The objection expects that pleasure, benefit, and likeness would overlap if pleasure is defined broadly.
3
Understand the answer
The answer restricts 'pleasure' to its linguistic sense: only sensory, not intellectual. This makes it a distinct category from benefit and likeness.
The commentator quotes Sharh al-Maqāsid to confirm that the lexical meaning of pleasure/pain is sensory only.
4
Apply to the broader discussion
Consider how this clarification affects the classification of motives: sensory pleasure, benefit (which may include intellectual), and likeness (which is neither).
This distinction is crucial for understanding the taxonomy of motivations in kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( أو منفعة أو مشاكلة فإن قلت : سيجيء أن اللذة إدراك الملائم فظاهر أن تخيل اللذة موجود في جميع الصور الثلاث فما معنى جعل قوله : أو منفعة أو مشاكلة قسيما لقوله : من لذة قلت : المراد باللذة هاهنا معناها اللغوي قال في شرح المقاصد : لا شك أن لفظة اللذة أو الألم بحسب اللغة إنما هو للحسي دون العقلي.
His statement: 'or benefit or likeness' — for if you say: it will come that pleasure is the perception of what is agreeable, so it is apparent that the imagination of pleasure exists in all three forms. So what is the meaning of making his statement 'or benefit or likeness' as a counterpart to his statement 'from pleasure'? I say: the intended meaning of pleasure here is its linguistic sense. He said in Sharh al-Maqāsid: there is no doubt that the word pleasure or pain, according to language, applies only to the sensory, not the intellectual.
Source pages p. 139

Commentary
This passage is a gloss on a phrase 'or benefit or likeness' (أو منفعة أو مشاكلة). It presents an objection: if pleasure is the perception of what is agreeable, then imagining pleasure is present in all three categories (pleasure, benefit, likeness), so why differentiate them? The answer is that in this context, 'pleasure' is used in its linguistic sense, referring only to sensory pleasure, not intellectual. The commentator cites Sharh al-Maqāsid to support that lexical usage restricts pleasure/pain to sensory experiences.

Persistence of Non-Existence Within Power
Gloss clarifies indirect power over non-existence's persistence

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how kalam scholars distinguish between direct and indirect power over the persistence of non-existence, and how the occurrence of the act resolves the issue.

Core Idea
Power over non-existence's persistence is indirect—through the act that ends it.

In this passage, the commentator clarifies a subtle point about the scope of divine power. The persistence of non-existence over time is not directly willed; rather, it is the act that removes that persistence that is within power. A follow-up objection and answer refine this idea, showing how even the renewed persistence of non-existence in a given day can be accounted for. This analysis sharpens our understanding of how power relates to privations and their temporal extensions.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Read the Arabic passage once for overall flow, then read the English translation. Note the two parts introduced by 'قوله'.
The passage is a continuous gloss; the two 'قوله' refer to phrases from the original text.
2
Analyze the first gloss
Identify the key distinction: power does not attach directly to the persistence of non-existence, but to the act that removes it. Understand why direct attachment would imply power over eternal non-existence.
Remember that originated power cannot attach to something eternal.
3
Examine the second gloss
Read the objection and response. Notice the objection: persistence would be originated, but original non-existence is eternal. The answer: persistence is renewed for each specific time, so it is not eternal.
Consider the phrase 'دوام استمرار عدم الفعل في هذا اليوم متجدد'.
4
Synthesize the lesson
Combine both parts into a coherent understanding: power over non-existence's persistence is always mediated by power over an act that changes the situation.
This is a standard kalam move to avoid attributing direct power to privations.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( دوام استمراره أي بقاء استمرار ذلك العدم في الأزمنة الآتية مقدور لا بمعنى أنه يتعلق به القدرة الحادثة حتى يلزم تعلق القدرة بالعدم الأزلي، بل بمعنى أنه يتعلق القدرة بالفعل فيزول استمرار العدم في الأزمنة الآتية بحدوث ذلك الفعل. قوله : ( وقد يقال دوام استمراره مقدور إلخ فإن قلت : يلزم على هذا حدوث ذلك الدوام وفي ملاحظة حدوثه على تقدير أزلية أصل العدم خفاء ظاهر قلت يمكن أن يقال : دوام استمرار عدم الفعل في هذا اليوم مثلاً متجدد، إذ لم يوجد في الأمر الدوام بالنسبة إلى هذا اليوم لكن الكلام في انطباق كلام الشارح على هذا وأيضاً يمكن أن يتصور مثله في نفس العدم أيضاً.
His statement: 'persistence of its continuance' — i.e., the subsistence of the persistence of that non-existence in future times is within power, not in the sense that originated power attaches to it such that it would necessitate the attachment of power to eternal non-existence, but rather in the sense that power attaches to the act, so the persistence of non-existence in future times ceases by the occurrence of that act. His statement: 'And it is said: the persistence of its continuance is within power, etc.' — for if you say: this entails the occurrence of that persistence, and in observing its occurrence on the assumption of the eternity of the original non-existence, there is manifest obscurity. I say: it can be said that the persistence of the non-existence of the act in this day, for example, is renewed, since there does not exist in the matter persistence with respect to this day. But the discussion is about the applicability of the commentator's words to this, and also one can conceive something similar in the non-existence itself.
Source pages p. 139

Commentary
This passage contains two related glosses on the phrase 'persistence of its continuance is within power' (دوام استمراره مقدور). The first gloss clarifies that power over the persistence of non-existence is indirect: it is not that power directly attaches to the eternal non-existence, but rather that power attaches to the act, which by occurring removes the persistence of non-existence in future times. The second gloss presents an objection: if the persistence is renewed (not eternal), then its occurrence is problematic given the original eternal non-existence. The answer suggests that the persistence of non-existence for a particular day is renewed, so it is not eternal, and this resolves the issue. The commentator also notes that a similar case can be conceived for non-existence itself.

Key Terms

العدم
القدرة الحادثة
العدم الأزلي
متجدد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Thinking that power directly attaches to non-existence itself, like willing non-existence into being.
Correction: Power only attaches to acts; non-existence's persistence is removed by performing the opposite act.

Checkpoint
How is the persistence of non-existence said to be within power according to the first gloss?
A) By directly willing the non-existence
B) By performing the act that eliminates the persistence of non-existence
C) By intending the non-existence from eternity
D) By choosing not to act

Key Takeaway
The persistence of non-existence falls within power indirectly, by performing the act that eliminates it.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In your own life, there is a non-existence of good deeds that persists day after day. What specific act are you avoiding that, once performed, would break that persistence? What is stopping you from doing it today—and how does this lesson's insight about indirect power through the act give you a way forward?

You claim you 'cannot' do something that is within your power because of its persistence. But this lesson shows that power does not directly target the persistence—it targets the act. What act have you been delaying that would, if done, remove the persistence of a bad habit or a missed duty? Will you commit to that act in the next 24 hours?

Consider a time you procrastinated a good deed. Did you treat the persistence of your failure as something fixed and external? This lesson says that persistence is renewed each day—so today is a new opportunity. What will you do to ensure that the persistence of your inaction does not continue beyond this moment?

Objection and Answer on the Condition of Both Opposites Being Within Power for Omission
Objection and answer on the condition that both opposites must be within power for omission.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the condition that both opposites must be within power for omission is understood on a substitutive basis, resolving the apparent difficulty.

Core Idea
Both opposites are within power not simultaneously but substitutively.

This passage presents a critical objection regarding the condition that both opposites must be within power for omission (tark). The objection uses the example of leaving prayer by performing its opposite, and the answer clarifies that power over both opposites is on a substitutive basis, not simultaneous. Understanding this distinction is essential for grasping how kalam reconciles divine power with human action.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
The objection assumes that if both opposites must be within power, then either they are simultaneous (leading to impossibility) or not (leading to loss of omission). Notice the dilemma structure.
The objection uses 'if you say' (فإن قلت) format.
2
Identify the two horns of the dilemma
The objection presents two possibilities: either prayer is within power while its opposite is within power (simultaneous), or it is not. Understand why each leads to a problem.
The first horn conflicts with the principle that power cannot attach to two opposites simultaneously.
3
Study the answer
The answer rejects the assumption of simultaneity and clarifies that power over each opposite is on a substitutive basis (على سبيل البدل). This resolves the dilemma.
This answer aligns with the earlier gloss about power attaching to each on a substitutive basis.
4
Reflect on the implications
Consider how this concept preserves both divine power and human responsibility in acts of omission.
This is a nuanced point in kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( لا بد أن يكون إلخ) بأن يصلح تعلق القدرة لكل منهما على سبيل البدل . قوله : ( أو منفعة أو مشاكلة فإن قلت : سيجيء أن اللذة إدراك الملائم فظاهر أن تخيل اللذة موجود في جميع الصور الثلاث فما معنى جعل قوله : أو منفعة أو مشاكلة قسيما لقوله : من لذة قلت : المراد باللذة هاهنا معناها اللغوي قال في شرح المقاصد : لا شك أن لفظة اللذة أو الألم بحسب اللغة إنما هو للحسي دون العقلي. قوله : ( وقد يقال دوام استمراره مقدور إلخ ) فإن قلت : يلزم على هذا حدوث ذلك الدوام وفي ملاحظة حدوثه على تقدير أزلية أصل العدم خفاء ظاهر قلت يمكن أن يقال : دوام استمرار عدم الفعل في هذا اليوم مثلاً متجدد، إذ لم يوجد في الأمر الدوام بالنسبة إلى هذا اليوم لكن الكلام في انطباق كلام الشارح على هذا وأيضاً يمكن أن يتصور مثله في نفس العدم أيضاً. قوله : ( قالوا : ولا بد أن يكون كلا الضدين إلخ ) فإن قلت : إذا اشترط ذلك فمن ترك الصلاة بفعل ضدها، فأما أن يقولوا بأنَّ الصلاة مقدورة حال كون ضدها مقدوراً أو لا يقولوا بذلك، فإن كان الأول فهو خلاف أصلهم في تعلق قدرة واحدة أو قدرتين بضدين معا ضرورة أن المقدور لا بد أن يكون مقارناً للقدرة عند تعلقها به وذلك يفضي إلى اجتماع الضدين وهو محال، وإن كان الثاني فالصلاة غير متروكة لفوات شرط الترك وهو خلاف الشرع واصطلاح العقلاء وأهل اللسان، قلت : ليس مرادهم كون الضدين في الترك مقدورين معاً بل على سبيل البدل وذلك لا ينافي ما ذكر.
His statement: 'They said: It is necessary that both opposites be within power, etc.' — in that power may be attachable to each of them on a substitutive basis. For if you say: if that is required, then consider one who leaves prayer by performing its opposite. Either they say that prayer is within power while its opposite is within power, or they do not say that. If the first, then it conflicts with their principle regarding one power or two powers attaching to two opposites simultaneously, for what is within power must be simultaneous with the power when it attaches to it, and that leads to the conjunction of two opposites, which is impossible. If the second, then prayer is not left due to the loss of the condition of omitting, which contradicts the Sacred Law and the custom of rational people and the people of language. I say: what they mean is not that both opposites in omitting are within power simultaneously, but rather on a substitutive basis, and that does not contradict what was mentioned.
Source pages p. 139

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer regarding the condition that both opposites must be within power for omission (tark). The objection uses the example of leaving prayer by performing its opposite: either the prayer is within power while its opposite is within power (which leads to the impossibility of two opposites being simultaneously within power, since power requires simultaneity with its effect), or it is not, which makes the omission invalid. The answer clarifies that the condition is not simultaneous power over both, but rather power over each on a substitutive basis (بدل), so only one is actualized. This resolves the objection without violating principles.

Key Terms
الضدين
قدرة واحدة
قدرتين
اجتماع الضدين
على سبيل البدل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Power over both opposites for omission must be simultaneous.
Correction: It is on a substitutive basis, not simultaneous.

Checkpoint
How does the answer resolve the objection about leaving prayer by its opposite?
A) By asserting that power over both opposites is simultaneous
B) By stating that power over each opposite is on a substitutive basis, not simultaneously
C) By denying that the opposite of prayer is within power
D) By saying that omission does not require power over the opposite

Key Takeaway
The condition for omission is that power over both opposites is on a substitutive basis, not simultaneously.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you miss a compulsory prayer because you were occupied with work or family, do you ever think that you couldn't have done both – as if the obligation was not within your power at that moment? How does the truth that both actions were possible (substitutively) before your choice challenge that excuse?
?
Think of a time you prioritized something permissible over an obligation. Did you tell yourself that the other matter was unavoidable? How does the principle of substitutive power strip away that rationalization and leave you facing your own choice?
?
If you truly believe that both opposites were within your power on a substitutive basis, what would you need to change in your daily decisions to live consistently with that belief – starting tomorrow?

Determination: Firmness of Will After Hesitation
Defines azm as firm will after hesitation and ties its validity to interpretation of will.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of determination (azm) as firmness of will after hesitation and how its validity depends on the interpretation of will.

Core Idea
Determination is will made firm after hesitation, but only if will means inclination.

This lesson introduces the concept of determination (azm) as one of the psychological states. The author defines it as the firmness of the will after a state of hesitation caused by conflicting motives. However, whether this definition holds depends on how we understand the nature of will itself. Let's explore the definition and its conditional validity.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice how the author defines azm and then adds a condition.
2
Identify the definition and process
Find the definition of azm and the description of hesitation leading to either perplexity or determination.
The hesitation arises from different motives: rational, desirous, and aversive.
3
Understand the conditional clause
The passage states that this definition is valid only if will is interpreted in a certain way. Identify the two interpretations of will and which one works.
The condition is introduced with 'إنما يصح إذا لم يفسرها...'.
4
Analyze the reasoning
Think about why the definition fails if will is the specifying attribute. Consider the timing and scope of the specifying attribute.
The specifying attribute may specify what is not desired and must be simultaneous with the act.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(الرابع) من الأمور ( العزم وهو جزم الإرادة بعد التردد الحاصل من الدواعي المختلفة المنبعثة من الآراء العقلية والشهوات والنفرات النفسانية، فإن لم يترجح أحد الطرفين حصل التحير وإن ترجح حصل العزم ( وهذا كله أي الذي ذكرناه في تفسير ما عدا الترك ( إنما يصح إذا لم يفسرها ) أي الإرادة ( بالصفة المخصصة لأحد طرفي المقدور بالوقوع ( بل بالميل أو ما يقتضيه من اعتقاد النفع أو ظنه أما إذا فسرناه بالصفة المخصصة فلا يصح لأن الصفة المخصصة قد تخصص ما لا يكون محبوباً ولا مرضياً، والعزم قد يكون سابقاً على الفعل الذي يجب أن تقارنه الصفة المخصصة.
The fourth of the matters is determination, and it is the firmness of the will after hesitation arising from different motives emanating from rational opinions, desires, and psychological aversions. For if neither side preponderates, perplexity occurs; and if one side preponderates, determination occurs. And all this, i.e., what we have mentioned in explaining other than omission, is valid only if will is not interpreted as the attribute that specifies one of the two sides of the possible for occurrence, but rather as inclination or what necessitates it from belief in benefit or its presumption. But if we interpret it as the specifying attribute, it is not valid, because the specifying attribute may specify what is neither beloved nor approved, and determination may be prior to the act which the specifying attribute must be simultaneous with.
Source pages p. 139

Commentary
This passage defines determination (azm) as the fourth of the psychological states. It is the firming of the will (jazm al-irāda) after hesitation (taraddud) resulting from various motives: rational opinions, desires, and aversions. If neither side prevails, there is perplexity (tahayyur); if one side prevails, determination occurs. The passage then explains that this account of determination and other states (excluding omission) is valid only if will (irāda) is interpreted as inclination (mayl) or its consequence (belief or presumption of benefit), not as the specifying attribute (al-ṣifa al-mukhaṣṣiṣa) that determines one possible. If will is the specifying attribute, then determination cannot be defined as firmness of will, because the specifying attribute could specify something not desired, and determination may precede the act whereas the specifying attribute must be simultaneous with the act. Thus, the interpretation of will affects the definition of determination.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Determination is simply firm resolve without any prior hesitation.
Correction: Determination specifically arises after hesitation from competing motives, as defined in the passage.

Checkpoint

A) When will is the specifying attribute (al-ṣifa al-mukhaṣṣiṣa)
B) When will is interpreted as inclination (mayl) or belief in benefit
C) Under any interpretation of will
D) Only when will is considered a created attribute

Key Takeaway
Determination is will firmed after hesitation, but its definition depends on interpreting will as inclination rather than as the specifying attribute.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a recent important decision you made after hesitation. Did you genuinely weigh the motives, or did you rush to determination? How does this lesson's process of preponderance apply to that decision—and what does it reveal about the sincerity of your will?
?
The passage implies that if will is the specifying attribute, your determination might not reflect what God actually wills for you. Does that possibility unsettle you? How do you personally reconcile your own resolutions with the belief that all events are determined by God's specifying will?
?
If determination is only valid when will is an inclination toward perceived benefit, consider a time your determination led to harm. Was it truly a determination of will, or was the fault in your belief about what was beneficial? What does that teach you about the relationship between knowledge, intention, and resolve in your life?

Pleasure and Pain Are Self-Evident
Explains that pleasure and pain are self-evident and cannot be defined.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why pleasure and pain are considered self-evident and why they cannot be defined.

Core Idea
Pleasure and pain are directly known by inner sensation, not by definition.

This passage establishes a key epistemological principle: some things are known directly without need for definition. Pleasure and pain are prime examples, as every sentient being perceives them from within and distinguishes them necessarily. Therefore, attempts to define their essence are misguided; at best, one may explain the name or mention properties to avoid verbal confusion.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then its translation. Notice the author's concise style and the argument's structure.
The passage includes original text and commentary interwoven.
2
Identify the main argument
Find the reason why pleasure and pain are self-evident. The author states 'because every rational being... perceives them from himself.'
The proof is immediate perception, not reasoning.
3
Understand the consequence
Why can't pleasure and pain be defined? Because inner sensation already gives knowledge of their essence, and definition cannot add to that.
The author contrasts 'definition of essence' with 'explanation of name'.
4
Examine the commentary's clarification
The commentary explains key phrases: 'self-evident' means knowledge from imagination, 'from himself' means intuitive, and 'explanation of name' only removes verbal confusion.
5
Reflect on the broader implication
Consider why this matters in kalam: it establishes that some concepts are foundational and cannot be further analyzed.
This avoids infinite regress in definitions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اللذة والألم بديهيان) لأنَّ كل عاقل؛ بل كل حساس يدركهما من نفسه، ويميز كل واحد منهما عن صاحبه، ويميزهما عما عداهما بالضرورة ) فلا يعرفان ) لتحصيل ماهيتهما ، فإن الإحساس الوجداني بجزئياتهما قد أفاد العلم بتلك الماهية على وجه لا يتأتى لنا تحصيل مثله بطريق الاكتساب كما في سائر المحسوسات على ما مر، وهذا مما لا يخفى على ذي إنصاف، نعم قد يقصد في المحسوسات شرح الاسم وذكر الخواص دفعا للالتباس اللفظي
قوله : ( بديهيان) أي بالكنه الإجمالي الحاصل من حصول جزئياتهما في الخيال وحذف مشخصاتها في سائر المحسوسات . 
قوله : (من نفسه أي وجداناً حاصلاً من نفسه لا من النظر. 
قوله على وجه لا يتأتى إلخ لأنَّ هذه حصول لماهيتها بأنفسهما والحاصل بالتعريف حصولها بالوجه بناء على أنَّ الاطلاع على ذاتيات الحقائق متعذر . 
قوله: (شرح الاسم ومآله التصديق بالوضع للالتباس اللفظي بين الأمور الحاصلة في الذهن لا تحصيل ما لم يكن حاصلاً .
Pleasure and pain are self-evident, because every rational being, indeed every sentient being, perceives them from himself, and distinguishes each one from the other, and distinguishes them from what is other than them by necessity. So they are not known for the purpose of acquiring their essence, for the inner sensation of their particulars has given knowledge of that essence in a way that we cannot acquire its like by means of acquisition, as in the case of other sensed things as has passed. And this is not hidden from anyone fair-minded. Yes, in the case of sensed things, explanation of the name and mention of properties may be intended to remove verbal confusion. His saying: (self-evident) that is, by the summary core knowledge obtained from the occurrence of their particulars in the imagination and the removal of their specifics as in other sensed things. His saying: (from himself) that is, obtained intuitively from himself, not from reflection. His saying: (in a way that we cannot acquire) etc. because these essences are obtained by themselves, and what is obtained by definition is obtained in a certain manner, based on the fact that insight into the essences of realities is unattainable. His saying: (explanation of the name) and its upshot is the affirmation of the designation to remove verbal confusion between things present in the mind, not the acquisition of what was not present.
Source pages p. 141

Commentary
This passage establishes that pleasure and pain are self-evident (badihiyyan). It argues that every rational or sentient being perceives them directly from within themselves and can distinguish them from each other and from other things by necessity. Therefore, they are not defined in order to acquire their essence, because the inner sensation (al-ihsas al-wijdani) of their particulars already gives knowledge of that essence in a way that cannot be acquired through definition, just like other sense objects. The author notes that for sense objects, one may only explain the name and mention properties to remove verbal confusion, not to acquire the essence. The commentary then explains each part: 'self-evident' means by the summary core knowledge from the imagination; 'from himself' means intuitive, not from reflection; the impossibility of acquisition is because the essence is grasped directly; and 'explanation of the name' aims at affirming the designation to avoid ambiguity, not at acquiring new knowledge.

الإحساس الوجداني
شرح الاسم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that pleasure and pain need a definition to be understood.
Correction: The passage shows they are known directly by inner sensation, so definition is unnecessary and impossible for acquiring their essence.

Checkpoint
Why are pleasure and pain considered self-evident according to this passage?
A) Because they are known by reason alone.
B) Because every sentient being perceives them directly from themselves by necessity.
C) Because they are defined in books.
D) Because they are complex concepts requiring proof.

Key Takeaway
Pleasure and pain are known directly through inner sensation, not through definition; any verbal explanation only clarifies the name.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You know that pleasure and pain are self-evident, yet you often seek external definitions or justifications for your feelings. When was the last time you trusted your own inner sensation of discomfort rather than letting others define what you should feel? Will you commit to honoring your direct perceptions, especially when they conflict with societal expectations?
?
The passage says every sentient being knows pleasure and pain from themselves. But you may have allowed intellectual doubts to distance you from your own immediate experience. What specific area of your life are you letting definitions from others override your own inner sense? Write down one 'definition' you need to discard today.
?
Reflect on a time you tried to 'prove' your pain or pleasure to someone. Did that attempt diminish the self-evident nature of your experience? How can you more confidently rest in the direct knowledge Allah has placed within you, without needing external validation?

The Definition of Pleasure and Pain and Its Three Conditions
Defines pleasure and pain and enumerates three necessary conditions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of pleasure and pain as perception of agreeable/disagreeable and the three necessary conditions for their occurrence.

Core Idea
Pleasure and pain require perception, agreeableness/disagreeableness, and actual attainment.

This passage from al-Ījī's 'Mawāqif' clarifies what pleasure (al-ladhdha) and pain (al-alam) truly are. It begins by defining them as perception of the agreeable and disagreeable, but immediately notes that three conditions must be met for them to occur. Understanding these conditions prevents confusion between mere imagination and actual experience.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis. Then read the translation to grasp the overall meaning.
Notice the concise style of the original.
2
Identify the three conditions
Locate the three conditions listed for pleasure and pain. Note the author's order and initial explanations.
The conditions are: perception, agreeable/disagreeable, and attainment.
3
Understand the necessity of attainment
Focus on why attainment (nayl) is required. Consider the counterexample of imagining sweetness – why doesn't that produce actual pleasure?
This distinguishes real experience from mere imagination.
4
Reflect on the grammatical note
Examine the commentator's note about the preposition 'min' being interpreted as 'fi'. See how linguistic precision supports theological meaning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( إدراك الملائم إلخ لا خفاء في أنه لا بد في اللذة والألم من أمور ثلاثة : الإدراك وكون المدرك ملائماً أو منافراً في اعتقاده، ونيل نفس ذلك المدرك، أما الأولان فظاهران إذ بانتفائهما تنتفي اللذة والألم، وأما الثالث فلأنه إذا لم يكن نيل هناك بل اللذة كان رضاك تخيل اللذة والألم لا يتصفهما كما في تخيل الحلاوة والمرارة ولا يختلجن في ذهنك التباس تخيل
قوله : ( يدركهما من نفسه الأقرب أنَّ من بمعنى في كما في قوله تعالى أروني ماذا خلقوا من الأرض ﴾ [فاطر: ٤٠] .
His saying: (perception of the agreeable etc.) There is no hidden that pleasure and pain necessarily involve three matters: perception, the perceived being agreeable or disagreeable in one's belief, and the attainment of that perceived. As for the first two, they are apparent, for by their negation pleasure and pain are negated. As for the third, because if there is no attainment, but rather the pleasure is your imagining of pleasure and pain, they are not characterized by them, as in imagining sweetness and bitterness, and let not confusion of imagining enter your mind. His saying: (he perceives them from himself) the closest is that 'min' is in the sense of 'fi' as in His statement 'Show Me what they have created from the earth' [Fatir:40].
Source pages p. 141

Commentary
This passage explains the definition of pleasure and pain. It states that pleasure is perception of the agreeable (idrak al-mula'im) and pain is perception of the disagreeable (idrak al-munafir), and then analyzes the necessary conditions. Three matters are required: (1) perception itself, (2) the perceived being agreeable or disagreeable in the belief of the perceiver, and (3) the attainment (nayl) of that perceived thing. The first two are obvious because without them pleasure and pain cease to exist. The third is necessary because if there is no actual attainment, but only imagination of pleasure or pain (like imagining sweetness), one does not actually experience pleasure or pain. The commentator also notes a grammatical point: the word 'min' in 'from himself' can be interpreted as 'fi' (in), citing a Quranic verse.

Key Terms
إدراك الملائم
إدراك المنافر
النيل
الأمور الثلاثة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think imagining pleasure (like imagining sweetness) is the same as experiencing pleasure.
Correction: The passage states that without actual attainment (nayl), one does not truly experience pleasure or pain; imagination yields only a mental representation, not the real thing.

Checkpoint
What are the three necessary conditions for pleasure and pain according to this passage?
A) Perception, imagination, and memory.
B) Perception, agreeableness/disagreeableness in belief, and attainment.
C) Sensation, emotion, and cognition.
D) Pleasure, pain, and indifference.

Key Takeaway
Pleasure and pain are not mere perceptions; they require actual attainment of the agreeable or disagreeable, preventing confusion with imagination.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a pleasure you chase repeatedly—social media approval, a hobby, a relationship. Does the passage's 'attainment' condition reveal that much of what you call pleasure is actually just imagination? What would it take to truly attain what you seek, and are you willing to do that today?
?
The passage teaches that pleasure requires not just any object but one you believe is agreeable. What are you currently believing to be good for you that the Qur'an or sound intellect calls harmful? Could your 'sweetness' be poison? Commit to re-examining one such belief this week.
?
You have likely experienced the hollow feeling after chasing a desire and realizing the anticipation was greater than the reality. How does this passage expose your tendency to settle for imagined pleasure? What is one concrete step you can take tonight to pursue a real, attainable good—like sincere worship or a hard conversation—instead of its shadow?
