Objection and Answer on Color in Khilqah
The commentator raises an objection about color not being specific to quantity and provides an answer

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that color is not specific to quantity and how the commentator resolves it by linking color to the surface.

Core Idea
Color can be quantity-specific because it is a property of the surface.

In this passage, the commentator addresses a potential objection to the classification of khilqah as a quality specific to quantities. The objection points out that while shape is quantity-specific, color is a perceived quality and not obviously so. The answer reframes color as a property of the surface, thereby linking it to quantity. This lesson unpacks the objection and its rebuttal.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the objection in the passage: shape is quantity-specific, but color is not, so how can a composite of both be quantity-specific?
Notice the logical problem: a composite of two different types does not fall under either.
2
Understand the answer's key move
The answer argues that color is a property of the surface, and since the surface is a quantity, color becomes quantity-specific. There is no contradiction between being perceived and being quantity-specific.
Consider how this resolves the objection by making both components quantity-specific.
3
Reflect on the commentator's critique
The commentator notes that this interpretation does not fully align with the author's phrase 'with something else', suggesting a different nuance. Consider what the author might have intended.
This shows the layered nature of scholarly commentary.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أورد على هذا أنَّ الشكل وإن كان الكيفيات من المختصة بالكميات إلا أن اللون من الكيفيات المحسوسة فكيف تكون الخلقة المركبة منهما من قبيل الكيفيات المختصة بالكميات والمركب من نوع ومما ليس ذلك النوع لا يندرج في أحدهما؟ وأجيب بأن هذا مبني على أن اللون من خواص السطح، وعلى هذا يكون اللون أيضاً من الكيفيات المختصة بالكميات، ولا تنافي بين كون الكيفية محسوسة وكونها مختصة بالكم، وأما إنَّ الكلام في الكيفية المفردة فكيف يعد الخلقة المركبة من الكيفيتين مما نحن فيه فسيجيء جوابه، وأنت خبير بأن هذا التوجيه لا يلائم قول المصنف : وإما مع غيرها : كالخلقة فإنه لم يعتبر في الخلقة على هذا التوجيه غير الكيفية المختصة بالكميات لأن كلا جزأيها حينئذ منها فالأنسب لكلام المصنف أن يقال: اللون، وإن لم يكن من الكيفيات المختصة بالكميات لنفوذه في الأعماق، إلا أنه يصدق على المركب منه ، ومن الشكل أنه كيفية مختصة بها، كما لا
It was objected against this that shape, although it is among the qualities specific to quantities, yet color is among the perceived qualities, so how can khilqah, composed of the two, be of the type of qualities specific to quantities? For a composite of a type and that which is not that type does not fall under either one. And it was answered: This is based on the fact that color is a property of the surface, and on this basis color is also among the qualities specific to quantities, and there is no contradiction between a quality being perceived and being specific to quantity. As for the discussion about the simple quality, how then is khilqah, composed of two qualities, counted among that which we are discussing, its answer will come. And you are well aware that this interpretation does not fit the author's statement: 'or with something else, like khilqah,' because he did not consider in khilqah, on this interpretation, anything other than qualities specific to quantities, since both of its parts are then from them. So the most appropriate for the author's statement is to say: color, even if it is not among the qualities specific to quantities due to its penetration into depths, yet it is true of the composite of it and shape that it is a quality specific to them, as not.
Source pages p. 159

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and its answer regarding the example of khilqah (form). The objection states that shape is indeed specific to quantity, but color is a perceived quality and not specific to quantity, so a composite of shape and color cannot be a quality specific to quantity. The answer is that this objection depends on the assumption that color is a property of the surface; if so, color too becomes specific to quantity because surface is a quantity. There is no contradiction between being perceived and being quantity-specific. Further, the objection about a simple quality is deferred. Then the commentator notes that the interpretation that both parts are quantity-specific does not align with the author's phrase 'with something else,' because both parts would then be quantity-specific, leaving nothing else. The better interpretation is that color, though not quantity-specific due to its penetration into depth, still makes the composite (shape+color) quantity-specific because shape is quantity-specific, and color is an additional factor that accompanies the quantity-specific quality.

Imam al-Razi on Qualities Specific to Quantities
Al-Razi defines qualities specific to quantities and discusses their bearers.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Imam al-Razi's definition of qualities specific to quantities and how he argues that shape, khilqah, color, and light fall under this category.

Core Idea
Qualities 'specific to quantities' always have a quantity as their primary bearer.

Imam al-Razi builds on the earlier classification of qualities to define a special subtype: those that primarily attach to quantities. This passage is important because it shows how shape, texture (khilqah), color, and even light are all rooted in quantity, despite common sense suggesting otherwise. The argument includes an objection and its resolution, revealing the subtlety of this classification.

Guided Path
1
Understand the definition
Read the opening sentence where al-Razi defines qualities specific to quantities as those that occur first and essentially to quantities.
2
Identify the two types
Notice the distinction between qualities that attach to quantity for itself (like shape) and those that attach via a part (like khilqah through shape).
3
Follow the objection and answer
See how al-Razi handles the objection that khilqah seems to belong to the natural body, not quantity, by distinguishing two ways of being accidental to quantity.
4
Examine the claim about color and light
Al-Razi argues that color's primary bearer is the surface (a quantity), so it too falls under this type. Consider the counterpoint about penetration.
This extends the definition to cover color and light.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الرازي هذا النوع من الكيفيات هو الكيفية التي تعرض أولا، وبالذات للكميات وبتوسطها لغيرها ويدخل في ذلك ما يكون كذلك إما لنفسه كالشكل العارض للمقدار، وإما لجزئه كالخلقة فإنها كذلك بواسطة جزئها الذي هو الشكل، فإن قيل: الخلقة عارضة للجسم الطبيعي إذ لولاه لم يكن خلقة قلنا العارض للكمية إما أن يعرض لها من حيث أنها كمية أو من حيث أنها كمية شيء مخصوص، وكلا القسمين عارض للكمية ثم إن اللون حامله الأول هو السطح الذي هو نهاية الجسم الطبيعي بتوسط الجسم التعليمي، ومعنى كون الجسم ملوناً أن سطحه ملون فكلا جزئي الخلقة حامله الأول هو المقدار فالخلقة عارضة بالذات للكم، قال : ويتوجه على هذا أن يكون اللون والضوء داخلين في هذا النوع من الكيفيات، لأنَّ حاملهما الأول هو السطح إذ لا لون ولا ضوء في عمق الجسم، وقد يقال : اللون قد يكون نافذاً داخل الجسم وكذلك الضوء في المضيء بالذات كالشمس فلا يختصان بالسطح والمتبادر
Al-Razi said: This type of quality is the quality that occurs first and essentially to quantities and through their mediation to other things. Included in this is that which is so either for itself, like shape that is accidental to magnitude, or for its part, like khilqah, for it is so by means of its part which is shape. For if it is said: Khilqah is accidental to the natural body, since without it there would be no khilqah, we say: That which is accidental to quantity either occurs to it insofar as it is quantity, or insofar as it is the quantity of a specific thing, and both divisions are accidental to quantity. Then, color: its primary bearer is the surface, which is the boundary of the natural body, mediated by the mathematical body. The meaning of a body being colored is that its surface is colored. So both parts of khilqah have their primary bearer as magnitude, so khilqah is essentially accidental to quantity. He said: It follows from this that color and light enter into this type of quality, because their primary bearer is the surface, since there is no color or light in the depth of a body. And it may be said: Color may penetrate inside the body, as well as light in something intrinsically luminous like the sun, so they are not exclusive to the surface. And the apparent meaning is
Source pages p. 160

Commentary
This passage presents Imam al-Razi's definition and analysis. He states that qualities specific to quantities are those that primarily and essentially attach to quantities, and via them to other things. They are either for themselves (like shape for magnitude) or for their part (like khilqah, which is quantity-specific through its part, shape). An objection is raised: khilqah is accidental to the natural body, not directly to quantity. The answer is that the accidental can relate to quantity in two ways: either directly as quantity or as quantity of a specific thing; both are tied to quantity. Then al-Razi explains that color's primary bearer is the surface (a quantity), so color too is essentially attached to quantity. Thus both parts of khilqah (shape and color) have magnitude as their primary bearer, making khilqah essentially accidental to quantity. This leads to the conclusion that color and light should also be included in this type, since their primary bearer is the surface. However, a counterpoint is raised that color and light can penetrate bodies, so they are not exclusive to the surface.

الجسم التعليمي

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Khilqah is accidental to the natural body, not to quantity.
Correction: Al-Razi clarifies that khilqah is accidental to quantity either directly or via its part (shape), and both are considered accidental to quantity.

Checkpoint

B) The mathematical body
C) The surface
D) The depth

Key Takeaway
Imam al-Razi argues that shape, khilqah, color, and light are all qualities specific to quantities because their primary bearer is a quantity (magnitude or surface).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

The lesson teaches that light itself is a quality specific to quantity. In your spiritual life, are you more focused on the 'light' of knowledge (which is accidental) or the Source of that light? What would it mean to prioritize the Substance over the quality in your relationship with Allah?

The Angle as a Simple Quality Specific to Quantity
Definition of angle and its simplicity vs. khilqah's compositeness

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the angle (al-zawiyah) is a simple quality specific to quantity, not composite like khilqah.

Core Idea
The angle is a pure shape, not mixed with color or other qualities.

In this passage, the commentator clarifies a common misconception: although the phrase 'and like the angle' might suggest the angle is composite like khilqah, he argues it is not. The definition of angle as a 'configuration of two lines encompassing' shows it belongs purely to quantity. Let's see why.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on the angle
Read the Arabic text that begins with 'والمتبادر قوله : ( وكالزاوية)' and the translation. Notice the apparent meaning and its correction.
Focus on the definition provided.
2
Identify the definition of angle
Find the phrase 'فإنها هيئة إحاطة الضلعين' and understand that this definition places the angle solely in the category of shape, without any other quality.
3
Contrast with khilqah
Recall that khilqah is composite (shape + color). The angle is not composite; it is a simple configuration.
The commentator uses this contrast to emphasize the angle's simplicity.
4
Note the significance
Understand that this classification matters for the broader division of qualities: some are specific to quantity alone, others require natural bodies.
The angle is a pure mathematical entity.

قوله : (إذ لا لون ولا ضوء في عمق الجسم وعلى هذا المذهب بنوا كلامهم حيث قالوا : عروض الألوان للسطوح إنما هو أولا وبالذات ولغيرها بواسطتها .
قوله : ( فإنها هيئة إحاطة إلخ قيل : ليس في هذا التعريف ما يحترز به عن القوسين إذا
And the apparent meaning of his statement: (and like the angle) is that the angle is like khilqah in that it is composed of a quality specific to quantities with something else, but it is not so, as indicated by his statement: (for it is a configuration of the two lines encompassing...
His statement: (for without it etc.) i.e., the occurrence of khilqah can only be conceived for a natural body, unlike the quality specific to quantity, for it only needs matter in existence, not in conception, as established in the division of wisdom into natural, mathematical, and divine.
His statement: (then color) i.e., after you have understood the state of khilqah in terms of its part with something else, that other is also a quality specific to quantity, so that does not contradict the other also being a quality specific to quantity.
His statement: (entering this type etc.) and what occurs in Sharh al-Tajrid and Sharh Hikmat al-'Ayn that there is no contradiction between a quality being specific to quantity... then in it there is that it necessarily results that the reality is one genus at one level, which is impossible, unless it is said that the four divisions are not intermediate genera, since quality is not a highest genus, and as a result many of their claims become invalid.
His statement: (and it may be said etc.) is a counterpart to his statement: then color has its primary bearer etc., so on this view the meaning of his statement: 'with something else' is 'with something other than the quality specific to quantity' absolutely.
It is obscure, and know that their discussion fluctuates over whether khilqah is the sum of shape and color, or shape adjoined to color, or a quality resulting from their combination, and this is closer to making them a separate type.
His statement: (like khilqah, for it etc.) The example is according to the people's claim, otherwise he will now mention that each of its parts has its primary bearer as magnitude and that it is essentially accidental to quantity, so consider.
His statement: (for there is no color or light in the depth of the body) and upon this school they based their discussion where they said: The occurrence of colors on surfaces is first and essentially, and on others by means of them.
His statement: (for it is a configuration of encompassing etc.) It is said: There is nothing in this definition that excludes the two arcs if...
Source pages p. 160

Commentary
This passage deals with the angle (الزاوية) as a potential quality specific to quantity. The apparent meaning of the author's phrase 'and like the angle' suggests the angle is composite like khilqah, but the commentator argues it is not. The definition of angle as 'a configuration of two lines encompassing' (هيئة إحاطة الضلعين) indicates it is purely a shape, not combined with another quality like color. The passage also includes several commentary notes: (1) Khilqah requires a natural body to be conceived, unlike pure quantity-specific qualities. (2) The discussion about color continues, emphasizing that shape's accompanier can also be quantity-specific. (3) A note from Sharh al-Tajrid and Sharh Hikmat al-'Ayn is critiqued about the classification of divisions. (4) The meaning of 'with something else' is clarified to mean 'with something other than the quantity-specific quality'. (5) There is uncertainty about whether khilqah is the sum of shape and color, shape adjoined to color, or a resultant quality. (6) The example of khilqah is said to be provisional. (7) The statement about color and light not penetrating is reiterated. (8) The definition of angle is critiqued for not excluding arcs.

Key Terms

هيئة إحاطة الضلعين
الخلقة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The angle is a composite quality like khilqah, combining shape with another quality like color.
Correction: The angle is simply a configuration of two lines, a pure quantity-specific quality.

B) As a configuration of two lines encompassing
C) As a type of natural body
D) As a sum of parts

Key Takeaway
The angle is a simple quality specific to quantity, defined as a configuration of two lines, not composite like khilqah.

Classification of Qualities and the Nature of Khilqah
Examination of khilqah's nature and classification problems

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the debate over whether khilqah is composite and how the classification of qualities into specific categories affects the discussion.

Core Idea
Khilqah is composite; its parts have different bearers.

The commentator continues by examining khilqah (complexion) in more detail. He evaluates different interpretations: is khilqah the sum of shape and color, a combination, or a resultant quality? The discussion also touches on the bearer of these qualities and critiques positions from Sharh al-Tajrid.

Guided Path
1
Read the extended discussion on khilqah
Read from 'ثم إن اللون' to 'ففيه أنه يلزم أن يكون الحقيقة واحدة جنسان في مرتبة واحدة' and the translation.
Pay attention to the different views.
2
Identify the three interpretations of khilqah
The passage mentions three possibilities: (1) sum of shape and color, (2) shape adjoined to color, (3) a quality resulting from their combination.
The third interpretation is considered closer to making them a separate type.
3
Understand the critique of classification
The commentator criticizes a view from Sharh al-Tajrid that there is no contradiction between a quality being specific to quantity and also being in another category. He argues this would make a single reality belong to two genera at the same level, which is impossible unless quality is not a highest genus.
4
Note the clarification of 'with something else'
The phrase 'with something else' in the context of khilqah means 'with something other than the quantity-specific quality'.
This clarifies the compositeness.

?

Why an Angle Is Not a Quantity: The Doubling Argument
Refutation of angle as quantity using doubling.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why angles cannot belong to the category of quantity (kamm) based on the property of doubling.

Core Idea
Doubling an angle can annihilate it; doubling a quantity always increases it.

In this passage, the author refutes the claim that an angle is a quantity (kamm) by focusing on the effect of doubling. If an angle were a quantity, doubling it would increase it. But we observe that doubling certain angles (like a right angle) makes them disappear entirely. This contradiction shows that angles are not from the category of quantity.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and its translation
Start by reading the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the English translation to get the overall argument.
Notice the parentheses indicate explanatory insertions.
2
Identify the two main parts
The passage first answers an objection regarding essential vs accidental properties, then presents a direct proof using doubling. This step-by-step structure is common in kalam refutations.
3
Examine the key examples
Focus on the examples: right angle annihilated by one doubling, acute angle of half right annihilated by two doublings. Understand why this contradicts the nature of quantity.
Also consider the nuance that some angles are not entirely annihilated.
4
Reflect on the logical conclusion
Ask yourself: if angle were a quantity, doubling would increase it, but instead it disappears. Therefore, angle is not from the category of quantity. The author shows the argument holds even for partial annihilation.
This is a clear case of demonstrating a negative property through a characteristic that quantity does not have.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والجواب أنه أي هذا الاستدلال (إنما يتم أنَّ لو كان عروض ذلك ) التفاوت والتجزؤ ( لها ) أي للزاوية (بالذات) حتى يلزم كونها كماً ( وأنه ممنوع بل) عروضه لها يجوز أن يكون ) لأنه ( أي لأن هذا المعروض الذي هو الزاوية ( عارض للكم) كما في الشكل، فإنه يعرض له ذلك بواسطة معروضه الذي هو الكم ( ويبطله ) أي يبطل كون الزاوية من الكم ( أنها تبطل بالتضعيف وتنعدم أما القائمة فإنها كلها تبطل بالتضعيف مرة واحدة بحيث لا تبقى هناك زاوية أصلا وأما الحادة فإنها تبطل إذا كانت نصف قائمة بالتضعيف ،مرتين كذلك ( بخلاف الكم فإنه يزيد ) بالتضعيف ولا يبطل فلا تكون الزاوية القائمة ولا الحادة المذكورة من مقولة الكم، فلا يكون مطلق الزاوية هذه المقولة أيضاً، ولو أبدل التضعيف بالزيادة لشمل البطلان الزوايا كلها فإنَّ كل زواية زيد عليها ما يجعلها مساوية لقائمتين لم يبق هناك زاوية أصلاً، وأما التضعيف فقد لا يبطل المنفرجة ولا الحادة التي هي أصغر من نصف قائمة أو أكبر منه، إذ أن يبقى هناك زاوية في الجهة الأخرى من الخط الآخر، نعم يلزم من تضعيف المنفرجة بطلان بعضها وكذا الحال في تلك الحادة إذا ضعفت مراراً، وقد يكتفى بذلك في الاستدلال لأنَّ الكم إذا ضعف لم يبطل منه شيء بل يزداد أبداً
And the answer is that this reasoning is only valid if the occurrence of that variation and division is essential to the angle, so that it would necessarily be a quantity. But this is denied; rather its occurrence may be accidental because the angle is an accident of quantity, like in a figure, it occurs to it via its substrate which is quantity. And it refutes the claim that angle is quantity because it is annihilated by doubling: the right angle is entirely annihilated by one doubling so that no angle remains, and the acute angle is annihilated by two doublings if it is half a right angle. But quantity increases by doubling and is not annihilated. Therefore neither the right angle nor the mentioned acute angle are from the category of quantity, so the absolute angle is not from this category either. And if one replaces doubling with increase, it would cover the annihilation of all angles, because every angle that is increased to equal two right angles leaves no angle. As for doubling, it may not annihilate the obtuse angle or the acute angle that is smaller or larger than half a right angle, because an angle remains on the other side of the other line. Yes, from doubling the obtuse angle, part of it is annihilated, and similarly for that acute angle if doubled repeatedly. And one may be content with that in reasoning, because quantity when doubled nothing from it is annihilated, but it always increases.
Source pages p. 162

Commentary
This passage presents a refutation of the claim that an angle belongs to the category of quantity (kamm). It first answers an objection: the reasoning that angle is quantity would only hold if variation and division were essential to it, but that is denied; they may be accidental via quantity itself. Then it gives a direct proof: angle is annihilated by doubling, whereas quantity increases by doubling. Examples: a right angle becomes a straight line (disappears) upon one doubling; an acute angle of half right angle disappears after two doublings. Therefore, angle cannot be quantity. The passage then discusses a nuance: if one replaces doubling with increase (making the angle equal to two right angles), all angles are annihilated. But with doubling, some angles (obtuse or certain acute) are not entirely annihilated; part remains on the other side. Still, the doubling causes some annihilation (partial loss), unlike quantity which only increases. So the proof stands.

Key Terms
زاوية (angle)
كم (quantity)
تضعيف (doubling)
قائمة (right angle)
حادة (acute angle)
منفرجة (obtuse angle)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that the argument applies to all angles equally, i.e., every angle is entirely annihilated by doubling.
Correction: The author specifies that obtuse angles and certain acute angles are not entirely annihilated by doubling; a portion remains on the other side. However, even partial annihilation contradicts the nature of quantity, so the argument still holds.

Checkpoint
Why does the author argue that an angle cannot be from the category of quantity (kamm)?
A) Because angles are abstract entities.
B) Because doubling an angle annihilates it, whereas quantity increases with doubling.
C) Because angles are not divisible.
D) Because quantity must be continuous.

Key Takeaway
Because doubling annihilates (or partially annihilates) an angle but always increases a quantity, an angle cannot belong to the category of quantity (kamm).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
What 'angles' in your life—things you treat as measurable and accumulable—might actually be annihilated when you try to double them? For example, do you count your good deeds as a quantity that grows, while neglecting that pride can annihilate them?
?
The argument distinguishes essential from accidental properties. Are there aspects of your faith that you consider essential (e.g., prayer, charity) but you treat them as accidental—doing them only when convenient?
?
If you are annihilated by increase (like an angle), what does that say about the nature of your soul? Are you building a self that grows with doubling, or one that vanishes at the first increase? What needs to change?

Geometrical Entities Are Imaginary Yet Yield Certain Knowledge
The author asserts geometrical entities are imaginary and defends their certainty.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the claim that geometrical entities are imaginary, why their external existence is not known, and how they can still yield certain judgments, using the analogy of numbers.

Core Idea
Imaginary entities can produce certain knowledge, just like numbers.

This passage addresses a key methodological issue: although geometrical entities such as lines and points are not physically real, the judgments derived from them are unquestionably certain. The commentator defends this position against an objection by drawing an analogy with numbers, which are mental constructs yet yield indisputable truths. We'll trace the argument from the initial claim to the final rebuttal.

Guided Path
1
Read the author's claim
Read the author's statement that geometrical entities are imaginary and their external existence is unknown. Notice how this challenges the common assumption that geometry deals with real objects.
2
Understand why external existence is unknown
The commentator explains that knowledge of external existence depends on the body being continuous. If matter is composed of indivisible atoms, then continuous magnitude exists only in the imagination.
This relates to the Kalam atomism debate.
3
Consider the objection to certainty
The objection asks: even if we grant they are imaginary, how can we have certainty? And is there any value in knowing about imaginary things?
4
See the analogy with numbers
The commentator responds by pointing to numbers: they are composed of units that are mental entities, yet arithmetic yields certain truths. Similarly, geometry can be certain.
This is the central analogy.
5
Grasp the rebuttal about correspondence
Finally, the commentator argues that imaginary attributes can belong to real objects, so judgments about them apply to reality. This is evident in astronomy, which uses geometry and arithmetic.
This shows the practical value of imaginary entities.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال المصنف : (وهذا) الذي ذكره المهندسون من الخطوط والسطوح والمجسمات كله أمور وهمية لا يعلم وجودها خارجاً وعليها مبنى علمهم الذي عنه قوله : ( والعبارة الظاهرة فإن عبارة المتن يوهم أنَّ للمربع ،ضلعين، وأنه قد لا يكون متوازي الأضلاع من حيث أنها كمية لشيء مخصوص كالخلقة. قوله : (من الضلعين (الثابتين إنما كانا ثابتين لأن أحد طرفيهما متصل بالضلع الثابت والطرف الثاني متصل بالمتحرك، كما كان متصلاً حال عدم الحركة فلا يكون بذاته، وإن حصل الحركة بما يعرض ولذا أحدتث القاعدة . قوله : ( حصل المخروط المستدير) أي المستدير القائم وهو ما يكون سهمه عموداً على قاعدته وأما المستدير الغير القائم فيحصل من حركة الضلع الغير المحيط بالقائمة إلى وضع الاستقامة بأنَّ يصير المتحرك الضلع الثابت خطاً واحداً . قوله: (لا يعلم وجودها خارجاً لتوقفه على كون الجسم متصلاً في نفسه فإنه إذا كان مركباً من أجزاء لا تتجزئ، فلا مقدار إلا في الوهم. الضلعين بالذكر فيما له أربعة أضلاع متساوية، وقد يقال : عبارة الشارح يتناول المسدس مع أنه لا يحصل منه أسطوانة مستديرة فينبغي أن يقيد الأضلاع بالأربعة. مثلاً يدعون فيه اليقين وقد يقال : قامت البراهين على وجودها في مواضعها، وإن سلم كونها أموراً وهمية فلا ينافي ذلك كون أحكامها يقينية، ألا ترى أن العدد المركب من الوحدات التي هي أمور اعتبارية له أحكام صادقة بلا شبهة، ومن أنكر كونها يقينية فقد كابر وكذا الحال في المباحث الهندسية يعلمها من يزاولها، فإن قيل : لا كمال في معرفة أحوال الموهومات قلنا : إِنَّ الموهومات قد تكون عارضة في نفس الأمر للأعيان الموجودة فيحصل لتلك الأعيان بسبب ذلك أحكام مطابقة للواقع، وقد يستدل بأحكام الأمور الوهمية على أحوال الأمور العينية ولا يخفى شيء من ذلك على من له شعور ببراهين علم الهيئة من الحساب والهندسة )
The author said: 'These (things) that the engineers mentioned of lines, surfaces, and solids are all imaginary matters; their existence outside is not known, and upon them is based their science from which...' His statement: 'And the apparent expression, for the expression of the text implies that the square has two sides, and that it may not be a parallelogram, because it is a quantity for a specific thing like form (khalqah).' His statement: 'From the two fixed sides, they are fixed because one of their ends is connected to the fixed side and the other end is connected to the moving side, just as it was connected in the state of no motion, so it is not by itself; and if motion occurs by what accidentally happens, therefore the base arises.' His statement: 'The circular cone resulted' meaning the upright circular cone, which is the one whose altitude is perpendicular to its base. As for the non-upright circular cone, it results from the motion of the side that does not surround the right angle to the position of straightness, such that the moving side becomes one line with the fixed side. His statement: 'Its existence outside is not known because it depends on the body being continuous in itself; for if it is composed of indivisible parts, then there is no magnitude except in imagination.' And the two sides are mentioned in what has four equal sides, and it may be said: the expression of the commentator encompasses the hexagon, even though a circular cylinder does not result from it, so it should be restricted to four sides. For example, they claim certainty in it, and it may be said: proofs have been established for their existence in their places; and if it is granted that they are imaginary matters, that does not preclude their judgments being certain. Do you not see that number composed of units, which are mental entities, has true judgments without doubt? Whoever denies that they are certain has obstinately denied. Likewise is the case in geometric investigations; whoever practices them knows. For if it is said: 'There is no perfection in knowing the states of imaginary things,' we say: 'Indeed, imaginary things may sometimes be accidental to actually existing objects, so those objects acquire due to that judgments that correspond to reality; and one may infer from the judgments of imaginary matters to the states of actual things. And nothing of that is hidden from one who has awareness of the proofs of astronomy from arithmetic and geometry.'
Source pages p. 164

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the author's (al-‘Allāmah's) statement that geometrical entities are imaginary. It begins by quoting the author: 'All that the engineers mention of lines, surfaces, and solids are imaginary matters; their external existence is not known, and their science is built upon them.' Then the commentator explains several points: first, a note on the apparent wording of the text concerning the square having 'two sides' and the issue of it being a parallelogram; second, an explanation of the 'two fixed sides' in the generation of the cone; third, a clarification of the upright vs. non-upright cone; fourth, the reason external existence is not known—it requires the body to be continuous, but if it is composed of indivisible parts, no magnitude exists except in imagination. Then there is a note about square and hexagon in relation to the cylinder. The passage then presents an objection: proofs have been established for the existence of these things, and even if they are imaginary, their judgments can be certain, as is the case with numbers composed of units (which are mental). The objector claims there is no perfection in knowing imaginary things. The answer: imaginary things may correspond to actual objects, and knowledge of them yields true judgments and can be used to infer about real things. This is evident to anyone familiar with astronomy and its proofs from arithmetic and geometry.

عدد
وحدات اعتبارية
براهين

Correction: The passage uses the example of numbers (composed of mental units) to show that imaginary entities can have certain judgments.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
Even though geometrical entities are imaginary, their judgments are certain, and they often correspond to real objects, making them valuable for sciences like astronomy.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You rely on mathematics every day, yet its objects are imaginary. Does this shake your confidence in the certainty of knowledge? Or does it reveal that truth is not limited to physical existence? What would it mean for you to treat your principles as certain even when they are not visible?
?

?
Astronomy uses geometry to infer truths about the cosmos. What 'imaginary' frameworks (ideals, goals, or beliefs) do you use to navigate your life? Are you willing to follow them to their logical conclusions, even if the end result is not yet visible?

Clarifying the Author's Intention on Imaginary Things
Clarification that the denial of perfection does not deny existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the author's denial of perfection in knowing imaginary things does not deny their external existence or the certainty of such knowledge, but rather distinguishes between certain knowledge and the soul's perfection, which consists in knowing actual existents.

Core Idea
Certainty is not enough; true perfection is knowing existent things as they are.

Before this passage, a question was raised: if knowledge of imaginary things is certain, why does the author deny it is perfect? The author clarifies that his intention is not to deny their existence or the correspondence of knowledge to reality. Rather, he defines perfection of the soul as knowledge of the essences of existing things. This lesson unpacks that clarification and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then read the literal translation. Notice the structure: the clarification begins with 'ليس مقصود المصنف' and then explains the actual intention.
The passage is a direct response to an implied objection.
2
Identify the two clarifications
First, the author clarifies what he does NOT intend: that imaginary things have no external existence, or that knowledge of them fails to correspond to reality. Second, he states what he DOES intend: that such knowledge, though certain, is not the perfection of the soul.
This distinction is key to avoiding misinterpretation.
3
Understand the definition of perfection
Perfection of the soul is defined as knowledge of the essences of existing things (أعيان الموجودات) to the extent of human capacity. This is why knowledge of imaginary things, even if certain, does not qualify as perfect.
4
Relate the example of astronomy
The passage mentions the proofs of astronomy (علم الهيئة) as an example of knowledge that does contribute to perfection, because it deals with actual celestial bodies. Contrast this with imaginary things.
This example reinforces the definition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( فإن قيل إلخ ) يعني ليس مقصود المصنف أنه ليس لها وجود في الخارج فلا يكون العلم بأحوالها مطابقاً للواقع، حتى يرد ما ذكر بل مقصوده أنه لا كمال للنفس في علمها، وإن كان يقيناً لأن الكمال معرفة أعيان الموجودات على ما هي عليه بقدر الطاقة. قوله : (ببراهين علم الهيئة فإنه يعلم بها أحوال الأجرام العلوية .
His statement: 'For if it is said, etc.' means it is not the intention of the author that they have no existence in the external world so that knowledge of their states would not correspond to reality, such that what was mentioned would be objected. Rather, his intention is that there is no perfection for the soul in knowing them, even if it is certain, because perfection is the knowledge of the essences of existing things as they are to the extent of capacity. His statement: 'By the proofs of astronomy, for through it the states of celestial bodies are known.'
Source pages p. 165

Commentary
This passage is a clarification of a previous objection. It explains that when the author says 'there is no perfection in knowing the states of imaginary things,' he does not mean that these imaginary things have no external existence, nor that knowledge of them fails to correspond to reality. Rather, even if such knowledge is certain, it is not perfect knowledge because true perfection of the soul consists in knowing actual existent beings (a‘yān al-mawjūdāt) as they are in themselves, within human capacity. The note further illustrates with astronomy: through its proofs, the states of celestial bodies are known, which is a higher form of knowledge.

أعيان الموجودات
علم الهيئة

Correction: The author explicitly says his intention is not to deny existence or correspondence, but to deny that such knowledge is the soul's perfection.

B) He means such knowledge is not certain
C) He means such knowledge, though certain, is not the perfection of the soul
D) He means such knowledge is useless

Key Takeaway
The author's denial of perfection in knowing imaginary things does not diminish its certainty or reality, but defines the soul's true perfection as the knowledge of existent beings.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You often pursue knowledge that feels certain, but does it actually correspond to real existent things? What imaginary pursuits distract you from knowing Allah and His creation as they truly are?
?

?
If perfection is not in certainty alone, how much of your learning is mere mental play? What will you change today to align your knowledge with the reality of what exists?

Certainty of Mathematical Judgments About Imaginary Objects
The author defends the certainty of mathematical judgments about imaginary objects.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why mathematical judgments about imaginary objects like lines and points are considered certain and how they can correspond to reality.

Core Idea
Imaginary objects can yield certain judgments by corresponding to real entities.

This lesson addresses a common objection: how can we be certain about geometry and arithmetic if their objects are imaginary? The author argues that even though lines and points are mental constructs, their judgments are certain because they can correspond to real things and be used to infer truths about the physical world.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on mathematical certainty
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Note that the author begins by stating the claim of engineers about certainty, then responds to objections.
Pay attention to the example of numbers composed of units.
2
Identify the objection and the author's reply
The objection is that knowing imaginary things is imperfect. The author replies that imaginary things can be accidental to real entities and thus judgments about them match reality.
Also note the inference from imaginary to real things.
3
Reflect on the significance
Consider how this argument supports the reliability of mathematics and its application to the physical world, as in astronomy.
This connects to the broader kalam methodology of using rational judgments.

ادعوا أنَّ بين الشكل واللون وحدة حقيقة منعناها، وإن اكتفوا بالوحدة الاعتبارية جاز اعتبارها في كل أمرين يجتمعان، وقد يقال : قد اعتبر الوحدة بينهما في متعارف الناس حيث عبر عنهما بالخلقة ووصف الشخص بحسبهما بحسن الصورة وقبحها فلذلك عددناهما كيفية واحدة وأدرجناها فيما اندرج فيه جزؤها كما عرفت ولم نجد لها نظيراً في ذلك فاكتفينا بها . قوله : ( فإن قيل إلخ ) يعني ليس مقصود المصنف أنه ليس لها وجود في الهيئة فإنه يعلم بها أحوال الأجرام العلوية .
The Third Chapter - The Second Objective: The statement of the engineers concerning the straight line and the point: they claim certainty in it. It may be said: proofs have been established for their existence in their places, and even if it is conceded that they are imaginary things, that does not negate that their judgments are certain. Do you not see that the number composed of units which are mental entities has true judgments without doubt? Whoever denies that they are certain has committed an obstinate denial. The same holds for geometrical investigations; one who practices them knows them. If it is said: there is no perfection in knowing the states of imaginary things, we say: indeed, imaginary things may in fact be accidental to existing entities, so those entities acquire due to that judgments that correspond to reality. And one may infer from the judgments of imaginary things to the states of real things. None of this is hidden from one who has awareness of the proofs of astronomy from arithmetic and geometry. (Note) Regarding what may be objected to making al-khilqa (complexion) among the qualities specific to quantities, namely: what is considered from the ten categories is only what falls under one of them only. And if composites were considered in the categories and their types, infinite categories would result, i.e., not limited but very many according to the pairings occurring between them two by two, three by three, up to ten; and also non-limited types would result from what they mentioned of their types according to the possible composition in what was composed, for example those types among the four divisions of quality some with others. On this basis, khilqa should not be counted among the qualities specific to quantities because it is composed of two different types. But they said: khilqa is considered and made to enter this type by virtue of a unity that occurs to the combination of shape and color. By virtue of that unity, it is described as beauty of form when color accompanies it and from them arises a unitary quality by virtue of which beauty and ugliness are valid, meaning that a thing is said to be beautiful in form or ugly in form. And they (beauty and ugliness) with respect to form are other than the beauty and ugliness that attach to shape alone or color alone. The author said: This is an unclear excuse, because if they claim that there is a real unity between shape and color, we deny it; and if they are satisfied with mental unity, it would be permissible to consider it in every two things that come together. And it may be said: unity between them has been considered in common usage, where they are expressed as khilqa, and a person is described by both as beautiful or ugly in form. Therefore we count them as one quality and include it in that in which its part is included, as you have learned, and we have found no parallel to it in that, so we restrict ourselves to it. His saying: (for if it is said, etc.) means that the author's intention is not that it has no existence in the external so that knowledge of its states would not correspond to reality, such that what was mentioned would be an objection; rather, his intention is that there is no perfection for the soul in knowing it, even if it is certain, because perfection is knowledge of the essences of existent things as they are to the extent of capability. His saying: (by proofs of astronomy) for by it the states of celestial bodies are known.
Source pages p. 165

Commentary
This passage is a defense of the certainty of mathematical judgments about straight lines and points, which are imaginary. The author begins by stating that proofs establish the existence of these objects in their proper places. Even if they are imaginary, their judgments are certain, like numbers composed of units. He then addresses the objection that knowing imaginary things is imperfect: imaginary things can correspond to real objects and be used to infer real things. He also includes a note on the category of 'khilqa' (complexion/form) being a composite of shape and color, and the debate about its unity. The author critiques the excuse that there is a unity between shape and color, arguing that if real unity is claimed, it is denied; if mental unity, it applies to any two things. The passage concludes with a clarification that the author's point is about perfection, not lack of existence.

اليقين
أمور وهمية
أمور اعتبارية

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think imaginary objects cannot yield certain judgments because they are not real.
Correction: Imaginary objects can correspond to real entities and yield judgments that match reality, as shown by arithmetic and geometry.

Checkpoint

B) Because they may correspond to real entities.

Imaginary objects like mathematical entities can ground certain knowledge because their judgments can correspond to real things and be used to infer truths about the physical world.

The Category of Khilqa: A Composite of Shape and Color
The author discusses why khilqa is considered a single quality despite being composite.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the reasoning behind classifying khilqa (complexion/form) as a single quality despite being composed of shape and color.

Core Idea
Khilqa is considered one quality based on common usage, not on real unity.

This note addresses an objection: how can khilqa (the combination of shape and color) be counted among the qualities specific to quantity when it seems composed of two different types? The author explains that scholars treated it as a single quality because in common language we refer to a person's khilqa as a unified whole, even though it comprises shape and color.

Guided Path
1
Read the note on khilqa
Read the Arabic passage and translation. Notice the objection that khilqa should not be in the category of qualities specific to quantities because it is composite.
The passage includes a critique by the author of the excuse given.
2
Understand the objection and the reply
The objection says khilqa is composite (shape+color) so it doesn't fit a single category. The reply is that it is considered one by virtue of a unity (mental or conventional) that arises when they combine.
The author finds the excuse unclear.
3
Reflect on the concept of mental unity
Consider how mental unity (al-wahda al-i'tibariyya) is used to treat composites as single entities for classification purposes.
The author notes that if mental unity is accepted, it could apply to any two things.

But they said: khilqa is considered and made to enter this type by virtue of a unity that occurs to the combination of shape and color. By virtue of that unity, it is described as beauty of form when color accompanies it and from them arises a unitary quality by virtue of which beauty and ugliness are valid, meaning that a thing is said to be beautiful in form or ugly in form. And they (beauty and ugliness) with respect to form are other than the beauty and ugliness that attach to shape alone or color alone. The author said: This is an unclear excuse, because if they claim that there is a real unity between shape and color, we deny it; and if they are satisfied with mental unity, it would be permissible to consider it in every two things that come together. And it may be said: unity between them has been considered in common usage, where they are expressed as khilqa, and a person is described by both as beautiful or ugly in form. Therefore we count them as one quality and include it in that in which its part is included, as you have learned, and we have found no parallel to it in that, so we restrict ourselves to it. His saying: (for if it is said, etc.) means that the author's intention is not that it has no existence in the external so that knowledge of its states would not correspond to reality, such that what was mentioned would be an objection; rather, his intention is that there is no perfection for the soul in knowing it, even if it is certain, because perfection is knowledge of the essences of existent things as they are to the extent of capability. His saying: (by proofs of astronomy) for by it the states of celestial bodies are known.
Source pages p. 165

Commentary
This passage is a defense of the certainty of mathematical judgments about straight lines and points, which are imaginary. The author begins by stating that proofs establish the existence of these objects in their proper places. Even if they are imaginary, their judgments are certain, like numbers composed of units. He then addresses the objection that knowing imaginary things is imperfect: imaginary things can correspond to real objects and be used to infer real things. He also includes a note on the category of 'khilqa' (complexion/form) being a composite of shape and color, and the debate about its unity. The author critiques the excuse that there is a unity between shape and color, arguing that if real unity is claimed, it is denied; if mental unity, it applies to any two things. The passage concludes with a clarification that the author's point is about perfection, not lack of existence.

الشكل
اللون
الوحدة الاعتبارية
الحسن
القبح

Misconception: One might think that because khilqa is composite, it cannot be a single quality in the categorical system.
Correction: Khilqa is considered a single quality based on common usage and the unitary quality that emerges from the combination of shape and color, despite being composite.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why do scholars consider khilqa as a single quality?
A) Because shape and color are really one thing.
B) Because common usage treats it as a unity, and a unitary quality emerges from their combination.
C) Because shape and color are both quantities.
D) Because the author insists it is simple.

Key Takeaway
Khilqa is classified as a single quality because in common usage the combination of shape and color forms a unitary concept of form, even though it is composite.

Will you examine that composite more closely today?

The author says knowledge of such classifications does not perfect the soul. When you seek knowledge, do you pursue it for intellectual satisfaction or for the perfection of your soul? What is one piece of knowledge you are currently seeking, and how does it actually change how you live?
?

Or do you insist on a 'real unity' that denies their complexity? Which approach brings you closer to Allah's mercy?

First Objection: The Problem of Infinite Regress
First objection: three regress arguments against relative accidents.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the existence of relative accidents leads to three distinct infinite regresses, each based on a different aspect of their nature.

Core Idea
Relative accidents create infinite chains of relations, making them rationally impossible.

The first objection against the existence of relative accidents is that they entail an infinite regress (tasalsul), which is impossible. The scholar presents three separate arguments for this regress, each arising from a different feature of relative accidents: their need for a substrate, the addition of existence to quiddity, and the relations of priority and posteriority among parts of time. We will examine each argument to see why relative accidents cannot exist as real entities.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then the translation. Let the author's argument unfold naturally.
The passage is dense; read slowly.
2
Examine the first argument (substrate)
Identify how the need for a substrate leads to an infinite chain of relations: each relative accident requires a substrate, which then has a relation to it, and that relation itself would be a relative accident, requiring another substrate.
The regress here is in the relations themselves, not in causes.
3
Examine the second argument (existence vs. quiddity)
Understand that existence is additional to quiddity; if a relative accident exists, its existence is additional, creating a relation between existence and quiddity, which again would be a relative accident, leading to regress.
4
Examine the third argument (priority and posteriority)
Consider the parts of time: if relations of priority and posteriority exist externally, then the priority of a prior time part itself has priority over the posteriority of a later time part, leading to an infinite regress of priorities.
This creates a third (and fourth) regress.
5
Reflect on the overall conclusion
Ask yourself: why are these regresses impossible? How do they collectively undermine the existence of relative accidents?
This connects to the learning objective.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه الأول: لو وجدت الأعراض النسبية لزم التسلسل في الأمور الموجودة. أما أولا: فلان هذه الأعراض لا بد لها من محل ولا شك أن محلها يتصف بها فله إليها نسبة بالمحلية والاتصاف، وهذه النسبة موجودة أيضا على ذلك التقدير ويعود الكلام فيها بأن يقال: هذه النسبة أيضا لها محل يتصف بها فله إليها نسبة ثالثة موجودة، وهكذا إلى ما لا نهاية له فهذا تسلسل. وأما ثانيا: فلان لوجودها الزائد على ماهيتها لما مر من أن وجود الممكنات زائد على ماهيتها إليها نسبة هي منها. وأما ثالثا: فلان لأجزاء الزمان بعضها إلى بعض نسبة بالتقدم والتأخر، فلو كانت النسب موجودة في الأعيان لكان التقدم والتأخر موجودين مع موصوفيهما وما مع المتقدم مقدم فيكون التقدم الموجود مع الزمان المتقدم متقدما على التأخر الموجود مع الزمان المتأخر، فللتقدم تقدم آخر وهكذا للتأخر تأخر آخر فهناك تسلسل ثالث بل رابع أيضا.
The first objection: If relative accidents existed, an infinite regress would follow in existing things. As for first, because these accidents necessarily have a substrate, and without doubt the substrate is characterized by them, so it has a relation to them of substratum and characterization, and this relation also exists on that assumption, and the discussion returns to it by saying: this relation also has a substrate characterized by it, so it has a third existing relation to it, and so on ad infinitum, so this is a regress. As for second, because their existence is additional to their quiddity, based on what has passed that the existence of possible things is additional to their quiddity, so they have a relation to it. As for third, because the parts of time have a relation to each other of priority and posteriority, so if relations existed externally, priority and posteriority would exist with their qualified things, and that which is with the prior is prior, so the priority existing with the prior time would be prior to the posteriority existing with the posterior time, so priority has another priority, and likewise posteriority has another posteriority, so there is a third regress, indeed a fourth.
Source pages p. 167

Commentary
This passage presents the first objection against the existence of relative accidents, which is that their existence would lead to an infinite regress (al-tasalsul). The objection provides three distinct arguments for this regress. The first argument begins from the fact that every accident needs a substrate (mahall). If a relative accident (like the relation between a substrate and its accident) exists, then the substrate has a relation of 'substratumness' (al-mahalliyya) to the accident. But that relation itself, if it is an existing accident, would also need a substrate, which would then have another relation, leading to an infinite chain of relations. The second argument points out that the existence of a possible thing is additional to its quiddity (dhat). If a relative accident exists, its existence would be additional to its quiddity, and then there would be a relation between its existence and its quiddity, leading again to a regress. The third argument involves the parts of time. The parts of time have relations of priority and posteriority (al-taqaddum wa al-ta'akhkhur). If these relations existed externally, then the priority that exists with the prior time would itself be prior to the posteriority with the posterior time, leading to a priority of priority, and so on, resulting in a third regress, and similarly a fourth regress for posteriority. Thus, the assumption of relative accidents leads to multiple infinite regresses, which are impossible.

Key Terms
الأعراض النسبية
التسلسل
المحل
الاتصاف
الوجود الزائد على الماهية
التقدم والتأخر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The infinite regress from relative accidents is the same as an infinite causal chain.
Correction: The regress here is not in causation but in the relations themselves; each new relation is a new relative accident, leading to an infinite series of distinct entities.

Checkpoint
How many distinct ways does the first objection argue that relative accidents lead to an infinite regress?
A) One
B) Two
C) Three
D) Four

Key Takeaway
The first objection demonstrates that the existence of relative accidents leads to an infinite regress in three distinct ways, showing the impossibility of such entities.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You constantly use relational concepts like 'father', 'teacher', 'friend'. This argument suggests that if these were real accidents, they'd lead to infinite chains. In light of this, do you still think your relationships are 'real' in the same sense as your own existence? How might this change your understanding of what it means to be related to someone?
?
You experience time as a series of 'befores' and 'afters'. The third regress argument shows that if priority and posteriority were real existing things, each would lead to another priority/posteriority ad infinitum. Does this make you question the objective reality of time as you perceive it? If time's relations are not real, what does that mean for your daily planning and sense of urgency?
?
The argument is a logical proof against the reality of relations. Yet you live your life as if relations matter. If these relations are mere mental constructs, as the argument suggests, then what is the foundation of your commitments to others? Are they based on illusion? This is a serious challenge: Will you accept this philosophical conclusion and change how you view your duties, or will you reject the premise? Commit to a decision right now.

Second Objection: The Implication for Relation (al-Idafa)
Second objection: relative categories would entail mutual simultaneity of prior and posterior.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the second objection argues that if relations existed externally, priority and posteriority would have to coexist, which is impossible.

Core Idea
Relations cannot exist externally because they would require contradictory simultaneity.

The second objection targets the concept of relational accidents (al-idafa). It argues that if relative categories existed, then priority and posteriority—which are types of relations—would demand that prior and posterior parts of time exist together. This contradiction shows that relations cannot be mind-independent. Let's read the passage to see the precise argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Note that this is a single continuous argument.
The passage is short but dense. Read carefully.
2
Understand the definition of al-idafa
Al-idafa is defined as a repeated relation (nisba mutakarrira) that requires the simultaneous existence of both relata. This is key to the argument.
The term 'repeated relation' refers to a relation that holds between two entities multiple times, but the essential point is coexistence.
3
Apply the argument to priority and posteriority
Priority and posteriority are divisions of al-idafa. Therefore, if idafa exists externally, the prior and posterior parts of time must exist together. But this is impossible, so the premise that relations exist externally must be false.
This shows the contradiction: prior and posterior are defined by temporal order, so they cannot be simultaneous.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه الثاني: لو وجدت النسب لوجدت الإضافة لأنها من النسب لكونها نسبة متكررة، وهي لا تتحقق إلا بوجود المنتسبين مجتمعين، ومن أقسام الإضافة التقدم والتأخر فيوجد المتقدم والمتأخر من أجزاء الزمان معا وإنه باطل قطعا.
The second objection: If relations existed, then relation (al-idafa) would exist, because it is among the relations, being a repeated relation. And it is not actualized except with the existence of the two relata together. And among the divisions of relation are priority and posteriority, so the prior and the posterior from the parts of time would exist together, which is absolutely false.
Source pages p. 168

Commentary
This passage presents the second objection against the external existence of relative categories. The argument is that if relative categories (al-nisab) existed, then relational accidents (al-idafa) would also exist, because they are a type of relation—specifically, a repeated relation (nisba mutakarrira). Relational accidents require both relata to exist simultaneously (mujtami'ayn). Among the divisions of relational accidents are priority (al-taqaddum) and posteriority (al-ta'akhkhur). If priority and posteriority are real relations, then when we consider the parts of time, the prior part and the posterior part would have to exist together simultaneously for the relation to hold. But it is impossible for the prior and posterior to exist together, since prior means it occurs before, not simultaneously. Hence, this proves that such relations cannot exist externally.

Key Terms
الإضافة
النسبة المتكررة
المنتسبين
التقدم والتأخر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The objection denies the concept of relation itself.
Correction: The objection only denies that relations exist as mind-independent entities; it does not deny the validity of relational concepts in the mind.

Checkpoint
Why does the second objection claim that if relations existed, priority and posteriority would have to exist together?
A) Because priority and posteriority are the same
B) Because relation requires both relata to exist at the same time
C) Because time is simultaneous
D) Because the argument is invalid

Key Takeaway
The second objection shows that if relations were external, contradictory coexistences like prior and posterior would occur, proving that relations exist only in the mind.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If relations like priority and posteriority exist only in the mind, then your sense of being 'ahead' or 'behind' others is a mental construct. In which area of your life are you most attached to the idea of being 'prior' or 'posterior' (e.g., career, spirituality, status), and how would that change if you truly accepted the mind-dependence of these comparisons?
?
This argument uses the impossibility of prior and posterior coexisting. When you reflect on your past sins or future hopes, do you sometimes treat them as present realities that weigh on you or drive you, rather than as mental concepts? What would it mean for your spiritual life to treat time's sequence as real in action but not as an external burden in your mind?
?
The objection denies external existence of relations, not their conceptual usefulness. Think of a relationship you have with someone: how much of your emotional state depends on assuming that bond is a 'real thing' outside your mind? If you knew it was only a mental construct, would you relate to that person differently starting today?

Philosophers' Proof: Necessary Knowledge of Relations
Philosophers' proof: necessary knowledge of relations implies external existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophers' argument that some relations are known necessarily, and this proves they exist in external reality.

Core Idea
Self-evident relations must be real, not just mental.

The philosophers (al‑ḥukamā') offer a straightforward proof for the existence of relative things (al‑umūr al‑nisabiyya). They point to everyday spatial relations—like the sky being above the earth—that we know with certainty. Because this knowledge is necessary and not derived from reasoning, the relations themselves must have a foundation outside the mind. Let's examine their argument closely.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Start by reading the Arabic text and its English translation. Notice the examples the philosophers give.
The phrase 'نعلمه ضرورة' (we know necessarily) is key.
2
Identify the examples given
The passage mentions two specific relations: heaven being above the earth, and the sun facing the earth's surface. These are meant to be obvious, everyday relations.
Think of other similar examples like 'the cup is on the table'.
3
Understand the logical inference
The philosophers argue that because we have necessary knowledge of these relations, they must be real features of the world—not mere mental constructs. If they were only in the mind, our knowledge of them would be subjective or inferred, not necessary.
This is a foundational move in the debate about the existence of relations.
4
Reflect on the significance
Consider why this matters: if relations are real, then reality includes not just substances but also the connections between them. This has implications for how we understand causation, time, and other relational categories.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واحتج الحكماء على وجود الأمور النسبية بأن كون السماء فوق الأرض ومقابلة الشمس لوجه الأرض وأمثالهما من النسب مما نعلمه ضرورة.
And the philosophers argued for the existence of relative things by the fact that heaven being above the earth, and the sun facing the surface of the earth, and similar [instances] from relations, are things we know necessarily.
Source pages p. 168

Commentary
This passage presents the philosophers' (al-hukama') proof for the external existence of relative categories (al-umur al-nisabiyya). They appeal to everyday examples of relations that we know with certainty: for instance, the relation of 'heaven being above the earth' and 'the sun facing the surface of the earth.' These are not mere mental constructions; they are perceived facts. We have necessary knowledge (darura) of such relations. Since these relations are known necessarily and are not purely subjective, they must have a foundation in external reality, implying that relative categories exist outside the mind.

Key Terms
الأمور النسبية
ضرورة
فوق
مقابلة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The philosophers claim that all relations are known necessarily.
Correction: The passage only says that some relations (like the examples) are known necessarily. Other relations may be inferred or known through reasoning.

Checkpoint
What kind of knowledge does the philosophers' proof appeal to for the existence of relative categories?
A) Inferred knowledge
B) Necessary knowledge
C) Revealed knowledge
D) Speculative knowledge

Key Takeaway
The philosophers prove the external existence of relative categories by pointing to our necessary knowledge of everyday relations like spatial positions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Do you ever assume that your perceptions of relationships (like 'above', 'before', 'because') are just mental constructs, not reflecting real connections? The philosophers argue that some relations are so self-evident they cannot be mere illusions. When have you dismissed a real relationship in your life—like your dependence on Allah—as a mere psychological feeling? Will you now acknowledge it as a necessary truth you cannot deny?
?
You know necessarily that the sky is above you. But do you know with the same certainty that your Lord is above you in rank and authority? If not, why does this relation seem less real to you than a physical location?
?
The philosophers used everyday examples to prove abstract realities. What everyday sign in your life—a relationship, a dependency, a purpose—do you treat as merely mental when it actually points to a reality you must answer to? Name one and commit to treating it as real starting today.

Refutation of the Regress Argument for al-Ayn
Al-Jurjani presents an objection and answer regarding al-ayn regress.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the mutakallimun defend the category of al-ayn against the charge of infinite regress by distinguishing original from dependent al-ayn and restricting it to substances.

Core Idea
The regress of al-ayn is blocked by limiting it to substances and distinguishing original from dependent.

In this passage, al-Jurjani addresses a key objection against the category of al-ayn (location): that its existence leads to an infinite regress. He presents the objection in two forms and then provides a refutation based on the mutakallimun's framework. By the end of this lesson, you will grasp the conceptual distinctions that prevent the regress and understand the deeper discussion about accident subsistence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the objection
Read the Arabic and translation carefully. Notice the objection is presented in two ways: first, that al-ayn would require a locus, leading to regress; second, that if it exists by dependence, it would have another ayn.
The objection is directed at the first proof of the original text.
2
Understand the answer and the counter-objection
The answer states that al-ayn is only for spatial substances, so it does not require another ayn. The counter-objection says that dependent al-ayn is established for accidents according to the mutakallimun.
This is where the discussion becomes nuanced.
3
Grasp the final resolution
Al-Jurjani points out that the mutakallimun only consider original al-ayn as existent, not absolute al-ayn. Also, even if the two ayns agree in essence, they may differ in existence. Hence, no regress.
The passage ends with a call to contemplate: 'falyutamal'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( عن بعض تلك الأدلة وهو الوجه الأول باعتبار لزوم التسلسل الأول والثاني وتقرير النقض أنه لو وجد الأين لزم التسلسل، أما أولاً فلانه لا بد من محل يتصف به نسبة إليه بالمحلية ويعود الكلام، وأما ثانياً فلان لوجوده الزائد نسبة إليه فإن أجبتم بأنه لا وجود لجميع النسب، بل لوجود المقولات فقط. قوله : ( وقد يجاب عن بعض تلك الأدلة إلخ) وذلك البعض هو الوجه الأول وتقرير النقض أنه إن كان الأين موجوداً وجب أن يحصل في حيز، وإن كان بالتبع فللاين أين آخر والكلام في الثاني كالكلام في الأول فيلزم التسلسل، وقد يجاب بأنه لا يلزم من وجود الأين أن يكون له أين آخر، إذ الأين إنما هو للجواهر المتحيزة ورد بأن الثابت للجواهر هو الأين أصالة وأما الأين التبعي فيلزم ثبوته للأعراض عند المتكلمين، بناء على أن قيام العرض بالمحل عندهم بمعنى التبعية في التحيز، وفيه بحث لأنَّ الموجود من الأين عند المتكلمين هو الأين أصالة، أعني حصول الجوهر في الحيز لا مطلق الأين وإلا لزم قيام العرض بالعرض وهم لا يقولون به وتوافق الأينين في الماهية على تقدير تسلميه لا يوجب توافقهما في الوجود كما صرحوا به فحينئذ فلا تسلسل ولا نقض، فليتأمل.
His statement: (Concerning some of those proofs, which is the first aspect, with consideration of the necessity of the first and second regress; and the statement of the refutation is that if al-ayn existed, regress would follow, firstly because it must have a locus that is described by it in relation to it as a locus, and the speech returns; secondly because its additional existence has a relation to it. If you answer that there is no existence for all relations, only for the categories.) His statement: (And it is possible to answer concerning some of those proofs etc.) That some is the first aspect. The statement of the refutation is that if al-ayn exists, it must obtain in a location; and if it is by dependence, then al-ayn has another ayn, and the speech concerning the second is like the speech concerning the first, so regress follows. It is answered that it does not follow from the existence of al-ayn that it has another ayn, because al-ayn is only for spatial substances. It is objected that what is established for substances is al-ayn as original, while dependent al-ayn is established for accidents according to the mutakallimun, based on the fact that the subsistence of an accident in a locus according to them means dependence in spatial occupancy. There is discussion in this, because what exists of al-ayn according to the mutakallimun is al-ayn as original, i.e., the substance's obtaining in the location, not absolute al-ayn; otherwise, the subsistence of an accident by an accident would follow, which they do not accept. And the agreement of the two ayns in essence, if conceded, does not necessitate their agreement in existence, as they have stated. Thus, there is no regress and no refutation. So let it be considered.
Source pages p. 170

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and its answer concerning the claim that the category of al-ayn leads to an infinite regress (tasalsul). The objection is formulated in two ways. First, it is argued that if al-ayn exists, it must itself occupy a place, requiring a further al-ayn, and so on. Second, if al-ayn exists by dependence (taba'iyya), then it would have another al-ayn, leading to the same regress.

The answer given is that al-ayn is not such that it requires another place, because al-ayn is only for spatial substances (jawahir mutahayyiza). However, the objector replies that what is established for substances is original al-ayn (al-ayn asala), while dependent al-ayn is established for accidents (a'rad) according to the mutakallimun, because for them the subsistence of an accident in its locus means dependence in spatial occupancy (taba'iyya fi al-tahayyuz).

The author then points out a further discussion: the mutakallimun consider only original al-ayn as existent—i.e., the substance's obtaining in its location—not absolute al-ayn. If absolute al-ayn were admitted, that would imply the subsistence of an accident by an accident, which they deny. Moreover, even if one concedes that the two ayns (original and dependent) agree in essence (mahiyya), that does not entail that they agree in existence (wujud). Hence, the regress argument fails and the refutation is not valid. The passage concludes with a call for contemplation: 'falyutamal' (so let it be considered).

Key Terms
أين
تسلسل
تحيز
تبعية
أصالة
جوهر
عرض
نقض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the category of al-ayn necessarily leads to an infinite regress because any location would itself require a location.
Correction: The mutakallimun restrict al-ayn to spatial substances and distinguish original from dependent al-ayn. Only original al-ayn is considered existent for substances, and it does not require another ayn. Dependent al-ayn for accidents is not considered a separate existence, thus blocking the regress.

Checkpoint
According to the mutakallimun in this passage, why does the existence of al-ayn not necessarily lead to an infinite regress?
A) Because al-ayn is only for substances, and its existence does not require another ayn.
B) Because al-ayn is a non-existent relation.
C) Because al-ayn is the same as the substance itself.
D) Because al-ayn is a quality and qualities do not require a place.

Key Takeaway
The mutakallimun's distinction between original and dependent al-ayn, along with restricting al-ayn to substances, prevents the infinite regress argument from undermining the category of location.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The mutakallimun refused to accept that al-ayn could apply to accidents because it would lead to an accident subsisting in an accident. In your own faith, are there beliefs you hold that, if followed to their logical conclusion, would lead to absurdity? What distinctions must you make to preserve coherence?
?
This lesson distinguishes between original existence (asala) and dependent existence (taba'iyya). In your spiritual life, what is truly original and what is merely dependent? Do you sometimes treat dependent matters as if they were original, leading to confusion or misguidance?
?
The argument against regress was resolved by defining terms precisely. Are there areas in your relationship with Allah where imprecise definitions have led you into confusion or error? How can you bring clarity to your understanding of tawheed and divine attributes?
