المرصد الرابع - المقصد الخامس : اتفاق القائلين على وجود جوهر فرد ولم يجوز ملاقاة الجوهر الفرد لأكثر من جوهر واحد ( حذراً من لزوم تجزئه وهو مكابرة وإنكار (للمحسوس) فإنَّ الحس يشهد بالتلاقي بين الجواهر من جميع الجهات (و) هو (مانع من تأليف الأجسام من الجواهر الفردة، فإنه إذا لم يمكن التلاقي من جميع الجوانب كيف يتحصل منها الجسم الطويل العريض العميق، بل لا يكون هناك إلا جواهر مبثوثة غير متلاقية ولا ممكنة التلاقي ( واتفقوا ) أيضاً (على) المجاورة والتأليف بين ذلك الجوهر والجواهر المحيطة به، ثم اختلفوا فقال الشيخ الأشعري : ( والمعتزلة المجاورة ) أي الاجتماع الذي هو كون الجوهرين بحيث لا قوله : ( حذراً من لزوم إلخ) فإنه إذ لاقى بجوهر واحد كان هناك ملاقاة واحدة قائمة بذلك الجوهر لا ببعضه فلا يلزم انقسامه والتداخل لعدم الاتحاد في الحيز بخلاف ما إذا لاقى بجوهرين فإن ملاقاته لآخر فيتعدد محل الملاقاتين فيلزم انقسام الجوهر لكن للجمهور أن يمنعوا استلزام تعدد الملاقاة بتعدد المحل لم لا يجوز أن تكون الملاقاة متعددة بحسب ما يلاقي له قائمة بنفس الجوهر من غير تعدد فيه كمحاذاة نقطة المركز للنقاط المفروضة في المحيط فإنها متعددة تلك النقاط بالمركز من غير لزوم انقسام المركز والقول بأن الملاقاة إن كان بالبعض يلزم الإنقسام وإن كان بالكل يلزم التداخل حكم وهمي ناشيء من قياس ملاقاة من غير المنقسم على ملاقاة المنقسم فتدبر فإنه دقيق. بحسب قوله : ( وإنكار) إشارة إلى أن تعدية المكابرة باللام بتضمين معنى الإنكار . قوله : ( فإن الحس إلخ) أي العقل بعد ثبوت الجوهر الفرد بمعونة الحس بالتلاقي وإنما قلنا : إنّ الحاكم هو العقل بناء على أن التلاقي ليس من المحسوسات بالذات . قوله : (وهو) مانع أي عدم التلاقي المفهوم من قوله : منع ذلك مانع عن تأليف الأجسام. قوله : (كيف يتحصل إلخ) إن أراد عدم تحصل الطول والعرض والعمق في نفس الأمر فمسلم لكن على القول بالملاقاة أيضاً يلزم ذلك بوجود المفاصل بين الجواهر وإن كانت متلاقية، ولذا أنكر المتكلمون المقدار وإن أراد عدم التحصل في الحس فممنوع، فاته إذا كانت مبثوثة لا يحصل التأليف لأنه يقتضي استلزام حركة الأخر، ففيه أنه يجوز أن يكون ذلك لإرادة الفاعل المختار من غير ملاقاة بينهما . قوله : ( حذرا من لزوم تجزئه) وقد يقال : التجزو لازم على تقدير ملاقاة جوهر واحد لمثله إذ ملاقاة كل منهما للآخر ببعضه لا كله، لأنه إن انطبق أحدهما على الآخر بحيث اتحدا وضعاً لم يحصل منهما جسم ذو حجم إلا أن لزوم التجزؤ على الأول ظاهر فلذا منعه ذلك البعض والتزم الثاني . الجهات قوله : ( فإن الحس يشهد إلخ) أي الحدس الحسي بواسطة إحساس التلاقي بين الجواهر من الجهات يشهد بذلك إلا أنه أحس بملاقاة جوهر فرد لجواهر متعددة من جميع جميع وهذا ظاهر.
The Fourth Observatory - The Fifth Objective: Agreement of those who affirm the existence of the indivisible atom [that] the indivisible atom is not permitted to touch more than one atom, out of caution against necessitating its division, and it is obstinacy and denial of the sensible, for the sense testifies to contact between atoms from all directions, and it is a prevention of the composition of bodies from indivisible atoms, for if contact from all sides is not possible, how can a body with length, width, and depth be composed from them? Rather, there would only be scattered atoms not in contact and not possible of contact. And they also agreed on contiguity and composition between that atom and the atoms surrounding it. Then they disagreed, and al-Shaykh al-Ash'ari said: (and the Mu'tazila) contiguity, i.e., the conjunction which is the two atoms being such that... Commentary: (His saying: 'out of caution against necessitating etc.') For if it touched a single atom, there would be one contact subsisting in that atom, not in a part of it, so its division and interpenetration would not be necessitated due to the lack of union in location, unlike if it touched two atoms, then its contact with another multiplies the locus of the two contacts, necessitating division of the atom. But the majority can deny the implication of multiplicity of contact by multiplicity of locus; why is it not permissible for the contact to be multiple according to what is touched, subsisting in the atom itself without multiplicity in it, like the alignment of a center point with points assumed on the circumference, for those points are multiple with the center without necessitating division of the center? And the statement that contact, if by a part, necessitates division, and if by the whole, necessitates interpenetration, is an illusory judgment arising from analogizing contact of the indivisible to contact of the divisible, so reflect, for it is subtle. (His saying: 'and denial') is a reference to the fact that the transitive use of 'obstinacy' with the lām implies the meaning of denial. (His saying: 'for the sense etc.') i.e., reason after establishing the indivisible atom with the aid of sense regarding contact, and we only say that the judge is reason based on the fact that contact is not among the sensibles per se. (His saying: 'and it is a prevention') means the lack of contact understood from his statement: that prevents composition of bodies. (His saying: 'how can it be composed etc.') If he means the non-acquisition of length, width, and depth in actuality, then it is conceded, but on the statement of contact also that is necessitated by the existence of joints between atoms even if they are in contact, and for this reason the theologians denied magnitude. But if he means non-acquisition in sensation, then it is denied; it (the composition) would not occur if they were scattered because it requires necessitating the movement of the other. It can be replied that that can be due to the will of the Free Agent without contact between them. (His saying: 'out of caution against necessitating division') And it may be said: division is necessitated on the assumption of one atom touching another like it, since the contact of each of them with the other is by a part of it, not the whole, because if one coincides with the other such that they unite in position, no body with volume results from them, except that the necessity of division in the first case is apparent, so that (the first) prevented it and adhered to the second. (His saying: 'for the sense testifies etc.') i.e., sensory intuition by means of sensing contact between atoms from directions testifies to that, except that it (the sense) senses the contact of an indivisible atom with multiple atoms from all, all, and this is apparent.
Source pages p. 184

Commentary
This passage is an argument defending the impossibility of an indivisible atom touching more than one other atom, combined with objections and answers. The author begins by stating the claim: those who affirm indivisible atoms hold that an atom cannot touch more than one atom, to avoid its division. This claim is then attacked as obstinate and contradictory to sense experience, since sense perceives contact between atoms from all directions. The author responds that sense alone does not judge; reason does, after establishing the atom via sense. The main argument: if an atom touches more than one atom, the contacts would be in different loci, necessitating division of the atom. Against this, objectors propose that multiple contacts can subsist in a single indivisible atom, similar to a center point aligning with multiple points on a circumference without dividing. The author then refutes the objection that contact by a part implies division and contact by the whole implies interpenetration, calling it a false analogy from divisible to indivisible. Further objections address how a body can be composed from atoms that cannot contact from all sides; the reply distinguishes between actual contact and the will of the agent. Finally, a separate objection considers whether even one atom touching another implies division, since contact is by a part; the answer notes that if they coincide fully, no volume results, but if they only touch partially, division follows. The passage concludes by affirming that sense testifies to an atom touching multiple atoms from all sides.

Key Terms
جوهر فرد
ملاقاة
تجزئه
محاذاة نقطة المركز
التلاقي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That an atom must be divisible if it touches two other atoms because multiple contacts require multiple loci.
Correction: The center-point analogy shows that multiple contacts can inhere in a single indivisible substance without division, just as a center point aligns with many circumference points.

Checkpoint
What analogy does the author use to deny that multiple contacts require division of the atom?
A) The analogy of a line divided into segments.
B) The analogy of a center point aligning with multiple points on a circumference.
C) The analogy of two bodies interpenetrating.
D) The analogy of a single atom touching another atom.

Key Takeaway
The center-point analogy demonstrates that multiple contacts can exist in an indivisible atom without requiring its division.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The author accuses those who insisted on the impossibility of multiple contacts of 'obstinacy and denial of the sensible.' In what area of your own religious or intellectual life have you been stubbornly clinging to a position despite clear evidence? What would it take for you to reconsider?
?
The center-point analogy shows how multiplicity can exist without division. In what ways might you be falsely assuming that complexity in your life (e.g., multiple responsibilities, relationships) requires you to be divided or overwhelmed? How does this lesson challenge that assumption?
?
The author warns against 'analogizing contact of the indivisible to contact of the divisible.' Where in your understanding of Allah's attributes or the unseen do you make inappropriate comparisons based on your limited experience? Commit to one specific way you will correct this tendency.

Body Composition and Sense Perception in the Atom Debate
Al-Jurjani addresses objections about body composition and sense perception.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how theologians defend the indivisible atom against objections about body composition and the role of sense perception.

Core Idea
Reason judges contact; composition can be by divine will without physical contact.

This lesson continues the defense of the indivisible atom. It addresses the objection that bodies cannot be composed from atoms that cannot contact from all sides, and clarifies that sense perception only aids reason. The final affirmation is that sense indeed testifies to an atom touching multiple atoms.

Guided Path
1
Read the second passage
Read the Arabic and translation. Focus on the objections and replies. Notice the shift from the analogy to new concerns.
The passage clarifies misunderstandings about sense and reason.
2
Identify the composition objection
Find the claim that if atoms cannot contact from all sides, no body with three dimensions can form. Underline the reply about joints and the will of the Free Agent.
3
Examine the sense testimony
Note how the author concludes that sense (with reason) does show an atom touching multiple atoms from all sides.
The phrase 'جميع جميع' may be a reduplication for emphasis.
4
Synthesize the lesson
Connect this lesson's points to the previous lesson. Understand the full defense of the indivisible atom.

يحصل التأليف لأنه يقتضي استلزام حركة الأخر، ففيه أنه يجوز أن يكون ذلك لإرادة الفاعل المختار من غير ملاقاة بينهما . قوله : ( حذرا من لزوم تجزئه) وقد يقال : التجزو لازم على تقدير ملاقاة جوهر واحد لمثله إذ ملاقاة كل منهما للآخر ببعضه لا كله، لأنه إن انطبق أحدهما على الآخر بحيث اتحدا وضعاً لم يحصل منهما جسم ذو حجم إلا أن لزوم التجزؤ على الأول ظاهر فلذا منعه ذلك البعض والتزم الثاني . الجهات قوله : ( فإن الحس يشهد إلخ) أي الحدس الحسي بواسطة إحساس التلاقي بين الجواهر من الجهات يشهد بذلك إلا أنه أحس بملاقاة جوهر فرد لجواهر متعددة من جميع جميع وهذا ظاهر.
The Fourth Observatory - The Fifth Objective: Agreement of those who affirm the existence of the indivisible atom [that] the indivisible atom is not permitted to touch more than one atom, out of caution against necessitating its division, and it is obstinacy and denial of the sensible, for the sense testifies to contact between atoms from all directions, and it is a prevention of the composition of bodies from indivisible atoms, for if contact from all sides is not possible, how can a body with length, width, and depth be composed from them? Rather, there would only be scattered atoms not in contact and not possible of contact. And they also agreed on contiguity and composition between that atom and the atoms surrounding it. Then they disagreed, and al-Shaykh al-Ash'ari said: (and the Mu'tazila) contiguity, i.e., the conjunction which is the two atoms being such that... Commentary: (His saying: 'out of caution against necessitating etc.') For if it touched a single atom, there would be one contact subsisting in that atom, not in a part of it, so its division and interpenetration would not be necessitated due to the lack of union in location, unlike if it touched two atoms, then its contact with another multiplies the locus of the two contacts, necessitating division of the atom. But the majority can deny the implication of multiplicity of contact by multiplicity of locus; why is it not permissible for the contact to be multiple according to what is touched, subsisting in the atom itself without multiplicity in it, like the alignment of a center point with points assumed on the circumference, for those points are multiple with the center without necessitating division of the center? And the statement that contact, if by a part, necessitates division, and if by the whole, necessitates interpenetration, is an illusory judgment arising from analogizing contact of the indivisible to contact of the divisible, so reflect, for it is subtle. (His saying: 'and denial') is a reference to the fact that the transitive use of 'obstinacy' with the lām implies the meaning of denial. (His saying: 'for the sense etc.') i.e., reason after establishing the indivisible atom with the aid of sense regarding contact, and we only say that the judge is reason based on the fact that contact is not among the sensibles per se. (His saying: 'and it is a prevention') means the lack of contact understood from his statement: that prevents composition of bodies. (His saying: 'how can it be composed etc.') If he means the non-acquisition of length, width, and depth in actuality, then it is conceded, but on the statement of contact also that is necessitated by the existence of joints between atoms even if they are in contact, and for this reason the theologians denied magnitude. But if he means non-acquisition in sensation, then it is denied; it (the composition) would not occur if they were scattered because it requires necessitating the movement of the other. It can be replied that that can be due to the will of the Free Agent without contact between them. (His saying: 'out of caution against necessitating division') And it may be said: division is necessitated on the assumption of one atom touching another like it, since the contact of each of them with the other is by a part of it, not the whole, because if one coincides with the other such that they unite in position, no body with volume results from them, except that the necessity of division in the first case is apparent, so that (the first) prevented it and adhered to the second. (His saying: 'for the sense testifies etc.') i.e., sensory intuition by means of sensing contact between atoms from directions testifies to that, except that it (the sense) senses the contact of an indivisible atom with multiple atoms from all, all, and this is apparent.
Source pages p. 184

Commentary
This passage is an argument defending the impossibility of an indivisible atom touching more than one other atom, combined with objections and answers. The author begins by stating the claim: those who affirm indivisible atoms hold that an atom cannot touch more than one atom, to avoid its division. This claim is then attacked as obstinate and contradictory to sense experience, since sense perceives contact between atoms from all directions. The author responds that sense alone does not judge; reason does, after establishing the atom via sense. The main argument: if an atom touches more than one atom, the contacts would be in different loci, necessitating division of the atom. Against this, objectors propose that multiple contacts can subsist in a single indivisible atom, similar to a center point aligning with multiple points on a circumference without dividing. The author then refutes the objection that contact by a part implies division and contact by the whole implies interpenetration, calling it a false analogy from divisible to indivisible. Further objections address how a body can be composed from atoms that cannot contact from all sides; the reply distinguishes between actual contact and the will of the agent. Finally, a separate objection considers whether even one atom touching another implies division, since contact is by a part; the answer notes that if they coincide fully, no volume results, but if they only touch partially, division follows. The passage concludes by affirming that sense testifies to an atom touching multiple atoms from all sides.

Key Terms
مكابرة
المجازة
التأليف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That sense perception alone can judge whether atoms touch multiple others or not.
Correction: The text clarifies that reason is the ultimate judge; sense only assists in establishing the existence of atoms and contact.

Checkpoint
How do theologians respond to the objection that composition of bodies requires contact from all sides?
A) They deny that bodies are composed of atoms.
B) They argue that composition can be due to the will of the Free Agent without physical contact.
C) They claim that atoms can contact from all sides after all.
D) They abandon the theory of indivisible atoms.

Key Takeaway
Composition of bodies from atoms can be achieved by divine will, not requiring physical contact from all sides, and reason, not raw sense, is the final arbiter.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you rely more on your senses or on reason when forming beliefs about the unseen? When has your sensory experience led you to doubt something your reason and revelation affirm—and what did you do about it? This week, identify one such area and consciously submit your senses to the authority of divine revelation and sound reasoning.
?
If divine will alone composes atoms without physical contact, what does that imply about your daily reliance on material cause-and-effect? Are you willing to see every outcome as directly coming from Allah, not from natural laws? Commit tonight to thanking Allah before investigating the means for every result that occurs.
?
The passage distinguishes between 'actual' and 'sensory' dimensions. Where in your faith do you confuse what is actually true with what you perceive? For example, when you feel distant from Allah, do you treat that feeling as reality, or do you anchor yourself in the actual truth of His closeness? Make a concrete resolution to counter one such feeling with a specific verse or rational truth today.

Real Dispositions vs. Mental Considerations
Al-Jurjani distinguishes real attributes from mental considerations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the kalam distinction between real attributes (like potentiality as a disposition) and mental considerations (like actuality), and why this distinction is foundational for the proof that follows.

Core Idea
Potentiality is a real disposition; actuality is a mental extraction.

Al-Jurjani begins by unpacking the phrase 'from every aspect'. He clarifies that he means aspects that are real attributes of a thing, like existence and potentiality, which is a real disposition. In contrast, actuality is merely a conceptual consideration extracted by the mind. This distinction is crucial because the entire proof hinges on what counts as a real attribute.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once to get the main point, then read the translation. Notice the contrast between 'استعداد' (disposition) and 'اعتبار محض' (pure consideration).
2
Identify the two types of attributes
The passage distinguishes two categories: real attributes (existence, potentiality) and mental considerations (actuality). Write them down.
Potentiality is treated as a real disposition, not a mental construct.
3
Reflect on why this matters
Ask yourself: If actuality were a real attribute, would the proof still work? The answer lies in the fact that only real attributes can be subject to logical contradiction.
This distinction is key to the argument about pure potentiality.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: (من كل وجه أي وجه متقرر في ذات الشيء متصف به في حد نفسه كالوجود وكونه بالقوة فإنه استعداد في ذات الشيء بخلاف كونه بالفعل فإنه اعتبار محض ينتزعه العقل. هذا خلف، ويلزم أيضاً أن يكون بالقوة في كونه بالقوة، فتكون القوة حاصلة وغير حاصلة بل يكون بالفعل من وجه ولو في كونه موجوداً ومتصفاً بالقوة لا أقل ذلك ويكون بالقوة من وجه آخر لأنا فرضناه كذلك فظهر أنَّ الموجود يستحيل أن يكون بالقوة من جميع الوجوه، فهو إما بالفعل من جميع الجهات كالعقول على رأيهم، أو بالفعل في بعضها وبالقوة في بعضها والقسم الأول يستحيل عليه الحركة لأنها طلب لشيء وتوجه إليه وذلك غير متصور فيه، لأنَّ جميع ما يمكن أن يكون له فهو حاصل بالفعل، فلا طلب فلا حركة بل لا تغير فيه ولا انتقال من حال إلى حال أصلاً، بخلاف القسم الثاني إذ يتصور فيه الحركة والانتقال الدفعي أيضاً و إذا عرفت ذلك فنقول : المتحرك أي الموصوف بالحركة له حركة بالفعل حال من ملاحظة اتصاف الشيء بصفة وبخلاف كونه قبل شيء آخر أو معه أو بعد، فإنها اعتبارات ينتزعها العقل من ملاحظة حصول الشيئين بالقياس إلى الزمان وأنه لا يلزم من كونه بالفعل من جميع الوجوه التسلسل واندفع الشكوك التي عرضت لبعض الناظرين. قوله : ( لأنَّ جميع ما يمكن إلخ) أي جميع ما يمكن أن يتصف به في حد ذاته ولا يكون اعتباراً محضياً. قوله : (أي الموصوف إلخ) أشار به إلى دفع مناقشة وهو أنه إن أريد المتحرك بالفعل فالحكم لغو، وإن أريد بالقوة فالحكم غير صحيح، ووجه الدفع أنَّ المراد الموصوف بالحركة من غير ملاحظة بالقوة والفعل، ولا شك أنَّ الموصوف بالحركة لا بد أن تكون حركة بالفعل إذ لا يمكن الاتصاف بالحركة المعدومة منهما أيضاً على رأي المتكلمين بل هم لا يقولون بهما كما سيجيء هذا، واعلم أن البحث في الاصطلاح إثبات المحمول للموضوع، فالتعريف ليس منها بل هو من المبادي التصورية إلا أن يعتبر الحكم الضمني . قوله : (فهو إما بالفعل من جيمع الجهات كالعقول على رأيهم اعترض عليه بأنه لو كان الشيء بالفعل من كل الوجوه لكان كونه بالفعل أيضاً بالفعل وهكذا إلى غير النهاية فيلزم التسلسل وأيضاً لا بد لكل شيء من اتصافه بصفات إضافية لم يكن متصفاً بها . قيل : أقلها الإضافات مع الحوادث فيكون الشيء بالفعل من كل الوجوه والجواب عن الأول : إنَّ التسلسل المذكور في الأمور الاعتبارية. وعن الثاني أن الكلام في الأمور الحقيقية كذا في حواشي حكمة العين للشارح وفيه بحث، أما أولاً : فلان كون الشيء بالفعل إن كان من الأمور الاعتبارية كان كونه بالقوة من الاعتباريات أيضاً، فحينئذ لا يتم قولهم : هناك الموجود يستحيل أن يكون بالقوة من كل وجه، وإلا لكان كونه بالقوة بالقوة إلخ، وكذا قول الشارح: هاهنا ويلزم أيضاً أن يكون بالقوة في كونه بالقوة، إذ لا يلزم من كونه بالقوة في الحقيقيات على ما هو المبحث باعترافهم كونه بالقوة فيما هو في الاعتباريات، اللهم إلا أن يقال: كونه بالقوة إلخ، عبارة عن الاستعداد
His saying: 'from every aspect' meaning an aspect established in the essence of the thing, qualified by it in itself, such as existence and being potential, for it is a disposition in the essence of the thing, contrary to its being actual, for that is a pure consideration extracted by the intellect. This is contradictory; it also follows that it would be potential in its being potential, so potentiality would be both present and absent. Rather, it is actual in some respect, even if only in its existence and being qualified by potentiality, at the very least; and it is potential in another respect because we have assumed it to be so. Thus it appears that the existent cannot be potential in all respects. So it is either actual in all respects, like the intellects according to them, or actual in some respects and potential in others. The first kind cannot have motion, because motion is a seeking and tending towards something, which is inconceivable in it, since everything that can be for it is already actual, so there is no seeking, hence no motion, no change, no transition from state to state at all. The second kind, on the other hand, admits of motion and also instantaneous transition. And if you know that, we say: 'the moving thing' i.e., what is described by motion, has motion actually, as a state from observing the qualification of a thing by a quality, as opposed to its being before something else, with it, or after it, for those are considerations extracted by the intellect from observing two things in relation to time. And there does not follow from its being actual in all respects an infinite regress, and the doubts that occurred to some investigators are rebutted. His saying: 'because everything possible etc.' meaning everything that can be qualified by it in itself and is not a pure consideration. His saying: 'i.e., what is described etc.' He indicated by this a refutation of an objection: if by 'moving' is meant actually moving, the judgment is trivial; if potential, the judgment is not correct. The refutation is that what is meant is what is described by motion without considering potentiality or actuality, and there is no doubt that what is described by motion must have motion actually, because it is impossible to be described by non-existent motion. Also, according to the theologians they do not say that [motion is two things] as will come. And know that the investigation in terminology is the affirmation of the predicate for the subject, so the definition is not part of it but rather from the conceptual principles unless a implicit judgment is considered. His saying: 'So it is either actual from all aspects like the intellects according to them.' An objection was raised: if something were actual in all respects, then its being actual would also be actual, and so on to infinity, leading to infinite regress. Also, every thing must be qualified by additional attributes that it was not previously qualified by. It was said: at least the relations with accidents — thus the thing is actual in all respects. The answer to the first: that infinite regress is in mental considerations. The answer to the second: the discussion is about real matters. Thus in the glosses on Hikmat al-‘Ayn by the commentator, and there is discussion in it. Firstly: if something's being actual is from mental matters, then its being potential would also be from mental matters. Then their statement that 'the existent cannot be potential in every respect' would not hold, otherwise its being potential would be potential etc., and similarly the commentator's statement here: 'and it also follows that it would be potential in its being potential' because it does not follow from its being potential in real matters, as is the subject of inquiry according to their admission, its being potential in what is mental. Unless it is said: its being potential etc. expresses disposition.
Source pages p. 198

Commentary
This passage is a proof followed by a refutation of objections. It begins by explaining the phrase 'from every aspect' as referring to real attributes like existence and potentiality (which is a real disposition) as opposed to actuality (which is a mental consideration). The commentator then presents a logical argument: if something were purely potential, it would lead to contradiction (potentiality would both exist and not exist). Therefore, every existent must have some actuality. It then divides existents into two types: fully actual (like intellects) and mixed (some actual, some potential). The fully actual cannot undergo motion because motion implies seeking what is not yet actual. The mixed can have motion. There follows a discussion of the statement 'the moving thing has motion actually', clarifying that it means the thing is described by motion, not considering potential or actual. Then three objections are addressed: (1) If something is actual in all respects, infinite regress would result; answer: that regress is in mental matters. (2) Every thing needs additional attributes; answer: the discussion is about real, not mental, matters. (3) A further objection about potentiality being real or mental; answer: potentiality in real things is a disposition, not mere mental consideration. The passage ends with a note about the definition being from conceptual principles.

الأمور الحقيقية

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Actuality is a real attribute like potentiality.
Correction: Actuality is a mental consideration extracted by the intellect, not a real disposition.

B) A pure mental consideration

Actuality is a mental extraction, not a real attribute; only potentiality and existence are real in this context.

When you think about your future 'actual' self (e.g., successful, knowledgeable), do you realize that it is just a mental extraction from your current potential? How does that awareness affect your responsibility to develop that potential today?
?

How can you realign your focus to the real dispositions within you rather than the mental constructs of what you have already become?

The Impossibility of Purely Potential Existence
Al-Jurjani proves that pure potentiality is impossible.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical proof that an existent cannot be purely potential, and why every existent must have some actuality.

Core Idea
Pure potentiality leads to contradiction: potentiality would be both present and absent.

Having established the distinction between real dispositions and mental considerations, al-Jurjani now presents the central proof. If something were potential in every respect, it would also be potential in its potentiality, leading to an infinite regress of potentialities and ultimately a contradiction. Therefore, every existent must have at least some actuality.

Guided Path
1
Follow the logical steps
Read the passage slowly and trace the argument: assume pure potentiality → deduce potentiality in potentiality → contradiction → conclusion that pure potentiality is impossible.
The contradiction arises because potentiality would both exist (as an attribute) and not exist (since nothing is actual).
2
Identify the conclusion
The passage concludes that every existent is either fully actual or mixed (some actual, some potential). Underline this division.
3
Ask why the proof works
Consider why the contradiction is unavoidable. The key is that potentiality is a real attribute, so it cannot be purely potential without self-reference.
This proof is foundational for the rest of the discussion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: (من كل وجه أي وجه متقرر في ذات الشيء متصف به في حد نفسه كالوجود وكونه بالقوة فإنه استعداد في ذات الشيء بخلاف كونه بالفعل فإنه اعتبار محض ينتزعه العقل. هذا خلف، ويلزم أيضاً أن يكون بالقوة في كونه بالقوة، فتكون القوة حاصلة وغير حاصلة بل يكون بالفعل من وجه ولو في كونه موجوداً ومتصفاً بالقوة لا أقل ذلك ويكون بالقوة من وجه آخر لأنا فرضناه كذلك فظهر أنَّ الموجود يستحيل أن يكون بالقوة من جميع فهو حاصل بالفعل، فلا طلب فلا حركة بل لا تغير فيه ولا انتقال من حال إلى حال أصلاً، بخلاف القسم الثاني إذ يتصور فيه الحركة والانتقال الدفعي أيضاً و إذا عرفت ذلك فنقول : المتحرك أي الموصوف بالحركة له الاتصاف بالحركة المعدومة منهما أيضاً على رأي المتكلمين بل هم لا يقولون بهما كما سيجيء هذا، واعلم أن البحث في الاصطلاح إثبات المحمول للموضوع، فالتعريف ليس منها بل هو من المبادي التصورية إلا أن يعتبر الحكم الضمني . قوله : (فهو إما بالفعل من جيمع الجهات كالعقول على رأيهم اعترض عليه بأنه لو كان الشيء بالفعل من كل الوجوه لكان كونه بالفعل أيضاً بالفعل وهكذا إلى غير النهاية فيلزم التسلسل وأيضاً لا بد لكل شيء من اتصافه بصفات إضافية لم يكن متصفاً بها . قيل : أقلها الإضافات مع الحوادث فيكون الشيء بالفعل من كل الوجوه والجواب عن الأول : إنَّ التسلسل المذكور في الأمور الاعتبارية. وعن الثاني أن الكلام في الأمور الحقيقية كذا في حواشي حكمة العين للشارح وفيه بحث، أما أولاً : فلان كون الشيء بالفعل إن كان من الأمور الاعتبارية كان كونه بالقوة من الاعتباريات أيضاً، فحينئذ لا يتم قولهم : هناك الموجود يستحيل أن يكون بالقوة من كل وجه، وإلا لكان كونه بالقوة بالقوة إلخ، وكذا قول الشارح: هاهنا ويلزم أيضاً أن يكون بالقوة في كونه بالقوة، إذ لا يلزم من كونه بالقوة في الحقيقيات على ما هو المبحث باعترافهم كونه بالقوة فيما هو في الاعتباريات، اللهم إلا أن يقال: كونه بالقوة إلخ، عبارة عن الاستعداد
His saying: 'from every aspect' meaning an aspect established in the essence of the thing, qualified by it in itself, such as existence and being potential, for it is a disposition in the essence of the thing, contrary to its being actual, for that is a pure consideration extracted by the intellect. This is contradictory; it also follows that it would be potential in its being potential, so potentiality would be both present and absent. Rather, it is actual in some respect, even if only in its existence and being qualified by potentiality, at the very least; and it is potential in another respect because we have assumed it to be so. Thus it appears that the existent cannot be potential in all respects. So it is either actual in all respects, like the intellects according to them, or actual in some respects and potential in others. The first kind cannot have motion, because motion is a seeking and tending towards something, which is inconceivable in it, since everything that can be for it is already actual, so there is no seeking, hence no motion, no change, no transition from state to state at all. The second kind, on the other hand, admits of motion and also instantaneous transition. And if you know that, we say: 'the moving thing' i.e., what is described by motion, has motion actually, as a state from observing the qualification of a thing by a quality, as opposed to its being before something else, with it, or after it, for those are considerations extracted by the intellect from observing two things in relation to time. And there does not follow from its being actual in all respects an infinite regress, and the doubts that occurred to some investigators are rebutted. His saying: 'because everything possible etc.' meaning everything that can be qualified by it in itself and is not a pure consideration. His saying: 'i.e., what is described etc.' He indicated by this a refutation of an objection: if by 'moving' is meant actually moving, the judgment is trivial; if potential, the judgment is not correct. The refutation is that what is meant is what is described by motion without considering potentiality or actuality, and there is no doubt that what is described by motion must have motion actually, because it is impossible to be described by non-existent motion. Also, according to the theologians they do not say that [motion is two things] as will come. And know that the investigation in terminology is the affirmation of the predicate for the subject, so the definition is not part of it but rather from the conceptual principles unless a implicit judgment is considered. His saying: 'So it is either actual from all aspects like the intellects according to them.' An objection was raised: if something were actual in all respects, then its being actual would also be actual, and so on to infinity, leading to infinite regress. Also, every thing must be qualified by additional attributes that it was not previously qualified by. It was said: at least the relations with accidents — thus the thing is actual in all respects. The answer to the first: that infinite regress is in mental considerations. The answer to the second: the discussion is about real matters. Thus in the glosses on Hikmat al-‘Ayn by the commentator, and there is discussion in it. Firstly: if something's being actual is from mental matters, then its being potential would also be from mental matters. Then their statement that 'the existent cannot be potential in every respect' would not hold, otherwise its being potential would be potential etc., and similarly the commentator's statement here: 'and it also follows that it would be potential in its being potential' because it does not follow from its being potential in real matters, as is the subject of inquiry according to their admission, its being potential in what is mental. Unless it is said: its being potential etc. expresses disposition.
Source pages p. 198

Commentary
This passage is a proof followed by a refutation of objections. It begins by explaining the phrase 'from every aspect' as referring to real attributes like existence and potentiality (which is a real disposition) as opposed to actuality (which is a mental consideration). The commentator then presents a logical argument: if something were purely potential, it would lead to contradiction (potentiality would both exist and not exist). Therefore, every existent must have some actuality. It then divides existents into two types: fully actual (like intellects) and mixed (some actual, some potential). The fully actual cannot undergo motion because motion implies seeking what is not yet actual. The mixed can have motion. There follows a discussion of the statement 'the moving thing has motion actually', clarifying that it means the thing is described by motion, not considering potential or actual. Then three objections are addressed: (1) If something is actual in all respects, infinite regress would result; answer: that regress is in mental matters. (2) Every thing needs additional attributes; answer: the discussion is about real, not mental, matters. (3) A further objection about potentiality being real or mental; answer: potentiality in real things is a disposition, not mere mental consideration. The passage ends with a note about the definition being from conceptual principles.

خلف
الاستعداد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Something can be purely potential (like a possibility without any actuality).
Correction: Al-Jurjani shows this leads to a logical contradiction, so it is impossible.

Checkpoint
What contradiction does al-Jurjani derive from assuming pure potentiality?
A) Potentiality would be infinite
B) Potentiality would be both present and absent
C) Actuality would be impossible
D) The thing would not exist

Key Takeaway
Pure potentiality is impossible because it leads to a logical contradiction; every existent must have some actuality.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
What area of your life have you kept as 'potential'—knowing you should act but never actualizing it? How does this proof expose that as a contradiction that you can no longer ignore?
?
If every existent must have at least some actuality, then your faith must be actualized in action. What is one specific way your iman remains merely potential, and what concrete step will you take today to make it actual?
?
The proof shows that pure potentiality is impossible. Are you living as though your repentance, your charity, or your prayers are 'potential'—something you intend but never do? How will you break that contradiction starting now?

Fully Actual Beings and the Impossibility of Motion
Al-Jurjani explains the consequences for motion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why a being that is fully actual cannot undergo motion, and how motion is possible for mixed beings.

Core Idea
Motion is seeking what one does not have; the fully actual has everything, so no motion.

After establishing that every existent is either fully actual or mixed, al-Jurjani explains the implications for motion. For something fully actual, all potentialities are realized, so there is nothing to seek. Hence motion, change, and transition are impossible. In contrast, mixed beings have both actual and potential aspects, allowing motion.

Guided Path
1
Read the classification
Remind yourself of the two categories: fully actual and mixed. Then read al-Jurjani's description of the first kind.
2
Understand why fully actual cannot move
Note that motion is defined as 'seeking and tending towards something'. Since all possibilities are actualized, there is no seeking, so motion is impossible.
This applies to intellects in the philosophers' view.
3
Contrast with mixed beings
Mixed beings have potentialities not yet actualized, so motion and transition are possible.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: (من كل وجه أي وجه متقرر في ذات الشيء متصف به في حد نفسه كالوجود وكونه بالقوة فإنه استعداد في ذات الشيء بخلاف كونه بالفعل فإنه اعتبار محض ينتزعه العقل. هذا خلف، ويلزم أيضاً أن يكون بالقوة في كونه بالقوة، فتكون القوة حاصلة وغير حاصلة بل يكون بالفعل من وجه ولو في كونه موجوداً ومتصفاً بالقوة لا أقل ذلك ويكون بالقوة من وجه آخر لأنا فرضناه كذلك فظهر أنَّ الموجود يستحيل أن يكون بالقوة من جميع الوجوه، فهو إما بالفعل من جميع الجهات كالعقول على رأيهم، أو بالفعل في بعضها وبالقوة في بعضها والقسم الأول يستحيل عليه الحركة لأنها طلب لشيء وتوجه إليه وذلك غير متصور فيه، لأنَّ جميع ما يمكن أن يكون له فهو حاصل بالفعل، فلا طلب فلا حركة بل لا تغير فيه ولا انتقال من حال إلى حال أصلاً، بخلاف القسم الثاني إذ يتصور فيه الحركة والانتقال الدفعي أيضاً و إذا عرفت ذلك فنقول : المتحرك أي الموصوف بالحركة له حركة بالفعل حال من ملاحظة اتصاف الشيء بصفة وبخلاف كونه قبل شيء آخر أو معه أو بعد، فإنها اعتبارات ينتزعها العقل من ملاحظة حصول الشيئين بالقياس إلى الزمان وأنه لا يلزم من كونه بالفعل من جميع الوجوه التسلسل واندفع الشكوك التي عرضت لبعض الناظرين. قوله : ( لأنَّ جميع ما يمكن إلخ) أي جميع ما يمكن أن يتصف به في حد ذاته ولا يكون اعتباراً محضياً. قوله : (أي الموصوف إلخ) أشار به إلى دفع مناقشة وهو أنه إن أريد المتحرك بالفعل فالحكم لغو، وإن أريد بالقوة فالحكم غير صحيح، ووجه الدفع أنَّ المراد الموصوف بالحركة من غير ملاحظة بالقوة والفعل، ولا شك أنَّ الموصوف بالحركة لا بد أن تكون حركة بالفعل إذ لا يمكن الاتصاف بالحركة المعدومة منهما أيضاً على رأي المتكلمين بل هم لا يقولون بهما كما سيجيء هذا، واعلم أن البحث في الاصطلاح إثبات المحمول للموضوع، فالتعريف ليس منها بل هو من المبادي التصورية إلا أن يعتبر الحكم الضمني . قوله : (فهو إما بالفعل من جيمع الجهات كالعقول على رأيهم اعترض عليه بأنه لو كان الشيء بالفعل من كل الوجوه لكان كونه بالفعل أيضاً بالفعل وهكذا إلى غير النهاية فيلزم التسلسل وأيضاً لا بد لكل شيء من اتصافه بصفات إضافية لم يكن متصفاً بها . قيل : أقلها الإضافات مع الحوادث فيكون الشيء بالفعل من كل الوجوه والجواب عن الأول : إنَّ التسلسل المذكور في الأمور الاعتبارية. وعن الثاني أن الكلام في الأمور الحقيقية كذا في حواشي حكمة العين للشارح وفيه بحث، أما أولاً : فلان كون الشيء بالفعل إن كان من الأمور الاعتبارية كان كونه بالقوة من الاعتباريات أيضاً، فحينئذ لا يتم قولهم : هناك الموجود يستحيل أن يكون بالقوة من كل وجه، وإلا لكان كونه بالقوة بالقوة إلخ، وكذا قول الشارح: هاهنا ويلزم أيضاً أن يكون بالقوة في كونه بالقوة، إذ لا يلزم من كونه بالقوة في الحقيقيات على ما هو المبحث باعترافهم كونه بالقوة فيما هو في الاعتباريات، اللهم إلا أن يقال: كونه بالقوة إلخ، عبارة عن الاستعداد

According to al-Jurjani, why can a fully actual being not have motion?
A) Because it is not in space

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You are constantly in motion—physically, emotionally, intellectually. But this lesson defines motion as 'seeking and tending'. What are you actually seeking right now? Could it be that your restlessness reveals a lack you haven't admitted?
?
If a fully actual being has no need to change, what does your constant dissatisfaction say about you? Are you willing to sit still and name what you already have—rather than grasping for what you think you lack?
?

Objections to Full Actuality and Their Solutions
Al-Jurjani refutes two objections about full actuality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two main objections against the concept of a fully actual being (infinite regress and need for additional attributes) and how al-Jurjani resolves them by distinguishing mental considerations from real matters.

Core Idea
Infinite regress in mental matters does not affect real attributes.

The notion of a being that is fully actual in all respects faces objections. The first is that if something is actual in all respects, then its being actual itself would also need to be actual, leading to infinite regress. The second objection is that every thing requires additional attributes it previously lacked. Al-Jurjani replies by distinguishing mental considerations from real matters, and by confining the discussion to real attributes.

Guided Path
1
Identify the first objection
Read the passage and find the objection about infinite regress: if something is actual in all respects, then its being actual is also actual, leading to regress.
2
Understand the answer to first objection
Al-Jurjani answers that such regress is only in mental considerations, not real attributes.
3
Identify the second objection
The second objection: every thing needs additional attributes it didn't have before, which seems to conflict with full actuality.
4
Understand the answer to second objection
The answer: the discussion is about real matters, not mental or relational attributes.

Then three objections are addressed: (1) If something is actual in all respects, infinite regress would result; answer: that regress is in mental matters. (2) Every thing needs additional attributes; answer: the discussion is about real, not mental, matters. (3) A further objection about potentiality being real or mental; answer: potentiality in real things is a disposition, not mere mental consideration. The passage ends with a note about the definition being from conceptual principles.

Key Terms
التسلسل
الأمور الاعتبارية
الأمور الحقيقية
الإضافات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
How does al-Jurjani resolve the objection of infinite regress against full actuality?
A) He denies the regress occurs
B) He says the regress is in mental considerations only
C) He says the regress is acceptable
D) He says the objection is invalid

Key Takeaway
The objections of infinite regress and additional attributes are resolved by maintaining the distinction between real attributes and mental considerations.

The Infinite Regress Objection and Its Initial Answer
Sanusi raises an objection and gives the initial answer based on conceptual vs real matters.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the philosophers answer the objection of infinite regress against a fully actual being by distinguishing between conceptual and real matters.

Core Idea
Infinite regress is only problematic in real matters, not conceptual ones.

The philosophers claim that separate intellects are fully actual. But an objection arises: if something is fully actual in every respect, then its actuality itself would be actual, leading to infinite regress. Also, everything must have accidental attributes. The answer cleverly distinguishes between conceptual and real matters. Let's see how this distinction works.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the Arabic passage that presents the objection: the claim that if a thing is fully actual, its being actual would lead to an infinite regress, and also that every thing needs accidental attributes.
Notice the two prongs of the objection.
2
Focus on the first answer
The answer to the first part says that the regress is in conceptual matters (al-umur al-i'tibariyya). Think about what that means.
Conceptual matters are mental considerations, not extra-mental realities.
3
Focus on the second answer
The answer to the second part states that the discussion is about real matters (al-umur al-haqiqiyya), implying that accidental attributes like relations are not part of the essence.
This preserves the idea that the being is fully actual in its essence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (فهو إما بالفعل من جميع الجهات كالعقول على رأيهم اعترض عليه بأنه لو كان الشيء بالفعل من كل الوجوه لكان كونه بالفعل أيضاً بالفعل وهكذا إلى غير النهاية فيلزم التسلسل وأيضاً لا بد لكل شيء من اتصافه بصفات إضافية لم يكن متصفاً بها . قيل : أقلها الإضافات مع الحوادث فيكون الشيء بالفعل من كل الوجوه والجواب عن الأول : إنَّ التسلسل المذكور في الأمور الاعتبارية. وعن الثاني أن الكلام في الأمور الحقيقية كذا في حواشي حكمة العين للشارح وفيه بحث، أما أولاً : فلان كون الشيء بالفعل إن كان من الأمور الاعتبارية كان كونه بالقوة من الاعتباريات أيضاً، فحينئذ لا يتم قولهم : هناك الموجود يستحيل أن يكون بالقوة من كل وجه، وإلا لكان كونه بالقوة بالقوة إلخ، وكذا قول الشارح: هاهنا ويلزم أيضاً أن يكون بالقوة في كونه بالقوة، إذ لا يلزم من كونه بالقوة في الحقيقيات على ما هو المبحث باعترافهم كونه بالقوة فيما هو في الاعتباريات، اللهم إلا أن يقال: كونه بالقوة إلخ، عبارة عن الاستعداد
His statement: ('So it is either fully actual from all directions, like the intellects according to their view.') An objection is raised against this: if a thing were fully actual in every respect, then its being actual would itself be actual, and so on ad infinitum, leading to infinite regress. Also, everything must be qualified by accidental attributes it did not possess. It is said: the least of them are the relations with contingent events, so the thing is fully actual in every respect. The answer to the first: that regress is in conceptual matters. And to the second: the discussion is about real matters. Thus in the glosses of Hikmat al-'Ayn by the commentator, and there is discussion therein: firstly, because if a thing's being actual is from conceptual matters, then its being potential would also be from conceptual matters; then their statement that 'an existent cannot be potential in every respect' would not hold, otherwise its being potential would be potential etc. Similarly the commentator's statement here: 'It is also necessary that it be potential with respect to its being potential,' because it does not follow from its being potential in real matters—which is the subject of inquiry according to their admission—that it is potential in conceptual matters. Unless it is said that 'its being potential etc.' is an expression of preparedness (al-isti'dad).
Source pages p. 199

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and its answer. The objection targets the claim that a thing can be fully actual: if it is fully actual, then its being actual would itself be actual, leading to infinite regress; also, every thing requires accidental attributes (like relations with contingent events). The answer distinguishes between conceptual (i'tibari) and real (haqiqi) matters: the regress is only in conceptual matters, so it does not affect real beings like the intellects. The second objection is answered by stating that the discussion is about real matters, not accidental attributes. The commentary then critiques this answer, noting that if 'being actual' is taken as conceptual, then 'being potential' would also be conceptual, undermining the earlier argument that an existent cannot be purely potential. However, it suggests that 'being potential' here refers to preparedness (isti'dad), which may be real.

Key Terms
التسلسل (al-tasalsul)
الأمور الاعتبارية (al-umur al-i'tibariyya)
الأمور الحقيقية (al-umur al-haqiqiyya)
صفات إضافية (sifat idafiyya)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that any infinite regress invalidates the concept of a fully actual being.
Correction: The answer shows that infinite regress is only problematic in real matters, not conceptual ones.

Checkpoint
According to the answer, why does the infinite regress not apply to the claim that something is fully actual?
A) Because it is in real matters.
B) Because it is in conceptual matters.
C) Because the thing is not fully actual.
D) Because the intellects are not subject to regress.

Key Takeaway
The philosophers defend fully actual beings by arguing that the infinite regress only occurs in conceptual matters, not real ones, and that accidental attributes are not part of the essence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When your mind spirals into endless 'what ifs' about your past or future, do you recognize those as conceptual regresses that have no real existence? How can you cut through them to focus on what is truly real before you right now?
?
In your understanding of Allah, do you ever slip into treating His attributes as if they were subject to the same conceptual regress as created things? What concrete step can you take today to ground your tawhid in the real, not the conceptual?
?
Your soul knows that only Allah is Fully Actual. Yet you chase shadows—conceptual achievements, imagined possibilities. When will you turn from the infinite regress of your own plans and stand in the light of His Reality? What would that look like tomorrow morning?

Critique of the Answer: Potentiality and Preparedness
Sanusi critiques the answer and refines the notion of potentiality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the subtle critique of the distinction between conceptual and real matters, and how the concept of 'isti'dad' (preparedness) is invoked to resolve it.

Core Idea
If being actual is conceptual, then being potential is also conceptual, undermining the initial argument.

The answer distinguishing conceptual and real matters seems neat, but a closer examination reveals a problem: if 'being actual' is treated as conceptual, then 'being potential' would also be conceptual. This would contradict the philosophers' earlier claim that an existent cannot be purely potential. The commentator then suggests that 'being potential' refers to preparedness (isti'dad), a real attribute. Let's unpack this nuanced discussion.

Guided Path
1
Read the critique passage
Read the Arabic passage that begins with 'كذا في حواشي حكمة العين للشارح وفيه بحث' and note the argument being made.
This is a critical analysis, not a direct objection.
2
Understand the first part of the critique
The critique argues that if 'being actual' is conceptual, then 'being potential' would also be conceptual, making their earlier statement invalid.
Think about why this is a problem.
3
Understand the commentary's resolution
The resolution suggests that 'being potential' here refers to 'isti'dad' (preparedness), which is a real attribute, not a conceptual one.
This preserves the argument.
4
Reflect on the importance
Consider how this shows the need for precise terminology in rational theology.
Even subtle distinctions matter.

Motion as First Perfection Leading to a Second Perfection
Explains motion as first perfection leading to a second perfection with example.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between first and second perfection in motion and how motion is defined as a first perfection that leads to a second perfection.

Core Idea
Motion is a first perfection that remains partly potential as it leads to a second perfection.

This passage from Sharh al-Mawaqif explains a key concept in kalam: motion as a first perfection that necessarily points to a second perfection. Through an example of a body moving to another place, the author distinguishes between the orientation (first perfection) and the actual attainment (second perfection). Understanding this distinction is crucial for grasping the nature of motion and its unique properties among accidents.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the literal translation to get the overall meaning.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Identify key definitions
Find how the text defines first perfection (كمال أول) and second perfection (كمال ثان). Notice that motion is called a first perfection relative to the possible it leads to.
The second perfection is the actual attainment at the endpoint.
3
Work through the example
The example of a body moving to another place illustrates the concepts: orientation (first perfection) and attainment (second perfection). See how orientation must be actual while attainment is potential.
4
Examine the two unique properties
The passage lists two ways motion differs from other perfections: it requires a desired possible not yet attained, and part of it remains potential. Identify and understand each.
These properties define motion as a special kind of perfection.
5
Reflect on the significance
Consider why this distinction matters for kalam. How does defining motion this way help in arguments about change and causality?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأنه ( أي ذلك الأمر الذي هو الحركة ( يؤدي) المتحرك ) إلى حصول ممكن آخر له وهو الحصول في المنتهى) مثلاً (فهذا ) الممكن الآخر ( كمال ثان ( إذا حصل بالفعل ) وذلك ) الأمر المؤدي إليه وهو الحركة الحاصلة ) كمال أول ) بالقياس إلى ذلك الممكن الذي يترتب عليه . أن يكون ثابتاً بالقوة ما دامت الحركة ثابتة بالفعل ( ثم إنه ) أي المتحرك ويجب ( مادام متحركاً) بالفعل ( فشيء منه ) أي من الكمال الأول الذي هو الحركة (بعد بالقوة فهو أي ذلك الكمال الأول إنما يثبت ) لما هو بالقوة ) من وجهين : : أحدهما ذلك الكمال الثاني المترقب حال الحركة، وثانيهما نفس هذا الكمال الأول وتوضيحه. قوله : ( وتوضيحه إلخ زاد في التوضيح تصوير الكمال الأول والثاني في صورة جزئية وبيان كونه تعريفاً للحركة بالخاصة وبيان احترازات القيد . أن الجسم إذا كان في مكان مثلاً، وأمكن حصوله في مكان آخر فله هناك إمكانان : إمكان الحصول في المكان الثاني وإمكان التوجه إليه وكل ما هو ممكن الحصول له، فإنه إذا حصل كان كمالاً له، فكل من التوجه إلى المكان الثاني والحصول فيه كمال، إلا أنَّ التوجه متقدم على الحصول لا محالة، فوجب أن يكون الحصول بالقوة ما دام التوجه بالفعل فالتوجه كمال أول للجسم الذي يجب أن يكون بالقوة في كماله الثاني الذي هو الحصول، ثم إِنَّ التوجه ما دام موجوداً فقد بقي منه شيء بالقوة، فالحركة تفارق سائر الكمالات بخاصيتين إحداهما أنها من حيث أنَّ التأدي إلى الغير والسلوك إليه تستلزم أن يكون هناك مطلوب ممكن الحصول غير حاصل معها بالفعل، ليكون التأدي تأدياً إليه وليس شيء من سائر الكمالات بهذه الصفة وثانيتهما أنها تقتضي أن يكون شيء منها بالقوة فإن المتحرك إنما حقيقتها هي التأدي.
And that it (i.e., that affair which is motion) leads the mover to the attainment of another possible for it, which is the attainment at the endpoint, for example. So this other possible is a second perfection when attained actually, and that affair leading to it, which is the motion that occurs, is a first perfection relative to that possible which is entailed by it. That it should be potential as long as motion is actual? Then, the mover, (and it must be) as long as it is actually moving, something of it (i.e., of the first perfection which is motion) remains potential. So that first perfection is only established for what is potential in two aspects: one of them is that second perfection expected during motion, and the second is this first perfection itself, and its clarification. He said: (and its clarification etc.) he added in the clarification a depiction of the first and second perfection in a particular example, and a statement that it is a definition of motion by a property, and a statement of the precautions of the restriction. That a body, if it is in a place, for instance, and it is possible for it to obtain in another place, then it has two possibilities there: the possibility of obtaining in the second place and the possibility of orientation towards it. And everything that is possible for it to obtain, if it obtains, it is a perfection for it. So both the orientation towards the second place and the obtaining in it are perfections, except that the orientation necessarily precedes the obtaining. So the obtaining must be potential as long as the orientation is actual. Thus the orientation is a first perfection for the body which must be potential in its second perfection which is the obtaining. Then, as long as the orientation exists, part of it remains potential. So motion differs from other perfections in two properties: one of them is that in as much as it is leading to another and proceeding towards it, it necessitates that there be a desired possible thing not yet attained with it actually, so that the leading is a leading to it, and none of the other perfections have this quality. And the second is that it requires that part of it be potential, for the mover's essence is leading.
Source pages p. 200

Commentary
This passage discusses the two perfections related to motion. It first states that motion leads the mover to another possible thing, namely the attainment of the endpoint. That other possible is a second perfection (كمال ثان) when actually attained, and motion itself is a first perfection (كمال أول) relative to that possible. The text then explains that as long as motion is actual, part of the first perfection remains potential. This is clarified from two perspectives: the second perfection expected during motion, and the first perfection itself. The commentator adds that the clarification depicts the first and second perfection with a particular example, and shows that this is a definition of motion by a property (بالخاصة). The example: a body in one place can potentially be in another; it has two possibilities: the attainment of the second place and the orientation towards it. Both are perfections, but orientation precedes attainment, so attainment is potential while orientation is actual. Thus orientation is a first perfection, and attainment is a second perfection. As long as orientation exists, part of it remains potential. The passage concludes that motion differs from other perfections in two properties: (1) as a leading to another, it requires a desired possible not yet attained; (2) it requires that part of it remain potential.

Key Terms
كمال أول
كمال ثان
توجه
حصول
إمكان
خاصة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Many think the first perfection is the motion itself and the second is the endpoint, but the text clarifies that orientation (the first perfection) remains partially potential while actual.
Correction: The first perfection (orientation) is not the endpoint; it is the motion toward it, and it retains potentiality as long as motion continues.

Checkpoint
In the example of a body moving to another place, what is the second perfection?
A) The movement itself
B) The orientation towards the other place
C) The actual attainment of the other place
D) The body's stability

Key Takeaway
Motion is a first perfection that leads to a second perfection, and it is unique in that it implies something potential and requires a desired possible not yet attained.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a goal you are actively pursuing right now. In what sense is your current effort a 'first perfection' — actual yet still partly potential? What aspect of it remains unrealized, and how does that tension motivate or discourage you?
?
The text says motion differs from other perfections because it requires a desired possible not yet attained. Is there a 'desired possible' in your faith or character that you have not yet moved toward? What keeps you from orienting yourself towards it?
?
During your pursuit of a goal, part of you remains potential. How do you remain patient with that incompleteness without losing the orientation? Are you more focused on the motion or the attainment? Which does your heart truly seek?

The Two Special Properties of Motion and Objections
Lists two properties unique to motion and addresses objections regarding dispositional potentiality and time.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two special properties of motion (essence as leading and requiring potentiality in part of its time) and how objections regarding dispositional potentiality and time are addressed.

Core Idea
Motion uniquely combines leading towards a goal and requiring potentiality, distinguishing it from other perfections.

This passage delves into the two unique characteristics that distinguish motion from other accidents. The commentator explains each property, addresses objections concerning dispositional potentiality and the nature of time, and clarifies why motion stands apart.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Read the Arabic passage and its english translation without analyzing. Let the author's own rhythm come through before interpreting.
Notice the dense, technical style of kalam reasoning.
2
Identify the first property
Find where the first property is stated: motion's essence is leading (التأدي) to a desired possible not yet attained.
The phrase 'أن حقيقتها هي التأدي' is key.
3
Understand the objection from dispositional potentiality
The objection claims that dispositional potentiality (الإمكان الاستعدادي) also involves something not attained. See how the response distinguishes it: only motion implies a desired goal.
The answer uses the phrase 'من حيث أن حقيقتها التأدي' to exclude the objection.
4
Examine the second property
The second property is that motion requires part of it to be potential (تقضي أن يكون شيء منها بالقوة). Follow the discussion about time and the 'connected now' (الآن الموصول).
Note the distinction between permanent and renewing motion.
5
Reflect on the key distinctions
Consider how motion's special properties set it apart from other perfections like time and dispositional potentiality.
The passage concludes that none of the other perfections have these qualities.

قوله : ( أن حقيقتها هي (التأدي) أي لازم لها ذلك كأنه نفس حقيقتها. قوله : ( تستلزم إلخ ) بخلاف الإمكان الاستعدادي فإنه لا يستلزم حصول ما يستعد لشيء له وإن كان يترتب عليه . قوله : (تقتضي أن يكون شيء منها أي بمحلها لقوة بخلاف الزمان فإنه وإن كان كمالاً للحركة لكونه مقداراً لها مقتضياً لأن يكون شيء منه بالقوة يستلزم أن يكون الزمان الذي هو مقدارها أن يكون شيء منه أيضاً بالقوة لكن ذلك الزمان ليس كمالاً لمحل الزمان الأول، بل للحركة التي هو مقدارها على اقتضاء الزمان الأول والثاني، بل المقتضى هي الحركة وما قيل : إِنَّ الحركة يتقدم الآن الموصول بلا شبهة وذلك الآن ظرف زمان حركة الجسم المتحرك مع أنه لا قوة بذلك الزمان بالنظر إلى أجزاء الحركة زمان تمام الحركة فمدفوع بأنك إن أردت أنَّ الجسم متحرك في ذلك بطريق الثبات باطل وإن أردت أنه متحرك بطريق التجدد وعدم الاستغراق فهو لا. قوله وليس شيء من سائر الكمالات بهذه الصفة فإن قلت: يرد عليه الإمكان الاستعدادي فإنه يستلزم أيضاً أن يكون المقبول غير حاصل معه بالفعل، فإنَّ التحقيق أنَّ الاستعداد سواء كان قريباً أو بعيداً يبطل مع الفعل قلت : الإمكان الاستعدادي وإن استلزم أن يكون هناك شيء غير حاصل لكن لا يستلزم أن يكون هناك مطلوب غير حاصل، إذ لا تعلق له بالطلب وأما استلزام الحركة للمطلوب الغير الحاصل فمن جهة أن حقيقتها التأدي إلى الغير وطلبه، وبالجملة الإمكان الاستعدادي يخرج بقوله : من حيث أن حقيقتها التأدي، فليتأمل. قوله : ( وثانيتهما أنها تقتضي إلخ) في ثبوت هذه الخصوصية للحركة وكونها خاصة لها على تقدير ثبوتها، لها بحث أما في الأول فلان الحركة تنعدم آن الوصول بلا شبهة، وذلك الآن طرف زمان الحركة، ففي زمان الحركة الجسم متحرك مع أنه لا قوة بعد ذلك الزمان بالنظر إلى بعض أجزاء الحركة لأنَّ الزمان تمام زمان الحركة، ويمكن أن يقال : هذه الخاصة للحركة بمعنى
He said: (that its essence is leading) i.e., it is necessary for it as if it is its very essence. He said: (it necessitates etc.) unlike dispositional potentiality, for it does not necessitate the attainment of that for which it is prepared, even if it is entailed by it. He said: (it requires that part of it be) i.e., in its subject because of potentiality, unlike time, for although it is a perfection for motion by being its measure, requiring that part of it be potential, it necessitates that time, which is its measure, also have part of it potential, but that time is not a perfection for the subject of the first time, but rather for motion which is its measure, upon the requirement of the first and second time; rather the requirer is motion. And what is said: that motion is preceded by the connected now without doubt, and that now is a time-container for the motion of the moving body, while there is no potentiality in that time with respect to the parts of motion at the time of the completion of motion, is refuted by the fact that if you mean that the body is moving in that time in a permanent way, it is false; and if you mean that it is moving in a way of renewal and non-saturation, then it is not. He said: and none of the other perfections have this quality. If you say: objection is raised against it by dispositional potentiality, for it also necessitates that the recipient is not actually attained with it, indeed the reality is that disposition, whether near or far, is nullified with the act. I say: dispositional potentiality, even if it necessitates that there is something not attained, does not necessitate that there is a desired object not attained, since it has no connection to desire. As for motion's necessitation of the non-attained desired object, it is because its essence is leading to another and seeking it. In short, dispositional potentiality is excluded by his statement: 'in as much as its essence is leading', so reflect. He said: (and the second is that it requires etc.) regarding the affirmation of this specificity for motion and its being specific to it on the assumption of its affirmation, there is a discussion. As for the first, because motion ceases at the instant of arrival without doubt, and that instant is an endpoint of the time of motion. So during the time of motion the body is moving, while there is no potentiality after that time with respect to some parts of motion because the time is the whole time of motion. And it can be said: this specificity of motion is in the sense of...

Commentary
This passage elaborates on the two special properties of motion and addresses objections. It begins with commentary on the first property: motion's essence is leading (التأدي), and this requires a desired possible not yet attained. An objection is raised: dispositional potentiality (الإمكان الاستعدادي) also implies something not yet attained. The answer distinguishes: dispositional potentiality does not imply a desired goal, while motion's essence is leading towards a goal, so it requires a desired object. The commentator notes that the phrase 'in as much as its essence is leading' excludes dispositional potentiality. For the second property (motion requires part of it to be potential), there is a discussion. An objection about time: at the instant of arrival, motion ceases, and there is no potentiality at that instant. The answer distinguishes between the time of motion and the endpoint; during motion, part is potential. The passage also discusses whether time itself has potentiality, concluding that time is a measure of motion and its potentiality derives from motion. The response to the objection about the 'connected now' is that motion is not permanent but renewing, so potentiality persists.

Correction: The passage clarifies that dispositional potentiality does not imply a desired goal, while motion's essence is leading to another and seeking it, so it requires a desired object not yet attained.

The passage distinguishes between motion's leading to a desired object and mere disposition. In your daily actions, are you moving toward a lofty goal (like seeking Allah's pleasure) or are you merely 'disposed' to good without the active striving? Which category better describes your worship—leading or potential? And how do you know?

Movement as Perfection: Two Potentialities and Two Types
Defines motion as perfection with two potentialities and two types.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophers' definition of motion as a perfection involving two types of potentiality, and the distinction between motion as traversal (القطع) and motion as mediation (التوسط).

Core Idea
Motion requires two potentialities, one of which differs between its two types.

This passage presents the philosophers' view on motion, defining it as a perfection for a body that has not yet reached its goal. Central to this definition are two potentialities: one relative to the motion itself and one relative to the intended end. Understanding how these potentialities apply differently to two types of motion—traversal and mediation—is key to grasping this metaphysical concept.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the definition of motion
Start by reading the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice that motion is defined as a state where the goal has not been reached, and the body possesses two potentialities.
The passage begins with 'المرصد الرابع...'.
2
Distinguish the two potentialities
Identify the two potentialities: one common to both types (relative to the goal) and one that differs (relative to the motion itself). The passage explains that the common potentiality is shared, while the other varies.
3
Compare the two types of motion
Focus on the difference between motion as traversal and motion as mediation. In traversal, some parts are potential and some actual simultaneously; in mediation, the motion is fully actual and potentiality only relates to the distance limits.
The passage uses 'القطع' and 'التوسط'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الرابع - المقصد الأول : رأي الحكماء في الجسم. يكون متحركاً إذا لم يصل إلى المقصد، فإنه إذا وصل إليه فقد انقطعت حركته، وما دام لم يصل فقد بقي من الحركة شيء بالقوة فهوية الحركة مستلزمة لأن يكون محلها حال اتصافه بها مشتملا على قوتين قوة بالقياس إليها وأخرى بالقياس إلى ما هو المقصود بها. أما القوة التي بالنسبة إلى المقصود فمشتركة بلا تفاوت بين الحركة بمعنى القطع والحركة بمعنى التوسط، فإن الجسم ما دام في المسافة لم يكن واصلا إلى المنتهى، وإذا وصل إليه لم يبق حركة أصلاً. وأما القوة الأخرى ففيها تفاوت بينهما، فإن الحركة بمعنى القطع حال اتصاف المتحرك بها يكون بعض أجزائها بالقوة وبعضها بالفعل فالقوة والفعل في ذات شيء واحد، والحركة بمعنى التوسط إذا حصلت كانت بالفعل ولم يكن هناك قوة متعلقة بذاتها بل بنسبتها إلى حدود المسافة، وتلك النسب خارجة عن ذاتها عارضة لها كما ستطلع عليه، فقد انكشف لك أن الحركة كمال بالمعنى المذكور للجسم الذي هو بالقوة في ذلك.
The fourth observation - first objective: The opinion of the philosophers regarding body. A body is in motion if it has not reached the goal; for if it has reached it, its motion has ceased, and as long as it has not reached, there remains some part of motion in potentiality. So the very essence of motion necessitates that its subject, while being described by it, comprises two potentialities: one with respect to it (the motion) and another with respect to that which is intended by it. As for the potentiality relative to the intended purpose, it is shared without distinction between motion in the sense of traversal (القطع) and motion in the sense of mediation (التوسط), for the body as long as it is in the distance has not reached the end, and when it reaches the end, no motion remains at all. As for the other potentiality, there is a difference between them: for motion in the sense of traversal, while the moving thing is described by it, some of its parts are in potentiality and some in actuality, so potentiality and actuality are in one and the same thing; whereas motion in the sense of mediation, when it occurs, is actual and there is no potentiality related to its essence but rather to its relation to the limits of the distance, and those relations are external to its essence and accidental to it, as you will see. Thus it has become clear to you that motion is a perfection in the mentioned sense for a body that is in potentiality in that respect.
Source pages p. 202

Commentary
This passage defines motion within the context of the philosophers' view on body. It begins by stating that motion exists when the body has not yet reached its goal. The essence of motion necessitates that its subject possesses two potentialities: one relative to the motion itself and one relative to the intended end. The potentiality for the end is common to both types of motion—motion as traversal (قطع) and motion as mediation (توسط)—since as long as the body is still en route, it has not reached the terminus. When it does, motion ceases entirely. However, the other potentiality differs: in motion as traversal, the moving thing has some parts of motion in potentiality and some in actuality at any given moment, so potentiality and actuality coexist in the same subject. In motion as mediation, the motion is fully actual and potentiality only pertains to the relations of the motion to the limits of the distance, which are accidental to the motion itself. The passage concludes that motion is a perfection for a body that is in potentiality in that specific respect.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Motion is a simple, homogeneous phenomenon.
Correction: Motion involves two types of potentiality and differs in structure between traversal and mediation.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the difference between motion as traversal (القطع) and motion as mediation (التوسط) regarding potentiality?
A) In traversal, potentiality and actuality coexist in the same thing; in mediation, potentiality is only in the relation to the distance.
B) In traversal, potentiality is only in the relation; in mediation, potentiality and actuality coexist.
C) Both types have the same potentiality.
D) Neither type has any potentiality.

Key Takeaway
Motion is a perfection that necessarily involves two potentialities, and the type of motion determines how potentiality and actuality are related.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have just learned that motion requires two potentialities: one for the motion itself and one for the goal. Reflect on your own spiritual journey: are you just going through the motions (tawassut) or are you actually making progress toward your goal (qat')? Which potentiality is lacking in your daily acts of worship?
?
The passage says that motion ceases when the goal is reached. What is your ultimate goal in life? If you are still moving, what is the goal you are striving toward? Are you honest about that goal, or have you settled for motion without destination?
?
Consider the distinction between potentiality in the essence (for traversal) and accidental potentiality (for mediation). In your own character, are you developing actual virtues or merely appearing virtuous? Do you allow Allah to transform you from potentiality to actuality, or do you remain in a comfortable state of potential, never reaching the goal?
