The Mental Existence of Motion as Qat'
Taftazani describes the mental construction of continuous motion via imagination.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Taftazani explains the mental existence of motion as qat' through imagination and the analogy of the falling drop and rotating flame.

Core Idea
Motion as continuous exists only in the mind through successive images retained in imagination.

Having established that motion as qat' has no external reality, Taftazani now explains its mental existence. He describes how the imagination combines two successive images—of the moving object at the start and at the endpoint—to produce the perception of a continuous whole. This is analogous to seeing a line from a falling drop or a circle from a rotating flame.

Guided Path
1
Read the mental existence explanation
Read the Arabic passage and translation. Notice that Taftazani begins with "n'am" (yes) to affirm mental existence.
2
Identify the role of imagination
Focus on how the imagination retains the image of the starting point while receiving the image of the endpoint. This combination creates the perception of a single extended thing.
The key is the overlap of images in the mind.
3
Understand the analogies
Consider the analogies of a falling drop (appears as a line) and a rotating flame (appears as a circle). These help illustrate how the mind constructs continuity from discrete inputs.
Note that these analogies involve the common sense, while motion as qat' involves only imagination.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قلت: إذا وصل إلى المنتهى فالحركة اتصفت حال الوصول بأنها وجدت في جميع ذلك الزمان، لا في شيء من أجزائه، قلت: حصول الشيء الواحد في نفسه على سبيل التدريج غير معقول، لأنَّ الحاصل في الجزء الأول من الزمان لا بد أن يكون مغايراً لما يحصل في الجزء الثاني لامتناع أن يكون الموجود عين المعدوم، فيكون هناك أشياء متغايرة متعاقبة لا يتصل بعضها ببعض اتصالاً حقيقياً لامتناع أن يتصل المعدوم بالموجود كذلك ويكون كل واحد منها حاصلاً دفعة لا تدريجاً، فلا وجود للحركة بمعنى القطع في الخارج (نعم) لها وجود في الذهن فإنه لما ارتسم (نسبته أي نسبة المتحرك ) إلى الجزء الثاني ) الذي أدركه ) في الخيال قبل أن تزول نسبته إلى ( الجزء (الأول) الذي ترك ( عنه ) أي عن الخيال ( يتخيل أمر ممتد ( يعني أن للمتحرك نسبة إلى المكان الذي تحركه ونسبة إلى المكان الذي أدركه ، فإذا ارتسمت في الخيال صورة كونه في المكان الأول، وقبل زوالها عن الخيال ارتسمت فيه صورة كونه في المكان الثاني فقد اجتمعت الصورتان في الخيال فيشعر الذهن بالصورتين معاً على أنهما شيء واحد ممتد (كما يحصل من القطرة النازلة والشعلة المدارة ( أمر ممتد ( في الحس المشترك فيرى ) لذلك ) خطأ أو دائرة ( كما مر في صدر الكتاب في مباحث أغلاط الحس، وإنما لم تكن الحركة بمعنى القطع مرئية مثلهما لأنَّ اجتماع الصور فيها إنما هو في الخيال لا في الحس المشترك )
If you say: When it reaches the endpoint, the motion is described at the moment of reaching as having existed in all of that time, not in any one of its parts. I say: The occurrence of a single thing in itself gradually is inconceivable, because what obtains in the first part of time must be different from what obtains in the second part, due to the impossibility of the existent being identical to the nonexistent. Thus there are multiple successive things, none of which truly connect to another due to the impossibility of the nonexistent connecting to the existent similarly, and each of them occurs instantaneously, not gradually. So motion in the sense of qat' (قطع) has no existence in the external world. (Yes) it has existence in the mind, for when his relation (that is, the relation of the moving thing) to the second part (which it has reached) is depicted in the imagination before its relation to the first part (which it has left) disappears from the imagination, an extended thing is imagined. That is, the moving thing has a relation to the place from which it moved and a relation to the place it reached. So when the image of its being in the first place is depicted in the imagination, and before that image disappears from the imagination, the image of its being in the second place is depicted, then the two images are combined in the imagination. The mind then perceives both images together as a single extended thing, just as an extended thing is produced from a falling drop and a rotating flame in the common sense, so one sees a line or a circle due to that, as mentioned earlier in the book in the section on errors of the senses. And motion in the sense of qat' is not visible like them only because the combination of images occurs in the imagination, not in the common sense.
Source pages p. 211

Commentary
This passage presents an objection-and-answer structure. The objector argues that if motion (as continuous traversal of distance) exists, it must be present at the moment of reaching the endpoint, but not in any part of the time before, which is contradictory. The answer (qultu) explains that the gradual existence of a single thing is impossible because each part of time yields a distinct state; thus motion as a single continuous thing cannot exist externally. However, it does have mental existence: the imagination retains the image of the starting point while receiving the image of the endpoint, resulting in the perception of an extended whole, analogous to seeing a line from a falling drop or a circle from a rotating flame. The passage ends by clarifying that such motion is not visible in the common sense because the combination happens only in the imagination.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The continuous motion we see is directly perceived by the senses.
Correction: It is actually a mental construction combining successive images, as explained by Taftazani.

Checkpoint
According to Taftazani, how does the mind perceive motion as qat'?
A) Through direct external sense perception.
B) By combining two images in the imagination.
C) By inferring from the object's acceleration.
D) Through divine revelation.

Key Takeaway
Motion as continuous exists only in the mind through the imagination's retention of successive images.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you observe motion around you, do you immediately assume it is continuous? How does knowing that this continuity is constructed in your mind challenge your confidence in your sensory perception? Will you question what you see more often?
?
Think of a time you were absolutely certain about something based on what you observed. How might this lesson's insight about mental construction reveal hidden assumptions in that certainty? What will you do to avoid being deceived by your own mind?
?
If continuous motion is only mental, what does that imply about your everyday experience of change and time? Are you prepared to accept that your perception of reality is not a direct copy of external truth? How will this affect your relationship with your own senses?

Motion in the Category of Position (al-Wad')
Definition of motion in position with example of celestial sphere.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of motion in the category of position and how it differs from local motion.

Core Idea
Positional motion changes orientation, not location.

This lesson introduces a crucial distinction in Islamic philosophy: motion that does not involve a change of place but a change in the arrangement of parts. The author uses the example of the celestial sphere rotating on its axis, which remains in the same place but its orientation relative to external things changes. This is the category of position (al-wad').

Guided Path
1
Read the definition of positional motion
Focus on how the author distinguishes it from local motion. Note the example of the sphere.
The Arabic term is al-wad'.
2
Understand the role of external reference
The passage says the proportion of parts to external things (containing or contained) changes. This is key to the definition.
3
Consider the historical note
The author corrects the impression that Ibn Sina discovered this motion; al-Farabi already mentioned it.
This shows the importance of ascription in philosophical tradition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الرابع - المقصد الثالث : وقع الحركة من المقولات وتغيراتها أخرى، وأما إن ذلك الانتقال بالتدريج، فكأنهم قنعوا فيه بما يحس به من انتقال الماء إلى السخونة يسيراً يسيراً (الثالثة : ( من تلك المقولات ( الوضع كحركة الفلك على نفسه فإنه لا يخرج بهذه الحركة ( عن مكان إلى مكان ( لتكون حركته أينية (و) ) لكن ( يتبدل بها وضعه ( لأنه يتغير بها نسبة أجزائه إلى أمور خارجة عنه، إما حاوية وإما محوية، وإذا تغيرت تلك النسبة تغيرت الهيئة الحاصلة بسببها، وهي الوضع وكلام ابن سينا يوهم أنه الذي وقف على الحركة الوضعية دون من قبله من الحكماء، وليس الأمر كذلك فإنَّ الفارابي قال في عيون المسائل : حركات الأفلاك دورية وضعية ( وفي حركة كل جزء منه أي من الفلك حركة مكانية (نظر) وتأمل ( فمنهم من ) قال : لا بالفعل) بل بالفرض ( فكيف يتحرك ) في الخارج ما لا وجود له فيه (بل ذلك ) أي تحرك جزء الفلك مع كونه مفروضاً (أمر موهوم ومنهم من قال : بتبادل النصفين الأعلى والأسفل وتغير نسبة الأجزاء إلى الأمور الخارجة ) أو المحوية (مع عدم حركتها غير معقول لأن مبدأ هذا التبادل والتغير قائم بتلك الأجزاء، لا بالأمور الخارجة عنها ( فعليك بالتأمل حتى يظهر لك ما هو الحق من هذين القولين فإنّ قلت : إذا كان كل واحد من أجزائه متحركاً حركة مكانية على القول الثاني لزم أن يكون الفلك أيضاً متحركاً حركة مكانية . قلت : ليس يلزم من تحرك الأجزاء عن أمكنتها وأينها أن يكون مجموعها كذلك، وأما الكواكب فيه متحركة حركة أينية على القول بأن المكان هو البعد وتطلق الاستدارة على حركتها كما تطلق على حركة من جزء عليه تسامحاً والأولى ما في عبارة الشفاء اختصاص أجزاء المفروض بجهة مفروضة، ولولا عبارة الشارح فيما سيأتي صريحه في إثبات الحيز للحيز لقلنا : إنّ لفظ الجوهر هاهنا تصحيف لفظ الجزء . قوله : ( إما حاوية وإما محوية ) على سبيل منع الخلو . قوله : (فعليك بالتأمل لا يخفى أنَّ الأجزاء الفرضية محققة [؟] الفرضية محققة الذوات في نفس الأمر، وإن كان وصف الجزئية بالفرض لعدم المفصل وتحقق ذواتها في نفس الأمر يكفي في اتصافها بالحركة فيها، ويؤيده أن الطبيعيين استدلوا على أنَّ في الفلك مبدأ ميل مستدير بأن أجزاءه الفرضية متساوية في الماهية فلا يكون اختصاص البعض بحيز أولى من العكس فيتبدل أحيازها وذلك بالحركة المستديرة كما سيأتي تفصيله . قوله : ( فإن قلت : إذا كان كل واحد من أجزائه متحركاً حركة مكانية على القول الثاني) لانزاع في أنَّ القطبين لا يتحركان أصلاً وعدم الاستثناء لشهرة أمرها . قوله : ( وأما الكواكب ) وكذا أفلاك التدويرات .
The Fourth Observatory - The Third Objective: The occurrence of motion from the categories and its changes. Another [category], and as for that transition gradually, it is as if they were content with what is sensed of the gradual heating of water. (The third:) from those categories (position, like the motion of the celestial sphere upon itself, for it does not, by this motion, depart from place to place so that its motion would be local; but) rather (its position changes thereby, because the proportion of its parts to external things, either containing or contained, changes thereby; and when that proportion changes, the configuration resulting from it changes, and that is position. The statement of Ibn Sina gives the impression that he is the one who understood positional motion, unlike the philosophers before him, but this is not the case, for al-Farabi said in 'Uyun al-Masa'il: 'The motions of the spheres are circular and positional.' (And regarding the motion of every part of it, i.e., of the sphere, there is a local motion [which is a matter of] consideration and reflection. Some of them said: not actually, but hypothetically. So how can something that has no existence in external reality move? Rather, that is an illusory matter. And some of them said: by the exchange of the upper and lower halves and the change of the proportion of the parts to external things, without motion, which is unintelligible because the principle of this exchange and change resides in those parts, not in external things. So you must reflect until the truth becomes clear to you from these two sayings. If you say: if each of its parts were moving locally according to the second saying, then the sphere would also be moving locally. I say: the movement of the parts from their places does not necessarily entail that the whole moves likewise. As for the stars within it, they move locally according to the view that place is dimension, and rotation is applied to their motion just as it is applied to the motion of a part of it, but loosely. The best is what is in the phrase of al-Shifa': the assignment of the hypothetical parts to a hypothetical direction. Were it not for the commentator's phrase to come, explicitly affirming the space for space, we would say that the word 'substance' here is a miswriting of the word 'part'. His statement: (either containing or contained) by way of preventing emptiness. His statement: (So you must reflect) – it is not hidden that the hypothetical parts have real essences in themselves, even if the description of being parts is hypothetical due to the lack of a divider; and their having real essences in themselves suffices for their being described as moving in it. And supporting this is that the natural philosophers argued that in the sphere there is a principle of circular inclination because its hypothetical parts are equal in essence, so the specification of some with a place is not more appropriate than the opposite, so their places change, and that is by circular motion, as will be detailed later. His statement: (If you say: if each of its parts were moving locally according to the second saying) – there is no dispute that the two poles do not move at all, and the absence of exception is due to the fame of the matter. His statement: (And as for the stars) – and likewise the epicycles.
Source pages p. 220

Commentary
This passage defines motion in the category of position (al-wad') and discusses the problem of whether the parts of the celestial sphere move. The author first states that the third category of motion is position, exemplified by the sphere's motion upon itself: it does not change place (so it is not local motion) but its position changes because the relative orientation of its parts to external things changes. The author then notes that Ibn Sina seemed to claim priority in discovering this motion, but al-Farabi already mentioned it. The main issue is whether the hypothetical parts of the sphere actually move locally. Two opinions are presented: (1) they do not move actually, only hypothetically, so the motion is illusory; (2) they move by exchanging upper and lower halves, but this is unintelligible because the principle of exchange is in the parts themselves. The author advises reflection. An objection is raised: if each part moves locally, the whole sphere would too. The answer: the movement of parts does not necessitate movement of the whole. The stars within the sphere do move locally. The passage also corrects a misreading in the text ('substance' for 'part'). The subsequent commentary confirms the reality of hypothetical parts and that their equal nature necessitates circular motion.

Key Terms
الوضع
الحركة الوضعية
الهيئة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Motion in position is the same as local motion because both involve movement.
Correction: Positional motion changes the orientation of parts relative to external references, not the place of the whole object. The sphere rotates but does not travel.

Checkpoint
What distinguishes motion in the category of position from local motion?
A) It involves a change of place
B) It involves a change of orientation relative to external things
C) It is the same as local motion
D) It only applies to the celestial sphere

Key Takeaway
Motion in position is a distinct category of motion that changes the configuration of parts relative to external references without changing location.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The celestial sphere changes its orientation without changing its place. Reflect on your own life: are you so focused on changing your circumstances (local motion) that you neglect the internal reorientation (positional motion) of your heart and intentions? What specific attitude or perspective do you need to adjust today, even if your situation remains the same?
?
The passage points out that Ibn Sina might have seemed innovative, but al-Farabi had already described positional motion. How often do you assume your own insights are original or unique, without acknowledging the contributions of those before you? In what area of your faith or knowledge do you need to practice more intellectual humility and seek out the wisdom of predecessors?
?
When the proportion of the sphere's parts changes, its configuration changes. Consider your relationships with others: how does the 'proportion' of your attention, care, or time toward different people change? Are there relationships where your 'position' needs realignment—not just a change in actions but a change in the very orientation of your heart toward them? What step will you take to adjust that orientation today?

Do the Parts of the Sphere Move Locally?
Presentation of two opinions and objection-answer regarding motion of sphere's parts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two opposing views on whether the hypothetical parts of the celestial sphere move locally, and the objection that if each part moves locally, the whole sphere would too.

Core Idea
The parts of the sphere do not necessarily move locally even if the whole rotates.

Having defined positional motion, the author delves into a long-standing debate: do the individual parts of the rotating celestial sphere undergo local motion? Two opinions are presented: one denies actual motion, the other claims an exchange of halves. The author then addresses an important objection that if each part moves locally, the whole sphere would also move locally. This lesson unpacks that debate.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two opinions
The first opinion says parts move only hypothetically, not actually. The second says they exchange upper and lower halves, which is unintelligible without actual motion.
Note the author's disapproval of the second opinion.
2
Understand the objection
If each part moves locally, the whole sphere would also move locally, contradicting the claim that the sphere's motion is positional.
This is a logical puzzle.
3
Analyze the answer
The author responds that the movement of parts from their places does not entail that the whole moves as a whole. The whole remains in place while parts change position.
This is a key distinction between part and whole.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

عبارة الشفاء اختصاص أجزاء المفروض بجهة مفروضة، ولولا عبارة الشارح فيما سيأتي صريحه في إثبات الحيز للحيز لقلنا : إنّ لفظ الجوهر هاهنا تصحيف لفظ الجزء . قوله : ( إما حاوية وإما محوية ) على سبيل منع الخلو . قوله : (فعليك بالتأمل لا يخفى أنَّ الأجزاء الفرضية محققة [؟] الفرضية محققة الذوات في نفس الأمر، وإن كان وصف الجزئية بالفرض لعدم المفصل وتحقق ذواتها في نفس الأمر يكفي في اتصافها بالحركة فيها، The Fourth Observatory - The Third Objective: The occurrence of motion from the categories and its changes. Another [category], and as for that transition gradually, it is as if they were content with what is sensed of the gradual heating of water. (The third:) from those categories (position, like the motion of the celestial sphere upon itself, for it does not, by this motion, depart from place to place so that its motion would be local; but) rather (its position changes thereby, because the proportion of its parts to external things, either containing or contained, changes thereby; and when that proportion changes, the configuration resulting from it changes, and that is position. The statement of Ibn Sina gives the impression that he is the one who understood positional motion, unlike the philosophers before him, but this is not the case, for al-Farabi said in 'Uyun al-Masa'il: 'The motions of the spheres are circular and positional.' (And regarding the motion of every part of it, i.e., of the sphere, there is a local motion [which is a matter of] consideration and reflection. Some of them said: not actually, but hypothetically. So how can something that has no existence in external reality move? Rather, that is an illusory matter. And some of them said: by the exchange of the upper and lower halves and the change of the proportion of the parts to external things, without motion, which is unintelligible because the principle of this exchange and change resides in those parts, not in external things. So you must reflect until the truth becomes clear to you from these two sayings. If you say: if each of its parts were moving locally according to the second saying, then the sphere would also be moving locally. I say: the movement of the parts from their places does not necessarily entail that the whole moves likewise. As for the stars within it, they move locally according to the view that place is dimension, and rotation is applied to their motion just as it is applied to the motion of a part of it, but loosely. The best is what is in the phrase of al-Shifa': the assignment of the hypothetical parts to a hypothetical direction. Were it not for the commentator's phrase to come, explicitly affirming the space for space, we would say that the word 'substance' here is a miswriting of the word 'part'. His statement: (either containing or contained) by way of preventing emptiness. His statement: (So you must reflect) – it is not hidden that the hypothetical parts have real essences in themselves, even if the description of being parts is hypothetical due to the lack of a divider; and their having real essences in themselves suffices for their being described as moving in it. And supporting this is that the natural philosophers argued that in the sphere there is a principle of circular inclination because its hypothetical parts are equal in essence, so the specification of some with a place is not more appropriate than the opposite, so their places change, and that is by circular motion, as will be detailed later. His statement: (If you say: if each of its parts were moving locally according to the second saying) – there is no dispute that the two poles do not move at all, and the absence of exception is due to the fame of the matter. His statement: (And as for the stars) – and likewise the epicycles.
Source pages p. 220

Commentary
This passage defines motion in the category of position (al-wad') and discusses the problem of whether the parts of the celestial sphere move. The author first states that the third category of motion is position, exemplified by the sphere's motion upon itself: it does not change place (so it is not local motion) but its position changes because the relative orientation of its parts to external things changes. The author then notes that Ibn Sina seemed to claim priority in discovering this motion, but al-Farabi already mentioned it. The main issue is whether the hypothetical parts of the sphere actually move locally. Two opinions are presented: (1) they do not move actually, only hypothetically, so the motion is illusory; (2) they move by exchanging upper and lower halves, but this is unintelligible because the principle of exchange is in the parts themselves. The author advises reflection. An objection is raised: if each part moves locally, the whole sphere would too. The answer: the movement of parts does not necessitate movement of the whole. The stars within the sphere do move locally. The passage also corrects a misreading in the text ('substance' for 'part'). The subsequent commentary confirms the reality of hypothetical parts and that their equal nature necessitates circular motion.

Misconception: If parts of a body move, the whole body must move as well.
Correction: The parts can move relative to each other while the whole remains stationary, as in rotation.

Key Takeaway
The parts of the rotating celestial sphere do not necessarily undergo local motion; the sphere's rotation is a positional motion of the whole, while the parts' motion is a matter of debate.

What will you do to make your internal principle stronger?

The Reality of Hypothetical Parts and the Proof of Circular Motion
Affirmation of real essences of hypothetical parts and argument for circular motion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that hypothetical parts of the sphere are real entities and how their equal nature necessitates circular motion.

Core Idea
Hypothetical parts are real, and their equality proves circular motion.

The author continues the discussion by affirming that the so-called 'hypothetical parts' of the celestial sphere actually have real essences, even if they are not physically separated. This has important implications: because all parts are equal in essence, none has a greater claim to a particular place, so they must continually exchange places, resulting in circular motion. This lesson also addresses the exception of the poles and the motion of stars and epicycles.

Guided Path
1
Understand the reality of hypothetical parts
The commentator argues that even though the parts are called 'hypothetical' due to lack of a divider, they have real essences in themselves, which is sufficient for motion.
This counters the first opinion that motion is illusory.
2
Follow the argument for circular motion
Natural philosophers deduced that there is a principle of circular inclination in the sphere because its hypothetical parts are equal in essence, so no part has an inherent place, hence they must move circularly to exchange places.
This is a key proof from early natural philosophy.
3
Note the exceptions
The poles do not move, and the stars and epicycles move locally, not positionally.
These nuances show the complexity of celestial motion.

Motion in Place: The General Problem and Continuous Solution
Definition of motion in 'ayn and resolution of the temporal atom problem.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how mutakallimun define motion in the category of place (al-ayn) and how they resolve the paradox of discrete places and moments using the concept of a continuous intermediate place.

Core Idea
Motion in place is explained by a continuous place, avoiding infinite successive moments.

This passage presents the definition of motion in the category of place (al-ayn), which is the primary sense of motion for the mutakallimun. It then raises a classic problem: if motion involves multiple places, it seems to require successive moments, which is invalid. The solution lies in positing a single continuous place that is divisible by hypothesis, much like a line is divisible into infinite points.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once and then the English translation. Get a feel for the argument before deep analysis.
2
Identify the definition of motion in 'ayn
The author defines motion in 'ayn as 'al-naqla' (translation). Note that this is the default meaning of motion for mutakallimun.
3
Understand the general problem (shubha)
The problem presents two possibilities: either the moving thing has one place (thus stationary) or multiple places (leading to either interruption or successive moments). Both are problematic.
This is a classic dilemma in kalam.
4
Grasp the solution: continuous 'ayn
The solution is that the moving thing has a single continuous place that is not fixed but varies in relation to the distance limits. It is like between start and end, and its 'places' are divisible by hypothesis.
Analogous to points on a line.
5
Consider the additional objection
The commentary adds that the multiple places cannot be infinite or finite, which also fails, reinforcing the solution.
This strengthens the argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الرابع - المقصد الثالث : وقع الحركة من المقولات وتغيراتها تلك يطوف حول شيء مع أنها حركة مكانية يتبدل بها أمكنته بلا شبهة . ( الرابعة : ) من تلك المقولات (الأين وهو) أي التحرك في الأين (النقلة التي يسميها المتكلم حركة ) فإن المتكلمين إذا أطلقوا الحركة أرادوا بها الحركة الأينية المسماة بالنقلة، وهي المتبادرة في استعمالات أهل اللغة أيضاً، وقد تطلق عندهم على الوضعية دون الكمية ،والكيفية، ثم إن في الحركة شبهة عامة وهي أن يقال : المتحرك في الأين إن كان له من مبدأ المسافة إلى منتهاها أين واحد فليس متحركاً في الأين، بل هو ساكن مستقر على أين واحد وإن كان له أيون متعددة فإما أن يستقر على واحد من الأيون في أكثر من آن واحد فقد انقطعت حركته وإما أن لا يستقر فلا يكون في كل أين إلا آن واحد ولا شك أن تلك الأيون الآنية متعاقبة متتالية، إذ لو كانت متفاصلة بزمان، ولم يوجد في ذلك الزمان شيء من تلك الأيون لزم انقطاع تلك الحركة الأينية، وإذا كانت تلك الأيون متعاقبة كانت الآنات متتالية وهو باطل عندهم، وهكذا يقال في الحركة الكمية والوضعية والكيفية، ولا مخلص عنها إلا بأن يقال للمتحرك في الأين من مبدأ المسافة إلى منتهاها أين واحد مستمر هو كونه متوسطاً بين المبدأ والمنتهى لكنه غير مستقر بل تختلف نسبته إلى حدود المسافة ويتعدد بحسب تعددها، وكما أن حدود المسافة تتعدد بالفرض كذلك تتعدد الأيون بحسب الفرض وكما أنه لا يمكن أن يفرض في المسافة حدان متلاقيان ليس بينهما مسافة أصلاً، كذلك لا يمكن أن يفرض في ذلك الأين المستمر أينان متصلان، بل كل أينين مفروضين في ذلك الأين المستمر يمكن أن يفرض بينهما أيون أخر، كما أن نقطتين مفروضتين على خط يمكن أن يفرض بينهما نقط أخرى فلا يلزم تتالي الآنات ولا انقطاع الحركة ولا كون المتحرك ساكناً، قوله : ( وإن كان له أيون متعددة فإما أن يستقر إلخ) وأيضاً تلك الأيون إما غير متناهية ويبطله الانحصار بين الحاصرين وإما متناهية ويبطله لزوم تناهي أجزاء المسافة مع أنه باطل عندهم.
The Fourth Observatory - The Third Objective: The occurrence of motion from the categories and its changes. Those revolve around something while they are a local motion by which their places change without doubt. (The fourth:) from those categories (place (al-ayn), i.e., movement in place, which is the translation that the mutakallim calls motion. For the mutakallimun, when they use the term motion absolutely, they mean local motion, called translation, and it is the primary usage in the language as well. It is also sometimes used for positional motion, but not for quantitative or qualitative motion. Then concerning motion there is a general problem: it is said that the moving thing in place, if it has a single place from the beginning of the distance to its end, then it is not moving in place, but rather stationary at one place; and if it has multiple places, then either it settles at one of the places for more than one moment, then its motion has stopped; or it does not settle, so it has only one moment in each place, and there is no doubt that those momentary places are consecutive and successive, for if they were separated by time and none of those places existed in that time, then that local motion would be interrupted. And if those places are consecutive, then the moments would be successive, which is invalid according to them. And the same is said about quantitative, positional, and qualitative motion. There is no escape from this except by saying that the moving thing in place, from the beginning of the distance to its end, has a single continuous place, namely its being intermediate between the beginning and the end, but it is not stationary; rather, its relation to the limits of the distance varies and becomes multiple according to their multiplicity. Just as the limits of the distance become multiple by hypothesis, so the places become multiple by hypothesis, and just as it is impossible to hypothesize two limits in the distance that meet without any distance between them, so it is impossible to hypothesize in that continuous place two contiguous places; rather, between any two hypothesized places in that continuous place, other places can be hypothesized, just as between two hypothesized points on a line, other points can be hypothesized. Thus, neither the succession of moments nor the interruption of motion nor the moving thing being stationary necessarily follows. His statement: (And if it has multiple places, then either it settles, etc.) – and also those places are either infinite, which is invalidated by being bounded between two bounds, or finite, which is invalidated by the necessity of the finitude of the parts of the distance, while that is invalid according to them.
Source pages p. 221

Commentary
This passage defines motion in the category of place (al-ayn) and presents a general problem (shubha) regarding motion in any category. The author first states that the fourth category is 'ayn, which is the translation (naqla) that mutakallimun call motion. They use 'motion' primarily for local motion. Then the problem is raised: if a moving thing has only one place from start to end, it is stationary; if it has multiple places, either it stays in one place for more than a moment (motion stops) or it stays only one moment per place, leading to successive moments – which is invalid. This applies to all categories. The solution is to posit a single continuous place (ayn mustamirr) for the moving thing, which is its being 'between' the start and end, but not fixed; its relation to the limits varies and is multiple by hypothesis. Just as a line has infinitely divisible points, the continuous place has infinitely divisible 'places' by hypothesis, avoiding both infinite succession of moments and disruption of motion. The commentary adds an additional objection: the multiple places are either infinite (invalid because bounded) or finite (invalid because distance parts would be finite, which is false). Thus the solution is upheld.

Key Terms
الأين
النقلة
الشبهة العامة
الأين المستمر
الآنات المتتالية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Motion in place requires distinct places and moments in succession.
Correction: The passage shows that motion is explained by a continuous place that is divisible by hypothesis, avoiding discrete moments and interruption.

Checkpoint
How does the passage resolve the general problem (shubha) of motion?
A) By positing that motion is an illusion
B) By asserting that the moving thing has a single continuous place that is divisible by hypothesis
C) By denying that motion occurs in place
D) By limiting motion to only one category

Key Takeaway
Motion in place is explained by a continuous intermediate place, avoiding discrete moments and interruptions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This argument shows that the mutakallimun defended motion against a logical puzzle. But when you look at your own spiritual growth—your movement toward God—do you see it as a series of discrete, disconnected 'good moments' or as a single continuous journey? Which view gives you more hope and perseverance? Commit to one view and act accordingly.
?
The solution to the dilemma is a 'continuous place' that is not stationary but always relating differently to the limits. In your daily worship, do you feel stuck in a fixed 'place'—repeating routines without movement—or are you constantly pushing toward new limits in your relationship with Allah? What is one relationship or habit you need to stop treating as static and start moving forward in right now?

If the mutakallimun rejected successive moments because they imply an impossible infinite chain, what does that say about how you view time? Do you treat each moment as isolated, or do you see your life as a continuous whole that Allah is holding together? How will you live today knowing that your now is connected to the past and future without gaps? Name one specific way you will make this connection real in your next action.

Objection to the Single Quality in Qualitative Motion
Objection to the claim that moving in quality has one quality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection against claiming that a moving thing in quality possesses a single quality, and why both interpretations (species and individual) are problematic.

Core Idea
A single quality in motion is either too vague or contradictory to sensory experience.

This passage presents a critical objection to the earlier claim that the 'ayn solution applies to qualitative motion just as it does to quantitative motion. The objector questions what 'single quality' could possibly mean. By unpacking two possible interpretations — one in species and one in individual — the author shows that neither resolves the problem, and the individual interpretation is directly refuted by our senses. This objection reveals a key vulnerability in extending the continuous solution to quality.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the objection's structure come through.
Note that the text is incomplete; it cuts off at 'مع أن'.
2
Identify the two interpretations
Find where the passage distinguishes between 'single quality in species' and 'single quality in individual'. Notice the different problems each faces.
3
Understand why each interpretation fails
The species interpretation does not solve the problem because individual qualities remain distinct. The individual interpretation contradicts sensory experience of color change.
The objection uses an example of motion from black to white passing through multiple colors.
4
Reflect on the missing conclusion
Since the text cuts off, consider how the objection likely concludes: the 'ayn solution cannot work for qualitative motion.

Ibn Sina's First Argument for Substantial Change
Ibn Sina's first logical proof against the impossibility of substantial change

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Ibn Sina uses logical conversion of a universal affirmative to prove the possibility of substantial change.

Core Idea
Logical conversion turns a universal truth into a proof of possibility.

Ibn Sina offers a clever logical proof that substantial change is possible. He does not need to show that all moving bodies change substance—only that some do. This lesson unpacks his argument using simple conversion.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the literal translation. Notice the key terms: الكون والفساد, الحركة المستقيمة, تبدل الصور, العكس المستوى.
The argument is concise; pay attention to the logical connector 'وتنعكس'.
2
Identify the premise
Ibn Sina states that every body subject to generation/corruption is subject to rectilinear motion. This is a universal affirmative: 'Every A is B'.
Premise is based on a later proof (not detailed here).
3
Understand simple conversion
A universal affirmative 'Every A is B' can be simply converted to a particular affirmative 'Some B is A'. This is called 'العكس المستوى'.
Simple conversion preserves truth but changes quantity.
4
Derive the conclusion
From the conversion, we get: some bodies subject to rectilinear motion are subject to generation/corruption. Therefore, substantial change is not impossible.
The argument does not require all moving bodies to change; existence of one suffices.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(وأبطله ) أي قول ذلك البعض . ( ابن سينا بوجهين : الأول : ( أنه ( مبرهن ) فيما بعد كما ستطلع عليه ( أن كل ما يصح عليه ) من الأجسام (الكون والفساد ) أعني تبدل الصورة بصورة أخرى تصح عليه الحركة المستقيمة المقتضية لخروج الجسم عن مكانه (وتنعكس) هذه الموجبة الكلية بالعكس المستوى إلى قولنا : بعض ما يصح عليه الحركة المستقيمة عليه الكون والفساد فثبت صحة تبدل الصور في بعض الأجسام وبطل القول بكونه محالاً
And he refuted it”—meaning the saying of that faction—“Ibn Sina in two ways: The first: that it is proven later, as you will see, that every body that is subject to generation and corruption—meaning the change of one form into another—is subject to rectilinear motion requiring the body to leave its place; and this universal affirmative is converted by simple conversion into our statement: some bodies subject to rectilinear motion are subject to generation and corruption. So the validity of change of forms in some bodies is established, and the claim that it is impossible is invalidated.
Source pages p. 223

Commentary
This passage presents Ibn Sina's first argument against those who deny substantial change (كون وفساد) and claim that only change in qualities occurs. The argument is based on logical conversion. Ibn Sina states that it has been proven (or will be proven) that every body that undergoes generation and corruption—i.e., the replacement of one substantial form by another—is also a body that can undergo rectilinear motion, meaning motion that takes the body out of its place. This is a universal affirmative judgment: 'Every A is B' (where A = subject to generation/corruption, B = subject to rectilinear motion). This judgment can be simply converted into 'Some B is A' (some bodies subject to rectilinear motion are subject to generation/corruption). Therefore, the existence of at least some bodies that undergo substantial change is established, refuting the claim that it is impossible. Notice the logical technique: Ibn Sina does not assert that all bodies that move rectilinearly are subject to generation, only that some are. This suffices to prove the possibility.

Key Terms

الحركة المستقيمة
تبدل الصور
العكس المستوى
الموجبة الكلية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument claims that all bodies subject to rectilinear motion undergo substantial change.
Correction: The conversion only gives a particular conclusion: some bodies that move rectilinearly also undergo substantial change. Not all.

Checkpoint
What logical operation does Ibn Sina apply to derive the possibility of substantial change?
A) Contraposition
B) Simple conversion of a universal affirmative
C) Conversion by limitation
D) Reductio ad absurdum

Key Takeaway
Simple conversion of a universal affirmative suffices to establish the possibility of substantial change in some bodies, refuting the claim of impossibility.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have just seen how a logical argument can establish the possibility of substantial change. When you encounter a truth that requires careful reasoning, do you embrace the mental effort or shy away from it? What does your reaction reveal about your desire for truth?
?
Ibn Sina used universal premises and conversion to refute a mistaken view. Are there beliefs you hold that you have never subjected to logical scrutiny? Are you willing to test them even if it means admitting error?
?
The argument proves that change is real and part of God's creation. How does the certainty that God constantly brings about change in the world affect your reliance on Him in the face of personal instability or loss?

Ibn Sina's Second Argument for Generation and Corruption
Ibn Sina presents the second argument for the possibility of generation and corruption.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ibn Sina's argument that the specialisation of elemental parts to their natural places implies that their forms are originated, establishing the possibility of substantial change.

Core Idea
Specialisation of place points to originated forms, proving generation and corruption is possible.

Ibn Sina builds his second argument on a simple observation: each part of an elemental body like water occupies a specific place. He argues that this specialisation cannot be due to the form's essence (since identical forms can be in different places) nor to a mover (since even without a mover, parts would still be somewhere). Therefore, the specialisation must come from the form being originated at that place. This leads to an infinite chain, implying that forms are not eternal but generated, making substantial change possible.

Guided Path
1
Identify the structure of the argument
Read the passage and notice that Ibn Sina presents a claim, then eliminates two alternatives, then concludes with an infinite regress.
2
Understand the two impossible alternatives
The specialisation cannot be due to the form's essence (because identical forms are in different places) nor due to a transporter (because even without one, parts have places).
These are the two possibilities he rejects.
3
Grasp the infinite regress and its implication
Ibn Sina asserts that the specialisation must be due to the form being originated at that place, and this leads back through previous forms ad infinitum, showing that forms are not eternal.
This implies that substantial change (generation and corruption) is possible.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

The second [way]: The specialisation of a particular part of an elemental body—like water, for example—to its natural place, i.e., to a specific part of the natural place of that body, is only due to its form, i.e., the form of that part. And this—I mean the attribution of that specialisation to the form of that part—is only conceivable if that form is originated. Because that specialisation cannot be attributed to the essence of the form as such, since we observe that parts equal in form are located in different places. Nor can it be attributed to a transporter that moved that part to that place, because if we assume the absence of a transporter, the parts of the element would still be in their places, and their presence there would then require a cause. The only cause is that the particular part, at the beginning of its generation, was in a place that was allocated to it by its origination from the agent, and it continued thereafter in it due to its form's requirement. And it was only at the beginning of its formation in that place because it was conceived with another form whose state is analogous to this form, and so on ad infinitum. ... And the answer to the first [way] is that the original ... [commentary explaining the phrase] ... And the answer to the second [way] is the denial of the necessity of origination, because it is possible that the determinant of the parts in their places is a separate entity, and its relation to the whole is not equal [i.e., it can differentiate], and it is possible that the separate entity is multiple in a way that requires that specialisation in those parts. So no reliance can be placed on either of these two ways. Rather, what is relied upon in refuting the denial of generation and corruption is experience and reliance on ... [commentary continues] ... and the statement of the Shifa is as follows: ... and it is not hidden from one of refined intelligence that there is no meaning to the vacillation mentioned in the text upon this explanation, and by my life, he seeks interpretation by changing his wording, and its corruptions are more than can be counted.
Source pages p. 223

Commentary
This passage covers Ibn Sina's second argument and its full refutation. The argument is based on the specialisation of each part of an elemental body to its natural place. Ibn Sina argues that this specialisation cannot be due to the form's essence (since identical forms can be in different places) nor to a mover (since without a mover the parts would still have places). Therefore, it must be due to the form's being originated at a particular place, implying that the form is not eternal but generated. This would mean that substantial change (change of forms) occurs. The refutation proceeds in two parts. First, it addresses the logical conversion used in the argument: if the universal judgment 'every body subject to generation/corruption is subject to rectilinear motion' is taken as an external judgment (about actually existing individuals), it is false because there may be no subject; if taken as a real judgment (about possible individuals), the converse is also real, but real particularity does not imply external existence. Hence the argument does not prove the actual existence of such bodies but only their possibility. Second, it directly denies the necessity that the form must be originated. It is possible that the specialisation is due to a separate entity (مفارق) that could be multiple and differentiate. Therefore, neither argument is conclusive; the real proof for substantial change is empirical (التجربة). The passage then includes extensive commentary explaining the logical terms and quoting Avicenna's own words from the Shifa, emphasizing that the text in question has been misinterpreted.

اختصاص الجزء
الحيز الطبيعي
الصورة الحادثة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think Ibn Sina's argument proves that generation and corruption actually occur in the world.
Correction: The argument only aims to prove that generation and corruption are possible, not that they actually occur.

Checkpoint

A) Specialisation of a part to its place is due to its form's essence.
B) Specialisation of a part to its place is due to an external mover.
C) Specialisation of a part to its place is due to the form being originated.
D) Specialisation of a part to its place is eternal.

Key Takeaway
Ibn Sina's second argument uses the specialisation of place to conclude that elemental forms must be originated, supporting the possibility of generation and corruption.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Ibn Sina argues that even the location of a drop of water is not random but requires an originated cause. When you look at the ordinary details of your life—your home, your health, your daily provisions—do you see them as simply 'natural' or as moments continually originated by Allah? What would it mean for your gratitude if you truly believed each detail was placed?
?
The argument says it is impossible for specialisation to be by essence because identical forms appear in different places. Could it be that you sometimes treat your own existence as if it is self-caused—as if you deserve your place in the world by your own essence? In what area of your life do you need to admit that you are placed by the will of Another?
?
An infinite regress of causes is rejected—there must be a beginning. Your own life had a beginning. Are you living as if you own your days, or as if they are given to you moment by moment by the Originator? What would change if you remembered that every breath is newly originated?

Refuting Ibn Sina's Second Argument: Logical and Causal Critique
Refutation of Ibn Sina's second argument in two parts: logical and causal.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two refutations of Ibn Sina's argument: first, the logical distinction between external and real judgments undermines the inference; second, the necessity of origination is denied by positing a separate determinant.

Core Idea
The argument only proves possibility; causation could be due to a separate entity.

The author now dismantles Ibn Sina's argument with two powerful critiques. First, he examines the logical form of the universal judgment used: if it's about actual individuals (external), it may be false; if about possible ones (real), the converse is also real, but real particularity doesn't entail external existence. Second, he directly challenges the need for the form to be originated, suggesting that a separate entity (مفارق) could determine the specialisation, and it could be multiple, thus not requiring an infinite regress.

Guided Path
1
Understand the external vs real judgment distinction
The refutation begins by analyzing the logical form of the universal judgment. An 'external' judgment is about actually existing individuals, while a 'real' judgment is about possible individuals regardless of existence.
This distinction is crucial for understanding the first refutation.
2
See how this distinction applies
If Ibn Sina's judgment is taken as external, it may be false because there may be no subject. If taken as real, the converse is also real, but that does not prove actual existence.
Thus the argument only shows possibility, not actuality.
3
Understand the second refutation
The second refutation denies the necessity that the form must be originated. It posits that a separate entity (مفارق) could determine the specialisation, and it could be multiple, so the infinite regress is unnecessary.
This directly attacks the core premise.
4
Note the appeal to experience
The author concludes that neither argument is reliable; the real proof for generation and corruption is empirical observation.
This is a methodological point.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه ( الثاني : اختصاص الجزء المعين من الجسم) العنصري كالماء مثلاً ( بحيز طبعاً ) أي بحيز معين من أجزاء الحيز الطبيعي لذلك الجسم، إنما يكون ) لصورته ) أي صورة ذلك الجزء ( وهذا أعني استناد ذلك الاختصاص إلى صورة ذلك الجزء ( أيضاً إنما يتصور إذا كانت تلك الصورة ( حادثة) فإن ذلك الاختصاص لا يجوز أن يستند إلى ذات الصورة من حيث هي لأنا نشاهد أن الأجزاء المتساوية في الصورة حاصلة في أحياز ،متباينة ولا يجوز أن تستند إلى ناقل نقل ذلك الجزء إلى ذلك الحيز، إذ لو قدرنا عدم الناقل لكانت أجزاء العنصر حاصلة في أحيازها ولا بد لحصولها فيها حينئذ من سبب، ولا سبب سوى أن الجزء المعين كان في ابتداء تكونه حاصلاً في حيز تخصص به حدوثه عن الفاعل، واستمر بعد ذلك فيه باقتضاء صورته وإنما كان في ابتداء التكون حاصلاً في ذلك الحيز لكونه متصوراً بصورة أخرى حالها على قياس هذه الصورة وهكذا إلى ما لا نهاية له ) وجواب الأول أنَّ الأصل) إنْ قوله : ( وهذا أيضاً إلخ) نقل عنه قدس سره لو كان لفظة أيضاً متقدماً على قوله : وهذا لكان معناها ظاهراً ، أي كما أن اختصاص الكل بحيزه لصورته كذلك اختصاص كل جزء بحيزه لصورته، وهذا لا يتصور إذا كانت الصورة حادثة وعلى تقدير تأخيرها كان المعنى كما أن الكون والفساد يستلزم صحة الحدوث، كذلك الاستناد إنما يتصور إذا كانت الصورة حادثة . قوله : ( وهذا أيضاً إنما يتصور إلخ) نقل عنه أنه لو كان كلمة أيضاً مقدمة على قوله : وهذا لكان معناها ظاهراً أي كما أن اختصاص الكل بحيزه لصورته كذلك اختصاص كل جزء بحيزه لصورته، وهذا إنما يتصور إذا كانت الصورة حادثة وعلى تقدير تأخيرها كان المعنى، كما أن الكون والفساد يستلزم صحة الحدوث، كذلك الاستناد مما يتصور إذا كانت حادثة . قوله : ( لكونه متصوراً بصورة أخرى حالها على قياس هذه الصورة) أي لكون الجزء من الجسم متصوراً بصورة سابقة على هذه الصورة مناسبة لذلك الجزء من الحيز، بسبب كالمجاورة والمحاذاة وتلخيصه أن هذا الجزء من الهواء، إنما اختص بهذا الجزء من الحيز لأنه ما كان قبل هذه الصورة متصوراً بصورة مائية، ومتحيزاً في جزء من حيز الماء محاذ لهذا الجزء من حيز الهواء، مثلاً فكما تبدل صورته المائية صورة هوائية انتقل إلى ذلك الجزء المحاذي من حيز الهواء، فإن سئل لماذا كان في ذلك الجزء من حيز الماء حال كونه متصوراً بصورة مائية؟ أجيب بأنه قبل ذلك متصور بصورة أخرى مناسبة لذلك الحيز على نحو ما سبق، وهكذا إلى غير النهاية فلا يلزم صدق العكس، إنما لم يقل فيلزم عدم صدق عكسه خارجياً، لأن الكاذب جاز أن يستلزم الصادق . أخذ خارجياً منعنا صدقه لعدم وجود الموضوع عندنا، فلا يلزم صدق العكس ( وإن أخذ حقيقياً صدق وكان العكس كذلك أي حقيقياً أيضاً (ولا يلزم) من صدق ) العكس حقيقياً (صدقه خارجياً لأنه أي الموجب الجزئي الخارجي (أخص) من الموجب الجزئي الحقيقي ومن صدق الأخص لا يلزم صدق الأعم ( فلا يفيد الوجود ) أي فلا يفيد البيان المذكور أن في الخارج جسماً موجوداً يصح عليه الكون والفساد، لجواز أن تصدق الموجبة الجزئية الحقيقية مع السالبة الكلية الخارجية التي تدعيها ( و ) جواب ( الثاني منع وجوب الحدوث ) لجواز أن يكون مخصص الأجزاء بأحيازها أمراً مفارقاً، وتساوي نسبته إلى الكل ممنوع، إذ يجوز أن يكون المفارق متعدداً على وجه يقتضي ذلك الاختصاص في تلك الأجزاء، فلا اعتماد على شيء من هذين الوجهين ( بل المعتمد ( في إبطال نفي الكون والفساد هو التجربة والتعويل على قوله : (إن أخذ خارجياً ) أي يكون الحكم على الأفراد المحققة في الخارج. قوله : ( عندنا ) هم أهل الكون والفساد. قوله : ( وإن أخذ حقيقياً) أي بكون الحكم على الأفراد المقدرة سواء كانت متحققة في الخارج أو لا . قوله : ( فلا يفيد البيان إلخ) فيه أن المطلوب إمكان الكون والفساد وإذا صدقت الموجبة الحقيقية ثبت الإمكان لأن الحكم في القضايا الحقيقية على الأفراد الممكنة، وإلا لم يصدق حقيقية كلية على ما بين في محله . قوله: لجواز أن تصدق الموجبة إلخ) أي بعض الأفراد المقدرة مما يصح عليه الحركة المستقيمة موجودة وهي العناصر فلا تصدق السالبة الكلية الخارجية، أعني لا شيء مما يصدق عليه الحركة المستقيمة يصح الكون والفساد، ثم إن عبارة الشفاء هكذا : إنَّ الأجسام التي في طباعها أن يقبل الكون والفساد في طباعها أن تتحرك على الاستقامة فيجب من ذلك لمن أحسن النظر أن بعض الأجسام المتحركة على الاستقامة تقبل الكون والفساد فيكون بعض الأجسام البسيط قابلة للكون والفساد .انتهى ولا يخفى على من له لطف قريحة أنه لا معنى للترديد المذكور في المتن على هذا البيان ولعمري إنه يطلب التفسير بتغيير عبارته، ومفاسده أكثر من أن يحصى.
The second [way]: The specialisation of a particular part of an elemental body—like water, for example—to its natural place, i.e., to a specific part of the natural place of that body, is only due to its form, i.e., the form of that part. And this—I mean the attribution of that specialisation to the form of that part—is only conceivable if that form is originated. Because that specialisation cannot be attributed to the essence of the form as such, since we observe that parts equal in form are located in different places. Nor can it be attributed to a transporter that moved that part to that place, because if we assume the absence of a transporter, the parts of the element would still be in their places, and their presence there would then require a cause. The only cause is that the particular part, at the beginning of its generation, was in a place that was allocated to it by its origination from the agent, and it continued thereafter in it due to its form's requirement. And it was only at the beginning of its formation in that place because it was conceived with another form whose state is analogous to this form, and so on ad infinitum. ... And the answer to the first [way] is that the original ... [commentary explaining the phrase] ... And the answer to the second [way] is the denial of the necessity of origination, because it is possible that the determinant of the parts in their places is a separate entity, and its relation to the whole is not equal [i.e., it can differentiate], and it is possible that the separate entity is multiple in a way that requires that specialisation in those parts. So no reliance can be placed on either of these two ways. Rather, what is relied upon in refuting the denial of generation and corruption is experience and reliance on ... [commentary continues] ... and the statement of the Shifa is as follows: ... and it is not hidden from one of refined intelligence that there is no meaning to the vacillation mentioned in the text upon this explanation, and by my life, he seeks interpretation by changing his wording, and its corruptions are more than can be counted.
Source pages p. 223

Commentary
This passage covers Ibn Sina's second argument and its full refutation. The argument is based on the specialisation of each part of an elemental body to its natural place. Ibn Sina argues that this specialisation cannot be due to the form's essence (since identical forms can be in different places) nor to a mover (since without a mover the parts would still have places). Therefore, it must be due to the form's being originated at a particular place, implying that the form is not eternal but generated. This would mean that substantial change (change of forms) occurs. The refutation proceeds in two parts. First, it addresses the logical conversion used in the argument: if the universal judgment 'every body subject to generation/corruption is subject to rectilinear motion' is taken as an external judgment (about actually existing individuals), it is false because there may be no subject; if taken as a real judgment (about possible individuals), the converse is also real, but real particularity does not imply external existence. Hence the argument does not prove the actual existence of such bodies but only their possibility. Second, it directly denies the necessity that the form must be originated. It is possible that the specialisation is due to a separate entity (مفارق) that could be multiple and differentiate. Therefore, neither argument is conclusive; the real proof for substantial change is empirical (التجربة). The passage then includes extensive commentary explaining the logical terms and quoting Avicenna's own words from the Shifa, emphasizing that the text in question has been misinterpreted.

Key Terms
الموجبة الجزئية الحقيقية
الموجبة الجزئية الخارجية
المفارق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the refutation rejects the entire argument as invalid, leaving no support for generation and corruption.
Correction: The refutation only shows that Ibn Sina's argument is not conclusive; it does not deny the possibility of generation and corruption. The author later appeals to experience as the real proof.

Checkpoint
According to the refutation, why does the logical conversion not prove the existence of bodies subject to generation and corruption?
A) Because the external judgment is false.
B) Because the real judgment does not require actual existence.
C) Because the form is not originated.
D) Because the mover is separate.

Key Takeaway
The refutation shows that Ibn Sina's argument only proves possibility, not actuality, and that causation can be due to a separate entity, thus the argument is not conclusive.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Ibn Sina's argument seemed logically sound but had hidden flaws. Are there any arguments or beliefs you hold about your faith that you have never critically examined? Will you set aside time this week to scrutinize one of them using the Quran and Sunnah?
?
The lesson shows that possibility does not equal actuality. In your daily life, do you treat possibilities (like 'maybe I'll pray better tomorrow') as if they are certain, rather than taking concrete steps? What is one possibility you need to turn into a definite action today?
?
The passage states that the decisive proof for generation and corruption is empirical, not just rational. What personal experiences have made the truths of Islam certain for you? If you lack such certainty, what steps can you take to seek direct experience of faith through sincere worship and reflection?

Objection to Continuous Motion in Action and Passion
Objection against continuous motion in action/passion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical objection that the transition between opposite qualities (e.g., heating to cooling) cannot be continuous but requires a pause.

Core Idea
Heating must stop before cooling begins, so no continuous motion.

The discussion of motion in the categories of action and passion raises a subtle puzzle: can something move continuously from being hot to being cold? The author presents an objection that challenges continuous motion, arguing that the subject cannot be simultaneously oriented towards both heat and cold, so a pause is inevitable. This passage examines that objection closely.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic or translation carefully to grasp the objection's structure.
Watch for the key argument: if heating remains, impossible orientation to both opposites.
2
Identify the objection's core reasoning
The objection says: if heating persists, then the subject is oriented to both heat and cold simultaneously, which is impossible. Therefore heating must cease before cooling begins.
This creates a necessary time of rest between them.
3
Compare with positional motions
The author analogizes this to opposing motions in place (e.g., up and down), which also require a pause.
Understand the logical necessity of a rest.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
The objection concludes that there is no continuous motion from heating to cooling. Ask yourself: does this objection succeed?
The passage only presents the objection; the answer may come later.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الرابع - المقصد الثالث : وقع الحركة من المقولات وتغيراتها يفعل وأن ينفعل فأثبت بعضهم فيهما الحركة وأبطل ( قول هذا المثبت ( بأن المنتقل من التسخن إلى التبرد ) مثلاً ( لا يكون تسخنه باقياً وإلا لزم التوجه إلى الضدين معاً ) لأن التبرد توجه إلى البرودة والتسخن توجه إلى السخونة، ومن المحال أن يكون الشيء الواحد في الزمان الواحد متوجهاً إلى الضدين، وإذا لم يكن التسخن باقياً فالتبرد لا يوجد إلا بعد وقوف التسخن ( فبينهما زمان سكون كما بين الحركتين الأينيتين المتضادتين فلا يكون هناك حركة من التسخن إلى التبرد على الاستمرار، وكذا الحال في التسخين والتبريد
The Fourth Observatory - The Third Objective: The occurrence of motion among the categories and their changes. To act and to be acted upon, so some affirmed motion in them and invalidated (the statement of this affirmer (that the one transitioning from heating to cooling) for example (his heating cannot remain otherwise it would necessitate facing both opposites together) because cooling is an orientation toward coldness and heating is an orientation toward heat, and it is impossible for one thing at one time to be oriented toward both opposites. And if heating does not remain, then cooling does not exist except after the cessation of heating. So between them is a time of rest just as between two opposing positional motions, so there is no continuous motion from heating to cooling. The same applies to heating and cooling.
Source pages p. 232

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer pair within a larger discussion about motion in the categories of action and passion. First, the author states that some affirmed motion in these categories. Then he presents an objection against this affirmation: the one who moves from being heated (al-tasakhkhun) to being cooled (al-tabarud) cannot have his heating persist, otherwise he would be simultaneously oriented toward both opposites (heat and cold). Since that is impossible, the heating must cease before cooling begins, implying a time of rest between them. This resembles the rest between two opposing motions in place (al-harakatān al-ayniyyatān al-mutaḍāddatān). Therefore, there is no continuous motion from heating to cooling. The same applies to heating and cooling. The author does not here give the answer; he merely reports the objection. However, the passage as given includes both the objection and the conclusion that there is a pause. The argument uses the principle that a single subject cannot be directed to two opposites simultaneously.

Key Terms
التسخن (heating)
التبرد (cooling)
التوجه إلى الضدين (orientation to opposites)
الحركتين الأينيتين المتضادتين (opposing positional motions)
زمان سكون (time of rest)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The transition between opposite qualities (like hot to cold) is smooth and continuous without any interruption.
Correction: This passage argues that a time of rest is logically necessary because the subject cannot be simultaneously oriented to both opposites.

Checkpoint
Why does the objection claim there cannot be continuous motion from heating to cooling?
A) Because heating and cooling are simultaneous.
B) Because the subject cannot be oriented to both heat and cold at once, so heating must cease before cooling begins, creating a pause.
C) Because motion only occurs in place.
D) Because the subject is not affected by heat or cold.

Key Takeaway
The transition between opposite qualities logically requires a pause, as simultaneous orientation to opposites is impossible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever been torn between two opposing desires, like wanting both comfort and discipline? How does this logical impossibility of being simultaneously oriented to opposites expose the need to decisively cease one before pursuing the other—and what would change in your life if you applied this to a current inner conflict?
?
The objection shows a necessary pause between heating and cooling. Where in your spiritual life have you tried to rush from one state to another without allowing a moment of stillness—such as quickly moving from repentance back to sin, or from focus to distraction? What would it look like to honor that 'time of rest' today?
?
This argument forces you to acknowledge that you cannot be moving toward two opposite ends at once. Is there an area of your belief or practice where you are trying to simultaneously affirm something and its opposite—like claiming to trust God while also hoarding control? What must cease for the opposite to begin, and will you take that step now?

The Objection of Degrees in Heating
An objection arguing for gradual change through degrees.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that gradual change through degrees avoids the problem of facing two opposites and keeps motion continuous until the endpoint.

Core Idea
Degrees allow gradual change without direct opposition.

Before we accept that motion in action and passion is dependent, we must consider a powerful objection: heating comes in degrees of intensity. This objection argues that a thing can gradually transition from strong heat to weak heat to cold, so motion is continuous and does not require facing both heat and cold at once. Let's examine this argument carefully.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection passage
Read the Arabic passage once. Then read the translation. Notice that the proponent (qā'il) starts by stating that heating has degrees.
The key term 'مراتب' means degrees or levels.
2
Understand the gradual transition
The objection claims that moving from a stronger degree to a weaker degree, then gradually to cooling, is possible. Why is this significant? It avoids the problem of moving directly from heat to cold.
Direct opposition would require instant change; gradual avoids that.
3
Grasp the implication for motion
The objection concludes that motion is not interrupted in the middle, only at the end. This challenges the earlier claim that motion in these categories is problematic.
The proponent is defending the independence of motion in action/passion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولقائل أن يقول : إن التسخن له مراتب مختلفة في القوة والضعف فيجوز أن ينتقل المتسخن من مرتبة إلى أضعف منها وهكذا إلى أن يصل بالتدريج إلى مرتبة من مراتب التبرد فلا يلزم التوجه إلى الضدين ولا انقطاع الحركة في أثنائها بل عند انتهائها والحق أنهما أي الحركة فيهما (تبع الحركة) في غيرهما لأنهما أيضاً حالتان نسبيتان فلا يستقلان في الثبات والتغير، فالحركة فيهما تابعة للحركة ( إما في القوة إرادة كانت أو طبيعة أو في الآلة وإما في القابل) وذلك لأن العزيمة قد تنفسخ يسيراً يسيرا، والطبيعة قد تخور كذلك والآلة قد تكل، هكذا ففي جميع هذه الصور يتبدل الحال أولاً، إما في الإرادة أو في الطبيعة أو في على سبيل التدريج ثم يتبعه التبدل في الفاعلية كذلك، وأما القابل فربما ينتقص قبوله واستعداده لتمام الفعل شيئاً فشيئاً، فتقع الحركة فيه أولاً، وتتبعها الحركة في الفاعلية، وأنت خبير بأن التبدل في التأثير يستلزم التبدل في التأثر فتقع الحركة في المقولتين تبعاً.
A proponent may say: 'Heating has different degrees of intensity and weakness. So it is permissible for the heated thing to transition from a stronger degree to a weaker one, and so on gradually until it reaches a degree of cooling. Thus there is no necessitation of orientation toward both opposites, nor interruption of motion in its midst, but only at its end.' And the truth is that the two (i.e., motion in them) are (dependent on motion) in other categories because they are also relational states and are not independent in persistence and change. So motion in them is dependent on motion (either in the power, be it will or nature, or in the instrument, or in the receptacle). That is because resolve may weaken little by little, nature may slacken likewise, and the instrument may tire. Thus in all these cases the state changes first, either in will, nature, or instrument gradually, then the change in the agent follows likewise. As for the receptacle, its receptivity and readiness for the completion of the action may diminish little by little, so motion occurs in it first, and motion in the agent follows it. And you are aware that change in the effect necessarily entails change in the being affected, so motion occurs in both categories dependently.
Source pages p. 232

Commentary
This passage presents a second objection and the author's conclusion on the topic. First, a proponent (qā'il) objects that heating has different degrees of intensity (quwwah) and weakness (ḍa'f). The transition from strong heating to weak heating and then gradually to cooling does not require simultaneously facing both opposites, because at each step the subject is only moving to a weaker degree of heat, not directly to cold. Thus the motion is continuous up to the endpoint. The author then states the truth (al-ḥaqq): motion in the categories of action and passion (fi'l and infi'āl) is actually dependent on motion in other categories, because these two are relational states (ḥālāt nisbiyyah) and cannot persist or change independently. He explains that motion in them follows motion in the power (either will or nature), in the instrument (ālah), or in the receptacle (qābil). He gives examples: resolve weakens gradually, nature slackens, the instrument tires. The change occurs first in these, then follows in the agent. Similarly, the receptacle's capacity may diminish, so motion occurs in it first and then in the agent. Ultimately, change in the effect (ta'thīr) necessitates change in the being affected (ta'aththur), so motion occurs in both categories dependently.

Key Terms
مراتب (degrees)
القوة (intensity)
الضعف (weakness)
التسخن (heating)
التبرد (cooling)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that any change from heat to cold must involve facing both opposites at the same instant.
Correction: The objection shows that gradual change through intermediate degrees avoids this, as the object always moves to a weaker degree of heat, not directly to cold.

Checkpoint
According to the objection, why does gradual transition through degrees avoid the problem of facing opposites?
A) Because the object moves directly to cold.
B) Because at each step the object moves to a weaker degree of heat, not the opposite cold.
C) Because motion is impossible.
D) Because degrees are not real.

Key Takeaway
Gradual change through degrees allows continuous motion without facing direct opposites.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a bad habit you have tried to change. Did you attempt to gradually reduce it, hoping to avoid the direct confrontation with its opposite virtue? According to the objection, this gradual approach seems seamless. But does the Qur'an command gradual change or a decisive turning? How does this lesson expose your desire for a painless transition? What one step can you take today that directly faces the opposite quality?
?
The objection claims that motion only stops at the end. In your life, do you mistake gradual progress for true change, delaying the final moment of full commitment? What would it mean for you to reach that endpoint without interruption? Are you willing to accept that real change requires a clear break, not just a slow drift?
