Motion in Action and Passion is Dependent
Conclusion that motion in action and passion is dependent on other categories.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that motion in the categories of action and passion is dependent on motion in other categories (power, instrument, receptacle) because they are relational states.

Core Idea
Motion in action/passion follows change in power, instrument, or receptacle.

The objection about degrees seems plausible, but the author now clarifies the truth: motion in action and passion is not independent. These categories are relational states that cannot change on their own. Instead, their motion depends on prior changes in the agent's power (will or nature), the instrument, or the receptacle. Let's see how the author explains this dependency.

Guided Path
1
Read the conclusion passage
Read the Arabic from 'والحق أنهما' to the end. Then read the translation. Notice the phrase 'الحركة فيهما تبع الحركة في غيرهما'.
'تبع' means dependent or following.
2
Identify the sources of dependency
The author lists three things on which motion in action/passion depends: the power (will or nature), the instrument, and the receptacle. Find these in the text.
The text says: 'إما في القوة إرادة كانت أو طبيعة أو في الآلة وإما في القابل'.
3
Understand the mechanism
The author gives examples: resolve weakens gradually, nature slackens, instrument tires, receptacle's capacity diminishes. The change occurs first in these, then follows in the agent and patient.
This shows that motion in action and passion is not primary but secondary.
4
Grasp the final point
The passage ends with the conclusion that change in the effect necessitates change in being affected, so motion occurs in both categories dependently.
This ties the two categories together.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولقائل أن يقول : إن التسخن له مراتب مختلفة في القوة والضعف فيجوز أن ينتقل المتسخن من مرتبة إلى أضعف منها وهكذا إلى أن يصل بالتدريج إلى مرتبة من مراتب التبرد فلا يلزم التوجه إلى الضدين ولا انقطاع الحركة في أثنائها بل عند انتهائها والحق أنهما أي الحركة فيهما (تبع الحركة) في غيرهما لأنهما أيضاً حالتان نسبيتان فلا يستقلان في الثبات والتغير، فالحركة فيهما تابعة للحركة ( إما في القوة إرادة كانت أو طبيعة أو في الآلة وإما في القابل) وذلك لأن العزيمة قد تنفسخ يسيراً يسيرا، والطبيعة قد تخور كذلك والآلة قد تكل، هكذا ففي جميع هذه الصور يتبدل الحال أولاً، إما في الإرادة أو في الطبيعة أو في على سبيل التدريج ثم يتبعه التبدل في الفاعلية كذلك، وأما القابل فربما ينتقص قبوله واستعداده لتمام الفعل شيئاً فشيئاً، فتقع الحركة فيه أولاً، وتتبعها الحركة في الفاعلية، وأنت خبير بأن التبدل في التأثير يستلزم التبدل في التأثر فتقع الحركة في المقولتين تبعاً.
A proponent may say: 'Heating has different degrees of intensity and weakness. So it is permissible for the heated thing to transition from a stronger degree to a weaker one, and so on gradually until it reaches a degree of cooling. Thus there is no necessitation of orientation toward both opposites, nor interruption of motion in its midst, but only at its end.' And the truth is that the two (i.e., motion in them) are (dependent on motion) in other categories because they are also relational states and are not independent in persistence and change. So motion in them is dependent on motion (either in the power, be it will or nature, or in the instrument, or in the receptacle). That is because resolve may weaken little by little, nature may slacken likewise, and the instrument may tire. Thus in all these cases the state changes first, either in will, nature, or instrument gradually, then the change in the agent follows likewise. As for the receptacle, its receptivity and readiness for the completion of the action may diminish little by little, so motion occurs in it first, and motion in the agent follows it. And you are aware that change in the effect necessarily entails change in the being affected, so motion occurs in both categories dependently.
Source pages p. 232

Commentary
This passage presents a second objection and the author's conclusion on the topic. First, a proponent (qā'il) objects that heating has different degrees of intensity (quwwah) and weakness (ḍa'f). The transition from strong heating to weak heating and then gradually to cooling does not require simultaneously facing both opposites, because at each step the subject is only moving to a weaker degree of heat, not directly to cold. Thus the motion is continuous up to the endpoint. The author then states the truth (al-ḥaqq): motion in the categories of action and passion (fi'l and infi'āl) is actually dependent on motion in other categories, because these two are relational states (ḥālāt nisbiyyah) and cannot persist or change independently. He explains that motion in them follows motion in the power (either will or nature), in the instrument (ālah), or in the receptacle (qābil). He gives examples: resolve weakens gradually, nature slackens, the instrument tires. The change occurs first in these, then follows in the agent. Similarly, the receptacle's capacity may diminish, so motion occurs in it first and then in the agent. Ultimately, change in the effect (ta'thīr) necessitates change in the being affected (ta'aththur), so motion occurs in both categories dependently.

الفاعلية (agentivity)
القابل (receptacle)
الحالتان النسبيتان (relational states)
الآلة (instrument)
القوة (power)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think motion in action and passion is independent and primary.
Correction: The author clarifies they are relational states and depend on prior changes in power, instrument, or receptacle.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the main reason motion in action and passion is not independent?

B) Because they are relational states that rely on change in other categories.
C) Because they are impossible.

Key Takeaway
Motion in action and passion is always dependent on prior changes in power, instrument, or receptacle.

Unity of Subject and Ma Fihi for Personal Unity of Motion
Explains that unity of motion requires unity of subject and unity of 'ma fihi'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that personal unity of motion requires unity of the subject (mahall) and unity of 'ma fihi' (the category), and that unity of 'ma fihi' suffices for unity of the extremes but not vice versa.

Core Idea
Unity of motion depends on the path, not just the start and end points.

This passage from Sharh al-Mawaqif discusses the precise conditions under which motion can be considered one and the same individual motion. It clarifies that mere unity of the subject or of the beginning and end is insufficient; the category in which motion occurs ('ma fihi') must also be one. Through concrete examples, the author demonstrates why this condition is both necessary and sufficient.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once through, then read the literal translation. Try to grasp the overall argument before diving into details.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the three key elements
Look for references to 'al-mahall' (subject), 'ma fihi' (category of motion), and 'ma minhu'/'ma ilayh' (beginning and end). Understand how they relate.
These are the conceptual building blocks.
3
Analyze the example of multiple motions in one subject
Notice that a single thing can undergo alteration, growth, and spatial motion simultaneously. These are distinct motions because their 'ma fihi' differs, even though the subject is one.
This shows unity of subject is not enough.
4
Examine the argument about extremes and paths
The passage argues that unity of 'ma minhu' and 'ma ilayh' does not guarantee unity of motion, because different paths (different 'ma fihi') can lead from the same start to the same end. The color-change examples illustrate this.
This refutes the misconception that extremes alone suffice.
5
Summarize the conditions for personal unity
Conclude that personal unity of motion requires unity of subject and, more crucially, unity of 'ma fihi'. Unity of extremes follows from unity of 'ma fihi', but not conversely.
This is the lesson's core takeaway.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يقوم العرض الواحد بالشخصين بمحلين، ولا بد أيضاً في وحدتها الشخصية من وحدة ما فيه الحركة أعني المقولة إذ الشيء الواحد قد يستحيل وينمو معاً في زمان كونه قاطعاً لمسافة فيكون كل من الاستحالة والنمو وقطع المسافة حركة على حدة وإن اتحد المحل وإنما تعددت الحركة هاهنا مع اتحاده من حيث اختلف ما فيه للحركة اختلافاً جنسياً موجباً لاختلاف الحركة بالجنس كما سيأتي بل قد يعرض له أي للشيء الواحد أنواع من الاستحالة كالتسخن والتسود والتروح في الفاكهة مثلا فتتعدد الحركة لاختلاف ما فيه بحسب النوع، وإن اندرجت تلك الأنواع في جنس واحد هو الكيف المحسوس بل نقول : إذا تعددت المسافة وما في حكمها بحسب الشخص تعددت الحركة بحسبه لأن الحركة في مسافة تغاير الحركة في مسافة أخرى قطعا ويتبع ذلك أي وحدة ما فيه الحركة وحدة ما منه وما إليه إذ لو اختلف المبدأ والمنتهى لم يكن ما فيه واحداً بالضرورة فوحدتهما تابعة لوحدة ما فيه فاشتراط وحدة ما فيه يغني عن اشتراط وحدتهما ولا يكفي في الوحدة الشخصية للحركة وحدة ما منه وما إليه دون اعتبار وحدة ما فيه لجواز اتحادهما بالشخص مع تعدد الحركة بأن تكون الطرق مختلفة فيما بين مبدأ معين ومنتهى معين كما يتوجه الجسم تارة من البياض إلى الغبرة الى العودية إلى السواد و تارة منه أي من البياض إلى الصفرة إلى الخضرة إلى النيلية إلى السواد و تارة منه إلى الحمرة إلى القتمة إلى السواد فالحركة من البياض إلى السواد المعينين يمكن أن تفرض على هذه الوجوه فيكون المبدأ والمنتهى واحداً مع تعدد الحركة بواسطة تعدد ما فيه وكذا الحال فيما إذا سلك الجسم من مبدأ معين إلى منتهى معين تارة على الاستقامة وتارة على الاستدارة فظهر أن اعتبار وحدتهما لا يغني عن اعتبار وحدة ما فيه كما كان اعتبار وحدته مغنياً عن اعتبار وحدتهما.
An individual accident subsists in two subjects (bi-mahallayn). Also necessary for its personal unity is the unity of 'what is in it' (ma fihi) for motion, I mean the category. For a single thing may undergo alteration and growth simultaneously while traversing a distance, so each of alteration, growth, and traversing distance is a separate motion even if the subject is one. Motion is multiplied here despite the unity of the subject due to the difference of what is in it for motion, a generic difference necessitating difference of motion in genus, as will come. Rather, a single thing may undergo types of alteration, like heating, blackening, and drying in fruit, for example, so motion multiplies due to the difference of what is in it in species, even if those species are under one genus which is sensible quality. Rather we say: if the distance and what is analogous to it are multiple in person, motion is multiple accordingly, because motion in one distance is different from motion in another distance necessarily. And that follows, i.e., the unity of what is in it for motion necessitates the unity of 'from which' (ma minhu) and 'to which' (ma ilayh), for if the beginning and end differed, what is in it would not be one necessarily. So their unity is dependent on the unity of what is in it. Thus stipulating unity of what is in it suffices for stipulating their unity. And it is not sufficient for the personal unity of motion to have unity of 'from which' and 'to which' without considering unity of what is in it, because it is possible for them to be one in person while motion is multiple, by the paths being different between a fixed beginning and a fixed end. As when a body goes sometimes from whiteness to gray to dark to black, and sometimes from whiteness to yellow to green to indigo to black, and sometimes from whiteness to red to brown to black. So motion from the fixed whiteness to the fixed blackness can be supposed on these paths, so the beginning and end are one while motion is multiple by virtue of the multiplicity of what is in it. And similarly if the body travels from a fixed beginning to a fixed end sometimes straight and sometimes curved. Thus it becomes clear that considering their unity does not suffice for considering the unity of what is in it, just as considering its unity sufficed for considering their unity.
Source pages p. 239

Commentary
This passage discusses the conditions for the personal unity (al-wahda al-shakhṣiyya) of motion. It begins by stating that an accident subsists in a subject, and for its personal unity, the unity of 'what is in it' (ma fihi) for motion is necessary. It explains that a single thing can undergo alteration, growth, and spatial motion simultaneously, and these are separate motions even if the subject is one, because the 'ma fihi' (the qualitative, quantitative, or spatial category in which motion occurs) differs. Motion multiplies if the 'ma fihi' differs in genus or species, as with types of alteration in a fruit. The passage then asserts that if the distance or analogous thing is multiple in person, motion is multiple, because motion in one distance is different from motion in another. It then argues that unity of 'ma fihi' entails unity of 'ma minhu' (the beginning) and 'ma ilayh' (the end), but the converse is not true: unity of beginning and end does not guarantee unity of motion, because different paths (different 'ma fihi') can lead from the same beginning to the same end, as illustrated by variations of color change from white to black or different spatial routes. Thus, considering the unity of the extremes is insufficient without considering the unity of 'ma fihi', while considering the unity of 'ma fihi' suffices.

Key Terms
الوحدة الشخصية
ما فيه الحركة
ما منه وما إليه

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If the starting and ending points of a motion are the same, then the motion is one and the same.
Correction: The passage shows that different intermediate paths (different 'ma fihi') can lead from the same start to the same end, resulting in multiple distinct motions. For example, changing from white to black via different color sequences yields different motions.

Checkpoint
Why does unity of 'ma minhu' and 'ma ilayh' not suffice for the personal unity of motion?
A) Because the subject must also be one
B) Because the time must also be one
C) Because the same beginning and end can be traversed via different paths, making motion multiple
D) Because the mover must be one

Key Takeaway
Personal unity of motion requires unity of 'ma fihi' (category), which automatically ensures unity of the beginning and end, but the reverse is not true.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In your spiritual journey, do you assume that because your ultimate goal is fixed (e.g., Jannah), your efforts are one unified motion? But what if the 'paths' you take—your different methods, intentions, and circumstances—are actually multiple disjoint motions? Examine your heart: are you moving in a straight line toward Allah, or are you taking detours that make your 'motion' fragmented? What would it mean for you to unify your 'ma fihi'—to seek consistency in the category of your worship?
?
Consider a sin you've repented from. You may have thought that leaving that sin is one motion—from disobedience to obedience. But were there different paths? Different excuses, different levels of sincerity, different temptations? Have you treated your repentance as a single event when in reality it was a series of separate struggles? How does this lesson challenge your view of your own moral growth, and what will you do to make your 'motion' truly one?

Time as a Necessary Condition for Motion Unity
Addresses an objection and concludes that time unity is also necessary.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why unity of time is a necessary condition for the personal unity of motion, based on the principle that the non-existent cannot be returned identically.

Core Idea
Time is not incidental; motion in different times is inherently different.

This passage tackles an objection about the role of time in defining a single motion. It argues that without time unity, even if subject and path are the same, the motion could be spatiotemporally distinct. The discussion relies on the fundamental kalam principle that the non-existent cannot be identically recreated.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Let the author's argument unfold.
Notice the objection starts with 'ولقائل أن يقول'
2
Identify the objection and its example
Find the objection: that without time unity, the unity of extremes (ma minhu and ma ilayh) does not guarantee unity of motion. The example is a body moving up and down the same distance.
3
Understand the resolution and conditions
The author asserts time unity is necessary because motion in one time is essentially different from motion in another time, based on the principle of non-return of the non-existent. Then enumerate the three conditions for motion unity: subject, time, and content (ma fihi).
This step connects to the learning objective.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولقائل أن يقول : إذا لم يلاحظ وحدة الزمان لم تكن وحدة ما فيه مستلزمة لوحدتهما ولا اختلافهما مستلزماً لاختلاف ما فيه، فإن جسماً واحداً قد يتحرك في مسافة واحدة تارة صاعداً وتارة هابطاً وإذا لوحظ وحدة الزمان كان وحدتهما مقتضية لوحدته أيضاً ولا بد في وحدة الحركة من وحدة الزمان، إذ الحركة في زمان غير الحركة في زمان آخر ضرورة، وذلك بناء على أن المعدوم لا يعاد بعينه فإنه لو جوز إعادته كذلك لجاز أن تكون الحركة في زمان عين الحركة في زمان آخر فظهر أنه لا بد للحركة في وحدتها الشخصية من وحدة الموضوع بالشخص ومن وحدة الزمان ومن وحدة ما فيه، وليست وحدته لازمة لوحدتهما لما مر من وقوع الاستحالة والنمو وقطع المسافة في جسم واحد في زمان واحد وإذا اتحدت هذه الثلاثة اتحد المبدأ والمنتهى أيضاً وكانت الحركة واحدة شخصية قطعاً ولو اعتبر وحدتهما مع وحدة المحل والزمان لكفي ولزم وحدة ما فيه كما أشرنا إليه إلا أن اعتبار الثلاثة أولى من اعتبار الأربعة والمآل فيهما واحد وهو أنه لا بد في تشخص الحركة من وحدة أمور تلك السنة لأن اختلاف واحد منها أي واحد كان يستلزم تعدد الحركة كما لا يخفى.
And a speaker may say: If the unity of time is not considered, then the unity of what is in it does not necessitate their (i.e., the extremes') unity, nor does their difference necessitate the difference of what is in it. For a single body may move in one distance sometimes ascending and sometimes descending. And if the unity of time is considered, their unity would necessitate its unity as well. And it is necessary for the unity of motion to have unity of time, because motion in one time is different from motion in another time necessarily. That is based on the principle that the non-existent is not returned in its very self. For if its return were permitted, then it would be permitted that motion in one time be the very motion in another time. Thus it becomes clear that motion in its personal unity requires the unity of the subject in person, the unity of time, and the unity of what is in it. And its unity is not necessitated by their unity, due to what has passed of alteration, growth, and traversing distance occurring in one body in one time. And when these three are united, the beginning and end are also united, and the motion is one personally definitely. And if their unity were considered along with the unity of the subject and time, it would suffice and necessitate the unity of what is in it, as we indicated. However, considering the three is preferable to considering the four, and the outcome of both is one: that for the individuation of motion, the unity of these matters is necessary, because the difference of any one of them, whichever it is, necessitates the multiplicity of motion, as is not hidden.
Source pages p. 239

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and its answer regarding the role of time in the unity of motion. The objector argues that without considering the unity of time, the unity of 'ma fihi' does not entail the unity of the extremes (ma minhu and ma ilayh), and their difference does not entail difference of 'ma fihi'. The example given is a body moving in one distance sometimes upward and sometimes downward. If time is unified, then the unity of extremes would also necessitate the unity of 'ma fihi'? Actually, the objector suggests that time must be factored in. The author then asserts that unity of time is necessary because motion in one time is essentially different from motion in another time, based on the principle that the non-existent (ma'dum) cannot be returned identically. Therefore, the personal unity of motion requires three things: unity of the subject (al-mawdu'), unity of time, and unity of 'ma fihi'. It is also noted that the unity of 'ma fihi' is not necessitated by the unity of the extremes and time alone, because a single body can undergo alteration, growth, and spatial motion simultaneously in one time. When these three (subject, time, ma fihi) are united, the extremes are automatically united, and motion is one personally. Considering only the extremes with subject and time would also imply unity of 'ma fihi', but the three-condition approach is more fundamental. The key point is that difference in any one of these conditions entails multiplicity of motion.

Key Terms
وحدة الزمان
المعدوم لا يعاد بعينه
وحدة الموضوع
تشخص الحركة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Unity of motion requires only unity of subject and extremes (path).
Correction: Unity of time is also necessary because motion in different times is inherently different; the non-existent cannot be returned identically.

Checkpoint
What is the basis for the necessity of unity of time for motion's personal unity?
A) Motion is defined by time
B) The non-existent cannot be returned in its very self, so motion in one time cannot be the same as motion in another time
C) Time is a category of motion
D) The mover requires time

Key Takeaway
Personal unity of motion requires unity of subject, unity of time, and unity of content (ma fihi); difference in any one entails multiplicity of motion.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If every action you do at a different time is a distinct motion with its own identity, how does that reshape your awareness of the uniqueness of each moment you live? Will you treat each moment as irreplaceable, belonging only to itself?
?
The principle 'the non-existent cannot be returned' means you can never repeat an act of worship or obedience exactly as before. How does this confront your tendency to perform acts of devotion mechanically, assuming they are the same? What will you do to ensure each act is present and intentional, as if it will never come again?
?
God sees each of your movements as unique, with its own time and subject. If your Lord records every motion distinctly, how does that accountability weigh on you today? Will you pause before every action, knowing it is a one-time event before Him?

The Condition for Opposite Motions: Opposition in Origin and Destination
Establishes that opposite motions require opposition in both their starting and ending points.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that opposite motions require opposition in both their starting point (origin) and ending point (destination), and how this differs from mere difference.

Core Idea
Opposite motions need opposite origins and destinations.

In this lesson, we examine a key principle from kalam cosmology: what makes two motions truly opposite? The passage explains that it is not enough for the starting points to be opposite, nor the endpoints alone. Both the 'from what' and 'to what' must be opposed in a specific way. This distinction clarifies many examples of motion and opposition.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation once to get the overall argument. Notice the examples and the logical flow.
2
Identify the core condition
The passage states that opposite motions require opposition in both the origin and destination, considered together as 'from what' and 'to what'. Understand why a single opposite is insufficient.
The key phrase is 'من حيث هما كذلك' (inasmuch as they are such).
3
Analyze the three classifications
The passage classifies the relationship between origin and destination into three types: essential opposition (e.g., black-white), difference without opposition (e.g., black-red), and accidental opposition (e.g., center-circumference). Study each with the given examples.
Only essential and accidental opposition produce opposite motions; mere difference does not.
4
Reflect on the role of 'extreme difference'
The condition hinges on 'غاية الخلاف' (extreme difference). Consider why this concept ties the origin and destination together.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بل تضاد الحركات للتوجه من الأطراف وإليها أعني بحسب ما منه وما إليه جميعاً من حيث هما كذلك. ولا يكفي لتضاد الحركة تضاد المبدأين فقط، فإن الحركة من السواد إلى الحمرة لا تضاد الحركة من البياض إلى الحمرة. ولا التضاد بين المنتهيين فقط، فإن الحركة من الحمرة إلى البياض لا تضاد الحركة من الحمرة إلى السواد. وذلك لانتفاء غاية الخلاف. وإنما اعتبر قيد الحيثية إذ لا بد من اعتباره. فإنهما قد يختلفان بالذات مع التضاد بينهما كالسواد والبياض، فالحركة من الأول إلى الثاني تضاد الحركة من الثاني إلى الأول لأن مبدآهما متضادان بالذات وكذلك منتهاهما. أو دونه كالسواد والحمرة فإنهما متخالفان بالماهية بلا تضاد لعدم التباعد في الغاية فلا تضاد أيضاً بين الحركة من أحدهما إلى الآخر وعكسها. أو بالعرض أي يختلفان لا بالذات بل باعتبار عارض مع التضاد بحسبه أيضاً كالمركز والمحيط لأنهما جزآن من جسم بسيط عرض لأحدهما أنه غاية القرب من المحيط وللآخر أنه غاية البعد عنه. وباعتبار هذين العارضين صارا متضادين مع تساويهما في الفلك.
Rather, the opposition of motions is due to the direction from the extremities and to them, meaning according to what they are from and what they are to, both together, inasmuch as they are such. And it is not sufficient for the opposition of motion the opposition of the two beginnings only, for the motion from blackness to redness does not oppose the motion from whiteness to redness. Nor is the opposition between the two endpoints sufficient, for the motion from redness to whiteness does not oppose the motion from redness to blackness. That is due to the absence of extreme difference. And the qualification of 'inasmuch as' is considered, for it must be considered. For they (the 'from what' and 'to what') may differ essentially with opposition between them, like blackness and whiteness; thus the motion from the first to the second opposes the motion from the second to the first because their beginnings are essentially opposite, and likewise their endpoints. Or below that (i.e., below opposition), like blackness and redness, for they differ in essence without opposition due to lack of distance in extremity, so there is no opposition between the motion from one to the other and its reverse. Or accidentally, i.e., they differ not essentially but by an accidental attribute, with opposition according to that attribute as well, like the center and the circumference, for they are two points of a simple body; one happens to be the extreme proximity to the circumference and the other the extreme distance from it. And by virtue of these two accidental attributes they become opposite, while being equal in the sphere.
Source pages p. 248

Commentary
This passage defines the proper condition for two motions to be opposite. It first states that opposition is due to the direction both from and to the extremities, not just one. Using examples, it shows that if only the beginnings are opposite (e.g., blackness and whiteness but same end redness), the motions are not opposite. Similarly, if only the ends are opposite but beginnings same, they are not opposite. The key is 'extreme difference' (ghayat al-khilaf). Then it classifies the relation between the origin and destination: they may be essentially opposite (e.g., black and white), and then motions from one to the other and back are opposite. They may be different but not opposite (e.g., black and red), then motions between them are not opposite. They may be opposite accidentally, like center and circumference, which are not essentially opposite but become opposite due to accidental attributes (extreme proximity/distance). In all cases, the condition for motion opposition is that the 'from what' and 'to what' of each motion are opposed to each other in the same way (essentially or accidentally).

Key Terms
تضاد
مبدأ
منتهى
بالذات
بالعرض
غاية الخلاف

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that because black and white are opposite, any motion involving them in any role will produce opposite motions.
Correction: The passage clarifies that both the beginning and end must be appropriately paired; e.g., motion from black to red and from white to red are not opposite because the endpoint is the same.

Checkpoint
Why is the motion from blackness to redness not opposite to the motion from whiteness to redness?
A) Because redness is common to both motions.
B) Because blackness and whiteness are opposite, but the endpoints are the same, lacking extreme difference in both beginning and end.
C) Because the motions are in opposite directions.
D) Because the moving object is different.

Key Takeaway
True opposition in motion requires that both the origin and destination are opposite in the same way (essentially or accidentally), not just one of them.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In your daily judgments, do you often assume that if two things are opposite in one aspect, they must be opposite in all? Think of a recent example where you conflated opposition of endpoints with opposition of whole motions. How does this lesson correct that error in your reasoning? Will you commit to examining both origins and destinations before labeling something as 'opposite'?
?
The passage distinguishes between essential and accidental opposition. In your spiritual life, have you mistaken accidental differences (like habits or appearances) for essential opposition, causing unnecessary division? Or have you ignored accidental opposition when it matters? How will this lesson sharpen your discernment between what is truly opposed and what is merely different?

Motion as Accidental Quantity and Its Three Divisions
Defines motion as accidental quantity and enumerates three types of division.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why motion is considered an accidental quantity and how it is divided into three categories: by distance, by time, and by the moving body.

Core Idea
Motion is not a quantity in itself; it becomes quantifiable accidentally through distance, time, and the body.

This passage from the Ninth Topic clarifies the nature of motion. Although motion belongs to the relational categories, it accidentally takes on quantitative aspects. The author then lists three distinct ways motion can be divided, each based on a different aspect: distance, time, and the moving body. Understanding these divisions helps clarify how motion relates to measurement and change.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Begin by reading the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Notice the structure: a definition followed by an enumeration of three divisions.
The passage uses technical terms like كم بالعرض (accidental quantity).
2
Identify the three divisions
The passage lists three types of division: according to distance, according to time, and according to the moving body. Locate each in the text and note how each is described.
The first two are illustrated with examples (half distance, half time).
3
Understand the difference between division by distance and time
The author emphasizes that division by time is distinct from division by distance, giving the example of fast and slow motion. Consider how two motions can be equal in distance but differ in time, and vice versa.
This shows that motion can be quantified in multiple independent ways.
4
Examine division by the moving body
The third division is based on the body's parts. When a body moves, its parts conceptually move, and if the body separates, each part has actual motion. This is compared to blackness inhering in a body.
Motion follows its subject in both conceptual and actual division.
5
Reflect on the accidental nature of motion's quantity
Conclude by understanding why motion is not a quantity by essence but accidentally. It belongs to the relational categories (like 'where'), yet it can be measured in these three ways.
This distinction is crucial in kalam for classifying attributes.

المقصد التاسع : الحركة ليست كماً بل عرضية ( الحركة ليست كماً بالذات فإنها من المقولات النسبية لا من مقولة الكم (بل) هي كم ( بالعرض ويعرض لها بسبب الكمية العرضية ( ثلاثة أنواع من الانقسام : الأول بحسب المسافة لانطباقهما ) فإن الحركة الأينية منطبقة على المسافة كأنها حالة فيها والمسافة منقسمة لانتفاء الجزء الذي لا يتجزأ فتنقسم الحركة بانقسامها ( فالحركة إلى نصفها نصف الحركة إلى كلها الثاني بحسب الزمان لأنه عارض لها فينقسم بانقسام عارضها ( فالحركة في نصف ساعة نصف الحركة في ساعة وهذا الانقسام الثابت للحركة بحسب الزمان ( غير ) الانقسام ( الذي بحسب المسافة إذ قد يختلفان كالسريعة والبطيئة ) فإنه إذا فرض اتحادهما في المسافة والانقسام بحسبها، فلا بد أن يختلف زمانهما والانقسام بحسبه، وإذا فرض اتحادهما في الزمان والانقسام بحسبه كانتا مختلفتين في المسافة والانقسام بحسبها ( الثالث : بحسب المتحرك فإن الجسم هو المتحرك وهو قابل للقسمة ولا شبهة في أنه ( إذا تحرك ) الجسم ( تحركت أجزاؤه المفروضة فيه والحركة القائمة بكل جزء غير القائمة بالآخر) فقد انقسمت الحركة أيضاً انقساماً فرضياً كمحلها ( فإذا عرض له) أي للجسم (انفصال خارجي ( حصل لكل جزء حركة بالفعل) فالحركة تابعة لمحلها في الانقسام الفرضي والفعلي الخارجي كالسواد القائم بالجسم فإنه يتبعه في هذين الانقسامين، وقد نبهناك على أن الانقسام بحسب المسافة إنما يتصور في الحركة الأينية بناء على أن الأين لكونه عبارة عن الحصول في المكان. قوله : ( أموراً موجودة إلخ ) فإذا اعتبر تمام الدور فلا وجود لها بالفعل وإذا اعتبر العكس فلا تضاد بينهما . قوله : ( إنما يتصور في الحركة الأينية ) بناء على أن الأين لكونه عبارة عن الحصول في المكان.
The Ninth Topic: Motion is not a quantity but an accident. Motion is not a quantity in essence, for it belongs to the relational categories, not to the category of quantity. Rather, it is a quantity accidentally, and there occur to it, due to accidental quantity, three types of division: First, according to distance, because of their correspondence. For locative motion corresponds to distance as if it is inherent in it, and distance is divisible due to the denial of the indivisible part, so motion divides by its division. So motion to half of it is half of motion to its whole. Second, according to time, because it is an accident to it, so it divides by the division of its accident. So motion in half an hour is half of motion in an hour. And this division established for motion according to time is other than the division according to distance, for they may differ, like fast and slow. For if the two are assumed to be united in distance and division according to it, then their times and division according to it must differ. And if they are assumed to be united in time and division according to it, then they differ in distance and division according to it. Third, according to the moving body, for the body is the moving thing and it is divisible, and there is no doubt that if the body moves, its assumed parts move, and the motion subsisting in each part is other than that subsisting in the other. So motion has also divided by a conceptual division like its subject. Then if an external separation occurs to it, i.e., to the body, then for each part an actual motion obtains. So motion follows its subject in conceptual and actual external division, like blackness subsisting in the body, for it follows it in these two divisions. And we have alerted you that division according to distance is only conceivable in locative motion, based on the fact that 'where' (ayn), since it is an expression for being in place. His statement: (existent things, etc.) So if the complete revolution is considered, then there is no actual existence for them, and if the opposite is considered, then there is no opposition between them. His statement: (It is only conceivable in locative motion) based on the fact that 'where', since it is an expression for being in place.
Source pages p. 257

Commentary
This passage is a definition and classification. It begins the Ninth Topic, stating that motion is not a quantity by essence; rather, it belongs to the relational categories (المقولات النسبية) such as 'where' (الأين). But it accidentally becomes a quantity (كم بالعرض) because it can be measured by distance, time, and the body. The author then enumerates three types of division: (1) by distance: motion divides as distance divides because locative motion corresponds to distance; (2) by time: motion divides as time divides, but this division is distinct from division by distance, as exemplified by fast and slow motions; (3) by the moving body: when a body moves, its parts conceptually move, and if the body actually separates, each part has its own actual motion. Motion follows its subject in both conceptual and actual division, like blackness. The author notes that division by distance only applies to locative motion, since 'where' (الأين) means being in place. The marginal comment adds a clarification about the earlier discussion: if the complete revolution is considered, the termini are not actual, so no opposition; division by distance only applies to locative motion.

?

Refuting Slowness as Solely Intercalating Rests
The author refutes slowness as solely from intercalating rests using a horse example.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why slowness cannot be reduced to intercalating rests alone, based on a reductio ad absurdum argument that shows the absurd consequence of such a view.

Core Idea
If slowness were only rests, even fast motion would be imperceptible—an absurdity.

In this passage, the author tackles a misconception about the nature of slowness. Some attribute slowness entirely to pauses between movements. Using a concrete example of a horse and a gnomon, he demonstrates that this view leads to a contradiction. Prepare to follow a logical reductio showing why the claim fails.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then the English translation. Let the argument's flow sink in before interpreting.
Notice the dense style and parenthetical inserts; they clarify logical connections.
2
Identify the thesis and reductio structure
Find where the author states the thesis (slowness is not solely from rests) and the starting assumption for the reductio (if slowness were only from rests then...).
The reductio begins with 'وإلا لم يحس...'
3
Analyze the horse-gnomon example
Understand why the horse's motion would be slower than the gnomon's according to the assumption, and how the number of rests would be huge, overwhelming the actual motions.
The gnomon covers a quarter circle in the same time, a vastly greater distance.
4
Grasp the absurd consequence and its refutation
See that if the horse had so many intercalating rests, its motion would be imperceptible—contradicting our sensory experience. Thus the original assumption is false.
The author then links this to the debate on indivisible parts.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الرابع - المقصد الحادي عشر : الحركة وقياسها بالسرعة والزمن

تقطع ( الأقل) من المسافة (في) الزمان (المساوي) وربما قطعت مسافة أقل في زمان الأكثر لكنه غير شامل لها ) وليس البطء ) أي ليس كل بطء ( لتخلل السكنات ) بين الحركات ) وإلا لم يحس بحركة الفرس ) وإن فرضت سريعة جداً ( واللازم بطلانه ظاهر بيان الملازمة أن البطء لو لم يكن إلا لتخلل السكنات ) فيما بين الحركات كان تفاوت السرعة والبطء بحسب تفاوت السكنات المتخللة) في القلة والكثرة ( فإذا) عدا فرس أشد عدو كما إذا قدر أنه عدا من أول اليوم إلى منتصفه خمسين فرسخاً ) كان حركته هذه ( أبطأ من حركة المحدد بنسبة غير قليلة) لأنها قطعت في المدة المذكورة ربع الدور وهو زائد على مسافة حركة الفرس بما لا يحيط الوهم به ( ويكون ) حينئذ ( زيادة سكناته ( أي سكنات الفرس ( على حركاته كزيادة حركة المحدد على حركاته ( لأن عدد سكناته يساوي عدد زيادات حركة المحدد لا محالة ( وأنه ( أي زيادة حركة المحدد على حركاته ) ألف ألف مرة فتكون زيادة سكناته على حركاته أيضاً ألف ألف مرة ( فلا تظهر تلك الحركات القليلة في تلك السكنات الكثيرة مثل هذه الكثرة الغامرة لتلك القليلة فوجب الحركة أصلاً وهو باطل قطعاً ، لأنا نحس بحركاته ولا نحس بشيء من سكناته ( واعلم أن دلائل إبطال الجزء المبنية على تلازم الحركتين المتخالفتين بالسرعة والبطء، وهي قوله : ( بنسبة غير قابلة أي بنسبة لا يمكن توصيفه بالقلة لأنه فرع إحاطة الوهم بتلك النسبة .
أن لا بهذه يحس تسليم انتفاء لزوم الجزء، فحينئذ إذا فرضت تلك الزيادة تمام مسافة السريعة لم تقطع البطيئة في الزمان المساوي أقل من تلك المسافة، فلم يكن قطع الأقل في الزمان المساوي خاصة شاملة للبطيئة، بل ولا قطع السريعة في الزمان المساوي مسافة أكثر خاصة شاملة للسريعة أيضاً كما لا يخفى اللهم إلا أن يقال تلك المقدار من المسافة لا يقطعها إلا بطيئة لا يتصور أبطأ منها حتى تكون هي أسرع بالنسبة إليه، ففرضها تمام مسافة السريعة فرض لا يمكن مطابقته للواقع وقد يجاب عن البحث بأن سريعاً إذا قطع في جزئين من الزمان غير منقسمين خارجاً مسافة، فالبطيء يقطع في ذلك الزمان مسافة أقصر فليفرض أنها نصف مسافة السريعة، فالزمان القصير هاهنا هو الجزء الواحد فقط، والسريع يقطع فيه مسافة مساوية فقط، وأنت خبير بأن كون الزمان القصير هو الجزء الواحد فقط لا يلائم أصول الفلاسفة كما مرت إليه الإشارة من الشارح في بحث الخلاء. قوله: (لأنا نحس بحركاته ولا نحس بشيء من سكناته وقد يجاب بأن السكون عندنا عدمي فلا نحس به والحركة وجودية فلذا نحس بها وفيه نظر إذ قد سبق أن السكون محسوس بالتبع وبالجملة قد يدرك بالحس عمى زيد واقطعيته، وليس أبعد منه أن يدرك حينئذ كون الفرس التي يعدو أشد عدواً في الزمان المتطاول في مكان واحد .
The fourth observatory - the eleventh topic: Motion and its measurement by speed and time. (The less) of distance is traversed (in) equal time, and perhaps a lesser distance is traversed in a greater time, but it is not inclusive of it. And slowness is not (i.e., not every slowness) due to intercalating rests between movements; otherwise, the motion of a horse would not be perceived, even if it were very fast. The necessary (consequence) is clearly false. The explanation of the entailment: if slowness were only from intercalating rests between movements, then the difference in speed and slowness would be according to the difference in the intercalating rests in fewness and multiplicity. Thus, if a horse runs very fast, as if it is assumed that it runs from the beginning of the day to its midpoint fifty farsakhs, this motion of it would be slower than the motion of the gnomon by a not small ratio, because it traversed in the mentioned period a quarter of the circle, which exceeds the distance of the horse's motion by something that imagination cannot encompass. Then the increase of its rests (i.e., the horse's rests) over its motions would be like the increase of the gnomon's motion over its motions, because the number of its rests necessarily equals the number of increments of the gnomon's motion, and that (i.e., the increase of the gnomon's motion over its motions) is a thousand thousand times, so the increase of its rests over its motions would also be a thousand thousand times. Thus, those few motions would not appear amidst those many rests, such that this overwhelming multitude would submerge those few, so motion would become necessary (i.e., impossible), which is absolutely false, because we perceive its motions and do not perceive any of its rests. And know that the proofs for invalidating the indivisible part, built upon the mutual entailment of two motions differing in speed and slowness, and they are: the saying: (by a ratio that is not small) i.e., a ratio that cannot be described as small because it is a branch of imagination encompassing that ratio. That not with this [؟] is perceived, granting the denial of the entailment of the part, so then if that increase is assumed to be the entire distance of the fast (mover), the slow (mover) would not traverse in equal time less than that distance, so the traversal of less in equal time would not be a property covering the slow, nor would the traversal of the fast in equal time of a greater distance be a property covering the fast, as is not hidden; unless it is said that that amount of distance is only traversed by a slow (mover) than which no slower is conceivable, so that it would be faster relative to it, so its assumption as the entire distance of the fast is an assumption impossible to correspond to reality. And it may be answered to the inquiry that if a fast (mover) traverses in two indivisible parts of time a distance, then the slow (mover) traverses in that time a shorter distance, so let it be assumed that it is half the distance of the fast, then the short time here is only the one part, and the fast traverses in it only an equal distance, and you are aware that the fact that the short time is only one part contradicts the principles of the philosophers, as the commentator alluded in the discussion of void. His words: (For we perceive its motions and do not perceive any of its rests). And it may be answered that rest according to us is privative, so we do not perceive it, and motion is positive, so we perceive it. But there is a consideration in this, since it has preceded that rest is perceived secondarily. In short, it may be perceived by sense—the blindness of Zayd and his muteness—and nothing is more far-fetched than that one would then perceive the horse that runs very fast in prolonged time in one place.
Source pages p. 261

Commentary
This passage refutes the claim that slowness arises only from intercalating rests between movements. The author begins by stating the thesis: 'not every slowness is due to intercalating rests.' The proof is a reductio ad absurdum. If slowness were solely from rests, then variations in speed would depend on the number of rests. The author then offers a concrete example: a horse that runs fifty farsakhs from dawn to noon, and the motion of a gnomon (a sundial pointer) that covers a quarter of a circle in the same time. The horse's motion is far slower. According to the assumption, the horse's slowness must be due to many rests, and the number of rests would equal the number of incremental motions of the gnomon—a vast number. But if the horse has so many rests, its few actual motions would be overwhelmed, making motion essentially impossible. Yet we do perceive the horse moving, so the assumption is false. The author then relates this to the debate on the indivisible part (al-juzʼ), noting that the proof relies on the mutual entailment of two motions differing in speed. A counter-argument is considered: if the slow mover's distance is assumed to be exactly the same as the fast mover's, the initial principle (that the slower covers less in equal time) would fail. The author responds that this assumption is impossible in reality. Another objection about the perception of rest (that rest is privative and imperceptible) is answered by noting that rest is perceived secondarily, e.g., we perceive blindness. The passage maintains that the original proof stands: slowness is not necessarily caused by intercalating rests.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that slowness always involves intercalating rests.
Correction: The author shows that if that were true, even slow motions would be imperceptible, which is false. Thus slowness can have other causes.

Checkpoint
What is the author's purpose in stating that if slowness were only due to intercalating rests, we would not perceive the horse's motion?
A) To prove that the horse is actually fast
B) To show that the consequence is absurd
C) To show that rests are not perceptual
D) To prove that speed is relative

Key Takeaway
Slowness cannot be solely caused by intercalating rests, as the reductio example shows that such a view leads to the absurd conclusion that slow motion would be imperceptible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You often measure your spiritual progress by dramatic leaps, but the horse’s motion is real even if slow. Where in your life have you dismissed your own small, steady movements as 'nothing' because they are not as fast as someone else’s? What one tiny motion can you acknowledge today as real worship?
?
The argument shows that if slowness were all rests, motion would vanish. Consider your own heart: do you treat your spiritual slowness as if it were just 'rests'—excuses, laziness, distractions—and forget that even a slow movement is still movement? Which 'rest' have you allowed to define you, and what will you do to replace it with a small, real motion?
?
The horse’s motion is perceived despite being vastly slower than the gnomon. In your private self-examination, do you genuinely perceive the movements of your soul toward Allah, or have you become numb, only noticing the rests? What would it take for you to feel the reality of your own spiritual motion right now, and what will you do to keep it perceptible?

The Shadow Argument for Slowness Without Rests
The author presents a proof from the motion of a shadow to show slowness without rests.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the motion of a shadow demonstrates that slowness can occur without any intervening rests, and how the argument is defended against a potential quibble.

Core Idea
Slowness does not require rests; the shadow's motion proves it.

Can something be slower without ever pausing? The Ashʿarite theologian argues yes, using a simple everyday example: the shadow of a stick as the sun rises. As the sun climbs, the shadow shortens, but not at the same rate. This lesson unpacks that proof and shows how the possibility of slowness without rests is established.

Guided Path
1
Understand the setup
Imagine a stick planted vertically. As the sun rises from the east, the shadow on the ground is long and gradually shortens. The sun's motion is continuous, but how does the shadow behave?
The shadow is an accident (ʿaraḍ), not a body, so motion is attributed metaphorically.
2
Consider two possibilities
The author presents two scenarios: either the shadow stops moving for some increments of the sun's rise, or it moves for every increment. Both are examined logically.
3
First possibility: shadow stops
If the shadow could remain still during one increment, it could remain still for multiple increments, leading to the absurdity that the sun completes a full revolution while the shadow never moves. This is false.
This absurdity shows that the shadow cannot stop at all if it ever stops.
4
Second possibility: shadow moves each time
If the shadow does move for each increment, its motion must be smaller in magnitude than the sun's motion, so it is slower—yet without any rest in between. Thus slowness without rests is proven.
5
Refute the quibble
An opponent might argue: if it's possible for the shadow to stop for one increment, it should be possible for all, leading to absurdity. The author replies that such a scenario is indeed possible in itself (by divine power), but custom prevents it. So the argument stands.
This reply relies on Ashʿarite occasionalism.

The fourth observatory - the eleventh topic: Motion and its measurement by speed and time. Six (as its turn will come to it, i.e., to its mention) indicate the falsity of this, meaning that slowness is confined to the intercalation of rests, so it is permissible to use them as evidence here. (In short, this inquiry)—that is, slowness being due to intercalation—is based on the inquiry of the part and is a branch of its branches, and its correctness or falsehood revolves with it. Among them (i.e., among those six proofs) is that if we plant a stick in the ground, then when the sun is on its eastern horizon, the shadow falls on the western side, long, and it continues to decrease (the shadow) according to the increase of the sun's altitude until the sun reaches its maximum altitude; and (whenever it rises), i.e., if the sun rises by an amount, (if the shadow stops and does not decrease at all), that (would be) permissible in the second and third, then it would be permissible that the sun completes the revolution while the shadow remains in its state, which is false. And if the shadow moves by a part whenever the sun moves by a part, it is possible that these two parts are equal in magnitude, or that the part of the shadow is larger, but rather it must be smaller; then corresponding to each motion of the sun (upward) there is a motion of the shadow (toward decrease) that is less in magnitude, so the motion of the shadow is slower without intercalation of rest. So it is established that speed and slowness can occur without intercalation of rests. And a quibble can be raised regarding their saying: if it were permissible for the sun to move by a part while the shadow remains in its state, it would be permissible in all; and if so, it would be permissible for the sun to complete the revolution while the shadow remains in its state. Then that (i.e., the completion of the revolution with the shadow remaining in its state) is permissible in our view, because all existents are dependent upon Him, may He be exalted, from the beginning without necessity or obligation, and custom is what judges its non-occurrence (i.e., the non-occurrence of this state, meaning the shadow remaining in its state while the sun completes the revolution). His words: (If it rises, etc.) is an indication that 'whenever' (kullamā) is not used correctly here because the consequent does not follow from it, and the answer is that the word 'if' (idhā) is different. His words: (Because all existents) contains taking something irrelevant, namely 'without necessity or obligation,' and leaving what is relevant, namely the condition 'from the beginning without depending on anything,' for the permissibility of disconnection between the two motions is based on that. His words: (And custom is what judges, etc.) is an explanation of the source of the illusion of impossibility, that it arises from the course of custom. His words: (It is based on the inquiry of the part; whoever affirms the part says it is correct, and whoever denies it says it is false). His words: (That is, if it rises, interpret the form of the measure? I mean, 'whenever' (kullamā) is accompanied by negligence, and it is 'if' (idhā) so that it is not corrected by his saying 'that is permissible in the second and third,' because if it were left on its apparent meaning, the stopping would enter in the second and third in the mentioned assumption. His words: (And if the shadow moves, etc.) The attribution of motion and rest to the shadow is metaphorical because they are properties of bodies, and the shadow is an accident because it is one of the degrees of light, as has preceded. His words: (And a quibble can be raised, etc.) means the quibble is in the falsity of the consequent, not in the entailment, as the expression might suggest; so what is meant is a quibble regarding the entire conditional proof.
Source pages p. 262

Commentary
This passage presents one of six proofs that slowness is not confined to intercalating rests. The proof uses a stick planted vertically: as the sun rises, the shadow on the ground shortens. The author considers two possibilities. First, if for each increment of the sun's altitude the shadow were to stop and not decrease, then it could remain stationary for several increments, ultimately allowing the sun to complete a full circle while the shadow never moves—an obvious absurdity. Second, if the shadow does move for each increment, its part must be smaller than the sun's part, so the shadow's motion is slower than the sun's motion, yet without any intervening rest. This establishes that slowness can occur without any rests. The author then addresses a potential quibble (mudāyaqah): if it is possible for the shadow to remain still for one increment, it would be possible for all, leading to the absurdity again. The author counters that such a scenario is indeed possible in our view (i.e., among Ashʿarites) because all things are directly dependent on God without intrinsic necessity; custom alone prevents it, not impossibility. The marginal notes clarify that the word 'kullamā' (whenever) is incorrect here; the proper word is 'idhā' (if). They also stress that the proof relies on the existence of indivisible parts (juzʼ) and that the attribution of motion and shadow is figurative. This proof successfully refutes the claim that slowness always involves rests.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Slowness always requires intercalated rests (sakanāt).
Correction: The shadow argument shows that a slower motion can occur without any rests; the shadow's motion is slower than the sun's yet continuous.

Checkpoint
What does the shadow argument conclude about the possibility of the shadow's motion being slower without rests?
A) It is impossible
B) It is possible and shows slowness can occur without rests
C) It is due to the indivisible part
D) It is due to God's habit

Key Takeaway
By analyzing the relative motion of sun and shadow, the proof establishes that slowness is possible without any intervening rests, relying on the Ashʿarite principle of direct divine causation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you experience a slow moment in your life—whether in difficulty, waiting, or routine—do you truly believe that it is not a pause in Allah's decree but a continuous act of His creation? How would your awareness change if you saw even the slightest motion as a direct creation of Allah, requiring no rest?
?
The shadow argument shows that what appears slow is not due to stops but to a smaller share of movement from Allah. Have you ever felt that your spiritual life is 'slow' or stagnant? Could it be that you are actually moving, but at a pace that requires you to see each moment as a direct gift from Allah, rather than expecting leaps?
?
If slowness does not require rests, then every fraction of a moment is filled with Allah's action. When you are impatient or frustrated with slow progress, do you reflect that every instant is full of His will? What would change if you thanked Him for even the smallest increments of movement in your life?

Defining Real Priority and Essential Dependence
Philosophers define real priority and classify essential dependence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of real priority and the concept of essential dependence in the philosophers' classification.

Core Idea
Real priority means the posterior depends on the prior without reciprocity.

The philosophers developed a detailed classification of priority to avoid circularity and clarify causal relationships. This lesson introduces the foundational distinction between real and mental priority, focusing on essential dependence where the very essence of the posterior requires the prior.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Notice how the philosophers begin by distinguishing real priority from mental priority.
2
Identify the key condition
The passage states that real priority must involve dependence of the posterior on the prior without reciprocity (no circularity). Highlight this condition.
Without this condition, real priority would not be meaningful.
3
Understand essential dependence
Essential dependence means the posterior's essence is only through the prior's essence, as with the numbers two and one. Reflect on why this is not temporal.
This is a logical, not temporal, priority.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وربما تكلف الحكماء للحصر أي حصر التقدم في الأنواع الخمسة (وجهاً) ليس حصراً عقلياً دائراً بين النفي والإثبات بل هو نوع ضبط للحصر الاستقرائي (فقالوا: التقدم إما أن يكون حقيقياً أو اعتبارياً، والأول لا بد فيه من توقف للمتأخر على المتقدم) إذ لو لم يتوقف عليه أصلاً لم يكن هناك تقدم حقيقي قطعاً ( من غير عكس) لئلا يلزم الدور ( فالمتوقف إما ) أن يكون توقفه ( بحسب الذات ) وذلك بأن لا ذات المتأخر إلا بذات المتقدم كما مر في الاثنين والواحد وهو التقدم بالذات (وإما) أن يكون توقفه ( بحسب الوجود) دون الذات بأن يتوقف وجود المتأخر على وجود المتقدم لا ذاته على ذاته، وذلك على قسمين لأنه إما أن يكون ( مع اشتراطه ) أي اشتراط وجود المتأخر ( بالعدم الطارئ عليه ) أي على المتقدم ( أم لا فالأول هو التقدم الزماني لأن وجود المتأخر من أجزاء الزمان متوقف على وجود المتقدم منها وعلى عدمه الطارئ عليه فإن المتقدم منها ما لم يوجد ولم يعدم بعد وجوده لم يتصور وجود المتأخر منها، وأما الزمانيات فقد عرفت أن تقدمها راجع إلى تقدم زمانها فلا يكون المتقدم منها من حيث هو متقدم مجامعاً للمتأخر، والثاني : وهو أن لا يشترط وجود المتأخر بالعدم الطارئ على المتقدم بل يتوقف وجوده على وجوده فقط، هو التقدم بالعلية المتناول لتقدم المؤثر التام، وتقدم العلل الناقصة سوى أجزاء المعلول.
قوله : (حقيقياً) لا يتبدل بالاعتبار.
قوله : ( إذ لو لم يتوقف إلخ لا يخفى ما فيه من المصادرة إلا أن يقال : المدعي أورد بعبارة أظهر من الأول فجعل علة له باعتبار الظهور .
قوله : لأن وجود المتأخر إلخ فيه أن الزمان متصل واحد لا جزء له بالفعل حتى يتصور فيه توقف وجود المتأخر على وجود المتقدم. غاية ما يقال : إنه بعد فرض القسمة وحصول الأجزاء بحكم الوهم بأنه لو كانت الأجزاء موجودة في الخارج توقف وجود المتأخر على وجود المتقدم بحيث لا يحتمعان، أما التوقف وإن اعتبر الشخص الوهمي ،فكلا والوجه أن يقال : ليس التوقف بمعنى الاحتياج بل حصول شيء بعد شيء مترتباً عليه سواء وجد الاحتياج أو لا .
قوله : ( وتقدم العلل الناقصة هذا على المشهور.
And perhaps the philosophers exerted themselves for the enumeration (i.e., enumeration of priority into five types) in a way (not a rational enumeration that turns between negation and affirmation, but rather a type of inductive enumeration). They said: Priority is either real or mental. The first (real) must involve the posterior depending on the prior because if it did not depend on it at all, there would certainly be no real priority, without reciprocity lest circularity occur. The dependent (posterior) is either dependent in essence—such that the essence of the posterior is only through the essence of the prior, as mentioned regarding two and one, and this is essential priority—or dependent in existence without essence, meaning the existence of the posterior depends on the existence of the prior, not its essence on its essence. That is of two types: either conditioned by the non-existence occurring to the prior or not. The first is temporal priority, because the existence of the posterior from the parts of time depends on the existence of the prior parts and on the non-existence occurring to it; the prior part is such that if it does not exist and its non-existence after its existence does not occur, the existence of the posterior part is inconceivable. As for temporal things, you have known that their priority reduces to the priority of their time, so the prior among them, insofar as it is prior, does not coexist with the posterior. The second (type) is when the existence of the posterior is not conditioned by the non-existence occurring to the prior, but rather its existence depends only on the existence of the prior—this is causal priority, encompassing the priority of the complete efficient cause and the priority of incomplete causes except the parts of the effect. His saying: (Real) does not change by consideration. His saying: (Because if it did not depend, etc.) it is not hidden what it contains of begging the question, except that one may say: The claimant presented it in a more apparent expression than the first, so he made it a cause for it by way of apparentness. His saying: (Because the existence of the posterior, etc.) contains that time is a single continuum not actually divided, so dependence of the posterior's existence on the prior's existence is inconceivable. What can be said is that after assuming division and obtaining parts by virtue of estimation, as if parts existed externally, the existence of the posterior depends on the existence of the prior such that they do not coexist. As for the dependence, even if considered by the estimative person, it is [not]? The correct view is that dependence is not in the sense of need but of one thing occurring after another in an order, whether there is need or not. His saying: (And the priority of incomplete causes) this is according to the well-known view.
Source pages p. 282

Commentary
This passage defines and classifies real priority (al-taqaddum al-haqiqi) as the philosophers' inductive enumeration. It first distinguishes real priority from mental priority. Real priority necessarily involves the posterior depending on the prior without the prior depending on the posterior (to avoid circularity). The dependence is then divided into two kinds: (1) essential dependence (bi-ḥasab al-dhāt), where the essence of the posterior is only through the essence of the prior, as in the relationship between two and one; (2) existential dependence (bi-ḥasab al-wujūd), where only the existence of the posterior depends on the existence of the prior, not its essence. Existential dependence is further subdivided into two: (a) temporal priority, where the posterior's existence is conditioned by the prior's non-existence after its existence (e.g., parts of time); (b) causal priority, where the posterior's existence depends solely on the prior's existence without the condition of non-existence (e.g., the complete cause and incomplete causes other than the parts of the effect). The marginal notes clarify issues: 'real' (ḥaqīqī) is not changed by consideration; the argument against circularity may appear circular itself but is defended as being a more apparent expression; the objection about time being indivisible is answered by considering estimative division; and the mention of incomplete causes is said to be according to the well-known view (al-mashhūr). Overall, the passage provides a detailed logical framework for understanding how one thing can be prior to another in a real sense.

Key Terms
تقدم حقيقي
تقدم ذاتي
توقف
دور

Checkpoint
What is the key condition for real priority according to the passage?
A) The posterior must exist after the prior in time.
B) The posterior must depend on the prior without the prior depending on the posterior.
C) The prior must be a complete cause.
D) The posterior must be mentally dependent on the prior.

Key Takeaway
Real priority requires one-way dependence of the posterior on the prior, and essential priority is one type where the essence itself depends.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In your own life, do you ever treat mental priorities as real ones (e.g., thinking that your own plans are the foundation of your day)? How does this lesson challenge you to reorder your actual dependencies back to Allah?
?
If everything other than Allah is essentially dependent on Him, does your daily awareness reflect that? What would change if you let that truth shape your fears and hopes starting tomorrow?

Existential Dependence: Temporal and Causal Priority
Philosophers classify existential dependence into temporal and causal priority.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how existential dependence is subdivided into temporal priority and causal priority.

Core Idea
Existential dependence has two forms: temporal (conditioned by the prior's non-existence) and causal (not so conditioned).

After defining essential priority, the passage turns to existential dependence, where the posterior's existence depends on the prior's existence, not its essence. This lesson focuses on the two subdivisions: temporal priority, which involves the prior's non-existence, and causal priority, which does not. You will also learn the definition of an incomplete cause.

Guided Path
1
Read the rest of the passage
Read from 'وإما بحسب الوجود' to the end of the main classification. Notice the two conditions: with and without the prior's non-existence.
2
Distinguish temporal and causal priority
Temporal priority requires that the prior ceases to exist for the posterior to exist (e.g., parts of time). Causal priority does not have this condition; the posterior depends solely on the prior's existence.
The complete efficient cause is an example of causal priority.
3
Understand the exception for incomplete causes
The passage states that causal priority includes incomplete causes except the parts of the effect. Think about why those are excluded.
Incomplete causes are part of the effect in some way, so their priority is different.

And perhaps the philosophers exerted themselves for the enumeration (i.e., enumeration of priority into five types) in a way (not a rational enumeration that turns between negation and affirmation, but rather a type of inductive enumeration). They said: Priority is either real or mental. The first (real) must involve the posterior depending on the prior because if it did not depend on it at all, there would certainly be no real priority, without reciprocity lest circularity occur. The dependent (posterior) is either dependent in essence—such that the essence of the posterior is only through the essence of the prior, as mentioned regarding two and one, and this is essential priority—or dependent in existence without essence, meaning the existence of the posterior depends on the existence of the prior, not its essence on its essence. That is of two types: either conditioned by the non-existence occurring to the prior or not. The first is temporal priority, because the existence of the posterior from the parts of time depends on the existence of the prior parts and on the non-existence occurring to it; the prior part is such that if it does not exist and its non-existence after its existence does not occur, the existence of the posterior part is inconceivable. As for temporal things, you have known that their priority reduces to the priority of their time, so the prior among them, insofar as it is prior, does not coexist with the posterior. The second (type) is when the existence of the posterior is not conditioned by the non-existence occurring to the prior, but rather its existence depends only on the existence of the prior—this is causal priority, encompassing the priority of the complete efficient cause and the priority of incomplete causes except the parts of the effect. His saying: (Real) does not change by consideration. His saying: (Because if it did not depend, etc.) it is not hidden what it contains of begging the question, except that one may say: The claimant presented it in a more apparent expression than the first, so he made it a cause for it by way of apparentness. His saying: (Because the existence of the posterior, etc.) contains that time is a single continuum not actually divided, so dependence of the posterior's existence on the prior's existence is inconceivable. What can be said is that after assuming division and obtaining parts by virtue of estimation, as if parts existed externally, the existence of the posterior depends on the existence of the prior such that they do not coexist. As for the dependence, even if considered by the estimative person, it is [not]? The correct view is that dependence is not in the sense of need but of one thing occurring after another in an order, whether there is need or not. His saying: (And the priority of incomplete causes) this is according to the well-known view.
Source pages p. 282

Commentary
This passage defines and classifies real priority (al-taqaddum al-haqiqi) as the philosophers' inductive enumeration. It first distinguishes real priority from mental priority. Real priority necessarily involves the posterior depending on the prior without the prior depending on the posterior (to avoid circularity). The dependence is then divided into two kinds: (1) essential dependence (bi-ḥasab al-dhāt), where the essence of the posterior is only through the essence of the prior, as in the relationship between two and one; (2) existential dependence (bi-ḥasab al-wujūd), where only the existence of the posterior depends on the existence of the prior, not its essence. Existential dependence is further subdivided into two: (a) temporal priority, where the posterior's existence is conditioned by the prior's non-existence after its existence (e.g., parts of time); (b) causal priority, where the posterior's existence depends solely on the prior's existence without the condition of non-existence (e.g., the complete cause and incomplete causes other than the parts of the effect). The marginal notes clarify issues: 'real' (ḥaqīqī) is not changed by consideration; the argument against circularity may appear circular itself but is defended as being a more apparent expression; the objection about time being indivisible is answered by considering estimative division; and the mention of incomplete causes is said to be according to the well-known view (al-mashhūr). Overall, the passage provides a detailed logical framework for understanding how one thing can be prior to another in a real sense.

Key Terms
تقدم وجودي
تقدم زماني
تقدم علّي
علة ناقصة
جزء الزمان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that temporal priority and causal priority are the same because both involve dependence. However, temporal priority requires the prior's non-existence, while causal priority does not.
Correction: The passage explicitly distinguishes them: temporal priority is conditioned by the prior's non-existence, causal priority is not.

Checkpoint
What distinguishes temporal priority from causal priority?
A) Temporal priority involves a condition that the prior must exist at the same time as the posterior.
B) Temporal priority requires the prior's non-existence after its existence for the posterior to exist.
C) Causal priority involves the essence of the posterior depending on the prior.
D) Causal priority only applies to complete causes.

Key Takeaway
Existential dependence divides into temporal priority (requiring the prior's non-existence) and causal priority (no such requirement, including complete and incomplete causes).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider your own existence, do you unconsciously assume that God's role is merely temporal—getting you started—or do you acknowledge His continuous causal priority every moment? What changes if you truly live as though your every breath is caused by Him right now?
?
The lesson distinguishes 'temporal priority' (where the prior must cease) from 'causal priority' (where the prior remains). Which pattern dominates your relationship with God? Do you seek Him only when you feel a 'gap' (temporal), or do you depend on Him even when things seem to be going well (causal)? What would shift if you chose the latter?
?
Incomplete causes are still considered causes in this framework. Are there 'small' dependencies in your life—like health, friendships, or daily provisions—that you treat as insignificant, forgetting that they are real, continuous dependencies on God? How might you cultivate awareness of these as genuine causes of your well-being?
