Philosophers' Classification of Mental Priority
Philosophers define mental priority and its two types: honorific and positional.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of mental priority (al-taqaddum al-iʿtibārī) and its two types: priority by honor and priority by rank, as well as the debate over their reducibility.

Core Idea
Mental priority is relative to a starting point; it comes in two forms: honor and rank.

This passage from the philosophers' discussion defines a key concept in metaphysical reasoning: mental priority. Unlike essential priority, mental priority depends on a chosen reference point. It then distinguishes two kinds based on whether that reference is perfection or not, and considers whether one type reduces to another. This lesson unpacks these distinctions and the logical objection raised.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then its literal translation. Get an overall sense of the definitions and the argument about reducibility.
2
Identify the definition and starting point
Notice that mental priority necessarily involves a starting point (mabdaʾ). Understand that this distinguishes it from essential priority.
The starting point is the reference relative to which priority is considered.
3
Distinguish the two types
There are two types: priority by honor (sharaf) when the starting point is perfection, and priority by rank (rutba) when it is not. Identify examples if possible.
Honor is based on virtue or excellence; rank is based on position in a sequence.
4
Examine the reducibility claims
The text mentions that some argue priority by honor reduces to priority by rank or to temporal priority, and priority by rank reduces to temporal priority. Understand the reasoning given.
The reduction arguments rely on observed correlations, e.g., the virtuous often get higher positions or act earlier.
5
Understand the commentator's objection
The marginal comment points out that entailment does not imply identity. One type following from another does not mean they are the same concept.
This objection preserves the distinctness of the categories despite their interrelations.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(والثاني) أعني التقدم الاعتباري ( لا بد فيه من مبدأ تعتبر إليه النسبة وذلك ) المبدأ (إما كمال) وهو التقدم بالشرف أم لا وهو التقدم بالرتبة وقد يقال : التقدم بالشرف راجع إلى التقدم بالرتبة لأن صاحب الفضيلة ربما يقدم في المراتب المكانية، أو إلى التقدم بالزمان، لأن الأفضل ربما كان أسبق في الشروع في الأمور، وكذلك التقدم بالرتبة راجع إلى التقدم الزماني إذ معناه أن زمان الوصول إليه من المبدأ قبل زمان الوصول إلى المتأخر )
قوله : ( لأن صاحب الفضيلة إلخ) فيه أن كون أحد التقدمين مستتبعاً للآخر لا يقتضي اتحادهما وكذا في الثاني .
(The second) i.e., mental priority necessarily involves a starting point to which the relation is considered. That starting point is either perfection, which is priority in honor, or not, which is priority in rank. It may be said: Priority in honor reduces to priority in rank because the possessor of virtue may be given precedence in spatial positions, or to temporal priority because the more excellent may be earlier in undertaking matters. Likewise, priority in rank reduces to temporal priority since its meaning is that the time of reaching it from the starting point is before the time of reaching the posterior. His saying: (Because the possessor of virtue, etc.) contains that one of the two priorities entailing the other does not necessitate their identity, and the same applies to the second.
Source pages p. 282

Commentary
This passage defines mental priority (al-taqaddum al-iʿtibārī) and its two types: priority by honor (al-sharaf) and priority by rank (al-rutba). Mental priority requires a point of reference (mabdaʾ) relative to which the priority is considered. If that reference is perfection, it is priority by honor; otherwise, it is priority by rank. The text then notes that some say priority by honor reduces to priority by rank (because a virtuous person may be given precedence in spatial arrangements) or to temporal priority (since the more excellent may initiate actions earlier). Similarly, priority by rank reduces to temporal priority, because its meaning is that the time of reaching the relative from the starting point is earlier than the time of reaching the posterior. The marginal comment objects that one priority entailing another does not imply they are identical; thus reduction is not necessary. Overall, the passage clarifies that these are conventional distinctions and may be further analyzed.

Key Terms
تقدم اعتباري
تقدم شرفي
تقدم رتبي
مبدأ
كمال
فضيلة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Since priority by honor may lead to priority by rank, they are actually the same thing.
Correction: The commentator points out that one entailing the other does not necessitate identity; they remain distinct concepts.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the meaning of priority by rank (al-taqaddum bi al-rutba)?
A) It is based on perfection and honor
B) It means the time of reaching the prior from the starting point is before the time of reaching the posterior
C) It is identical to essential priority
D) It requires no reference point

Key Takeaway
Mental priority requires a reference point and is divided into honor and rank; despite some claims of reducibility, the commentator insists they remain distinct.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In your own life, when you prioritize people or tasks, do you use a starting point of 'perfection' (honor) or something else (rank)? How does this distinction affect your decisions and relationships?
?
The commentator warns against conflating the types of priority even when they overlap. Is there a relationship in your life where you mistakenly assumed honor and rank are the same (e.g., equating a high-status person with a virtuous one)? How can you correct that today?
?
This lesson shows that mental priority is relative to a starting point you choose. What is the ultimate starting point (mabdaʾ) of your personal priorities? Is it aligned with what truly matters, or does it need adjustment?

Priority: Past vs. Future and Parts of the Future
First note on priority among parts of the future and between past and future.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how priority is assessed differently between parts of the future and between the past and future, and why the majority view holds the past as prior in essence.

Core Idea
Priority depends on context: essence vs. accident.

The first note addresses a subtle distinction: how we determine priority can change based on what we are comparing. Among future moments, priority is about proximity to the present. But when comparing the past and future as whole categories, a deeper question arises: which has priority in essence? This passage presents the majority view and a minority counterview.

Guided Path
1
Read the first note passage
Read the Arabic passage for the first note and its translation. Notice the two contexts: parts of the future, and past vs. future.
The passage uses 'فإن ذات الماضي متقدمة على ذات المستقبل'.
2
Identify the two criteria
Identify that priority among parts of the future is based on closeness to the present 'now', while priority between past and future is based on essence (majority view) or accident (minority view).
3
Contrast the majority and minority views
The majority say past's essence is prior; the minority say future's accident is prior because a time is first future then past. Consider the reasoning behind each.
This shows how different considerations (essence vs. accident) can reverse order.
4
Reflect on the importance
Ask yourself why this distinction matters for kalam. It helps define what we mean by 'priority' when discussing creation and time.
Connection to broader topic of priority types.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تنبيهان . الأول : ( إن التقدم إن اعتبر فيما بين أجزاء المستقبل فكل ما هو أقرب إلى الآن الحاضر فهو المتقدم، وإن اعتبر فيما بين الماضي والمستقبل فقد قيل : ( الماضي مقدم على المستقبل وهذا هو الصحيح (عند الجمهور) وإنما قالوا ذلك نظراً إلى ذاتهما فإن ذات الماضي متقدمة على ذات المستقبل ( ومنهم من عكس الأمر نظراً إلى عارضهما، فإن كل زمان يكون أولاً حالاً مستقبلاً ثم يصير ثم يصير ماضياً فكونه مستقبلاً يعرض له قبل كونه ماضياً. الثاني جميع أنواع التقدم مشترك في معنى واحد وهو أن للمتقدم أمراً زائداً ليس للمتأخر، ففي التقدم ( الذاتي كونه مقوماً ) أي جزءاً داخلاً في قوام المتأخر ( وفي ) التقدم ( العلي كونه موجداً، وفي الزماني كونه مضى له زمان أكثر لم يمض للمتأخر، وفي الشرفي زيادة كمال، وفي الرتبي وصول إليه من المبدأ أولاً.
قوله : ( أن التقدم إلخ) أي من حيث أنه تقدم أمر زائد ليس للمتأخر قد اعتبر في الشفاء أمراً آخر، وهو لا يكون شيء من ذلك الأمر للمتأخر إلا هو حاصل للمتقدم ولا بد منه ليظهر معنى التقدم والتأخر.
Two important notes: The first: (If priority is considered among parts of the future, then whatever is closer to the present now is the prior; if it is considered between the past and the future, it has been said: The past is prior to the future, and this is correct according to the majority. They said that in view of their essences, for the essence of the past is prior to the essence of the future. Some reversed the matter in view of their accidents, for every time first becomes a future state, then becomes past, so its being future occurs to it before its being past.) The second: All types of priority share one meaning: that the prior has an extra attribute that is not for the posterior. Thus, in essential priority, it is being constitutive (i.e., an internal part of the posterior's constitution); in causal priority, it is being the bringer into existence; in temporal priority, it is that a longer time has passed for it than has passed for the posterior; in honorific priority, it is an increase of perfection; and in positional priority, it is reaching it from the starting point first. His saying: (That priority, etc.) means that insofar as it is an extra attribute not possessed by the posterior, something else was considered in the Shifa; and that attribute must be present in the prior and not in the posterior to manifest the meaning of priority and posteriority.
Source pages p. 283

Commentary
This passage presents two important notes (tanbīhān) regarding priority. The first note discusses how priority is understood in different contexts: among parts of the future, priority is based on closeness to the present 'now'. Between past and future, the majority view is that the past is prior to the future with respect to their essences (the past has already occurred, so its essence is prior). Some, however, reverse this based on accidents, since every time is first future and then becomes past, so futurehood is an accident that occurs before pastness. The second note states that all types of priority share a common meaning: the prior possesses an extra attribute (amr zāʾid) that the posterior lacks. This attribute varies with each type: in essential priority it is being a constitutive part (muqawwim), in causal priority it is being the efficient cause (mūjid), in temporal priority it is having more time elapsed, in honorific priority it is an increase in perfection, and in positional priority it is being reached first from the starting point. The marginal note clarifies that this extra attribute is essential for distinguishing priority and posteriority, as discussed in Avicenna's Shifa. The passage thus unifies the five types under a single conceptual framework.

Key Terms
تقدم ماضوي
تقدم مستقبلي
عارض
ذات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Many assume priority is always temporal, but this passage shows it can also be by essence or accident.
Correction: Priority is context-dependent: among future parts it is temporal, but between past and future it is by essence (majority) or accident (minority).

Checkpoint
According to the first note, what is the basis for the majority view that the past is prior to the future?
A) The past is closer to the present
B) The essence of the past is prior to the essence of the future
C) The past has an extra attribute
D) The past is a cause of the future

Key Takeaway
Priority is not always temporal; it can be based on essence or accident, depending on the context.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In your daily decisions, do you prioritize things based on their essence (what they truly are) or based on their accidents (temporary circumstances)? This lesson shows that the majority considered the past prior by essence, while the minority considered the future prior by accident. Which perspective dominates your choices? Commit to examining one major decision this week through the lens of essence over accident.
?
Think of a time when you felt regret about the past or anxiety about the future. Which form of priority weighed on you more: the priority of the past (essence) or the priority of the future (accident)? How can recognizing both perspectives help you find balance between learning from the past and planning for the future without being overwhelmed by either?
?
The minority argued that every moment first becomes future then past, so the accident of 'future-ness' precedes 'past-ness'. Are there areas in your life where you focus too much on the accidental attributes of people or situations rather than their essence? For example, judging others by their changing moods rather than their inherent character. Make a commitment this week to intentionally focus on the essence of a person or situation, setting aside accidental features.
